<t396>"The children in this house are like idiots, always dazed," Ucsula said.</t396> <t397>"Maybe they are being bothered by worms."</t397> <t398>She prepared a bowl of paico tobacco water for them and both of them calmly drank it, and within a day they had sat on the potty eleven times, expelling a few pinkish worms.</t398> <t399>The two brothers eagerly showed everyone because it allowed them to distract Ucsula from the true cause of their melancholy.</t399> <t400>At that moment, Aureliano not only understood but also could feel his brother's sexual experiences as if he had gone through them himself, because once when his brother was describing in great detail how to make love, he interrupted to ask: "How did you feel?"...</t400> <t401>Without hesitation, José Arcadio immediately replied:</t401>
<t402>- It was as if the heavens and earth were shaking.</t402>
<t403>At two o'clock in the morning on a Thursday in January, Amaranta was born.</t403> <t404>Before anyone else entered the room, Úrsula examined the entire body of the infant.</t404> <t405>It was light and plump like a young lizard, but all its parts were human.</t405> <t406>Aureliano only learned the good news when he saw many people flocking to his house.</t406> <t407>To avoid this noise, he left the house to find his brother, who had left his bed since eleven o'clock, and it was such a sudden decision that he didn't have time to ask himself how to get his brother out of Pila Tecnéra's room.</t407> <t408>He wandered around her house for several hours, whistling continuously as a signal until dawn forced him to return.</t408> <t409>In his mother's room, he found José Arcadio with a calm face, playing with the newly born sister.</t409>
<t410>Ursula had just about finished her forty-day maternity leave when the gypsies arrived in the village.</t410> <t411>These were the same circus performers and puppeteers who had previously brought ice here.</t411>
<t412>Unlike Menkyadet's group, they soon displayed consumer goods instead of just the achievements of scientific progress as before.</t412> <t413>When they brought ice, they did not advertise its practical benefits for human life but only promoted it as a simple magic trick.</t413> <t414>This time, alongside other entertainments, they brought a flying carpet.</t414> <t415>They did not introduce it as a fundamental contribution to transportation development but presented it as a toy.</t415> <t416>Of course, the villagers dug out their last bits of gold to enjoy a swift flight over the rooftops in the village.</t416>
<t417>Taking advantage of the public's noisy and fascinating forgiveness, José Arcadio and Pilar Ternera lived hours of freedom.</t417> <t418>They were two lovers blissfully happy amidst the crowd, to the point of believing that their love was deeper and more profound than the ultimate and immediate pleasure they experienced during their secret nights.</t418> <t419>However, Pilar Ternera shattered that joy.</t419> <t420>Elated by José Arcadio's passion while enjoying their couple's happiness, she confused the manner and timing of her words, suddenly placing a heavy responsibility on his shoulders as vast as the globe.</t420> <t421>"Now, you are a man," she told him.</t421> <t422>And because he did not understand what she had just said, she calmly explained it in detail:</t422>
<t423>- It means you will have a son.</t423>
<t424>For several days in a row, José Arcadio did not dare to leave the house.</t424><t425>Just hearing the giggling of Pilar Ternera was enough to make him hide in the laboratory, where the handicrafts, the fruits of alchemy, were coming to life thanks to Úrsula's charity.</t425><t426>José Arcadio Buendía warmly welcomed the wayward son and guided him as an apprentice in the alchemical work that he had thoroughly understood.</t426><t427>One afternoon, the two brothers were very excited by the sight of a flying carpet passing by the laboratory window, carrying the gypsy driver and several village children who were happily waving, and José Arcadio Buendía did not even glance at the flying carpet.</t427><t428>"Let them dream," he said, "we will fly higher than them with scientific means far superior to this pathetic flying carpet."</t428><t429>Even though he had to pretend to be interested in the work, José Arcadio could never understand the grand possibilities of the alchemical furnace, which to him was simply a poorly made glass jar.</t429><t430>He lost his appetite, suffered from insomnia, became irritable, just like his father when he faced failures in some tasks, and his erratic state forced his father to take over all the work in the alchemical furnace, thinking that the work was too exhausting for him.</t430><t431>Aureliano, of course, understood that his brother's suffering had nothing to do with the alchemical furnace, but he could not get him to reveal the deeper issue. José Arcadio was no longer as lively as before.</t431><t432>From being witty and open, he became secretive and difficult.</t432><t433>Too fond of solitude, always angry at everything around him, one night, as usual, he left his bed empty and went out, but instead of going to Pilar Ternera's house, he went straight to the fair.</t433><t434>After wandering through all the amusements, uninterested in any of them, he noticed someone not performing, a very young gypsy girl, almost still a child, wearing a necklace full of fake crystal gems.</t434><t435>She was the most beautiful woman he had ever seen until then.</t435><t436>He was in the middle of a crowd engrossed in watching a man turn into a snake for not obeying his parents.</t436>
<t437>Hôsê Accađiô didn't pay attention.</t437> <t438>While the interrogation of the snake-man was taking place, he weaved through the crowd to reach the first place where he met the gypsy girl, stopping behind her.</t438> <t439>From behind, he wrapped his arms around her waist.</t439> <t440>She tried to break free, but Hôsê Accađiô held her tighter.</t440> <t441>So she was moved, standing still but still turning her back to him, trembling with surprise and fear, unable to believe it was real; finally, she turned her face, looking at him with a trembling smile.</t441> <t442>Meanwhile, two gypsy men shoved the snake-man into a bag and carried him inside the shop.</t442> <t443>A gypsy ringleader turned to the audience and said:</t443>
<t444>- Ladies and gentlemen, now we would like to present a thrilling act about a woman who, every night just like tonight, has been beheaded at this exact hour for one hundred and fifty years for daring to look at what she shouldn't have.</t444>
<t445>Hôsê Accađiô and the girl did not watch the beheading act.</t445> <t446>They went to her place, where in desperate longing they indulged in kissing while undressing.</t446> <t447>The gypsy girl took off all her outer garments, petticoats, heavily padded corset, and fake crystal pearl necklace, and suddenly her once full breasts and wide hips disappeared.</t447> <t448>In reality, her body was like a dry branch, with a flat chest and thin thighs that were not even as big as Hôsê Accađiô's arms, but she had a bold demeanor and a warm scent that compensated.</t448> <t449>However, Hôsê Accađiô couldn't immediately respond to her fervor because they were in a public place, where gypsies frequently passed by, sometimes carrying circus equipment, sometimes in costume, and occasionally gathering beside the bed to play a game of dice.</t449> <t450>A lamp hanging from a pole in the middle of the yard illuminated the surroundings.</t450> <t451>When they stopped caressing each other, Hôsê Accađiô lay naked, stretched out on the bed, not knowing what else to do, while the girl tried to seduce him.</t451> <t452>Later, a sleek-skinned gypsy woman walked in, followed by a man who was neither part of the circus troupe nor from the village.</t452> <t453>Then both of them undressed right in front of the bed where Hôsê Accađiô was lying.</t453> <t454>Without intending to tease him, the woman looked at Hôsê Accađiô, filled with desire, eagerly admiring his magnificent resting organ.</t454>
<t455>- Oh, my friend? - She exclaimed. - May God protect your sister for you, my friend.</t455>
<t456>The girlfriend of José Arcadio requested to be left alone and the couple lay down on the floor right next to the bed.</t456> <t457>The passion of the others awakened José Arcadio's fervor.</t457> <t458>From the very first moment, the girl's bones seemed to come loose, cracking like dominoes touching each other, her skin slick with a yellowish sweat, her eyes brimming with tears, her whole body emitting a moaning sound and a persistent smell of mud lingering around.</t458> <t459>But the resilient girl endured the encounter with a steadfast character and an admirable courage.</t459>
<t460>At that moment, José Arcadio felt himself being lifted to a breathless state where his heart melted into a well of gentle sensuality flowing into the girl through her ears and coming out through her mouth, transformed into words translated into her language.</t460> <t461>It was Thursday.</t461> <t462>On Saturday night, with a red scarf on his head, José Arcadio left home with the gypsies.</t462>
<t463>When Úrsula noticed his absence, she hurriedly went around the village looking for him.</t463> <t464>At the desolate camp, there was nothing more than a stream of dirty water amidst the still-hot ashes in the extinguished hearths.</t464> <t465>A person searching for fake crystal beads in the garbage told Úrsula that last night they had seen her son mingling with the noisy gypsies, pushing a cage cart with the snake-man inside.</t465>
<t466>"So he has gone off with the gypsies," she screamed into his ear, showing no sign of worry at the news of their son's disappearance.</t466>
<t467>- God willing, it will be so, - José Arcadio Buendía said as he continued to grind the material that had been ground a thousand times in the mortar, fired, and then ground again.</t467> <t468>- That way, it will learn to be human.</t468>
<t469>Ursula asked everyone which direction the gypsies had gone.</t469> <t470>On the way, she kept asking, and people kept pointing her in the direction, so she continued on, thinking there was still time to catch up with her son.</t470> <t471>In this manner, she went farther and farther from the village until she realized she had gone so far that she didn't think she could return.</t471> <t472>José Arcadio Buendía only noticed his wife's absence at eight o'clock in the evening, when he placed the remelted alloy on a layer of mulch and went to see why baby Amaranta was crying hoarsely.</t472> <t473>A few hours later, he gathered a group of men, equipped them neatly, placed baby Amaranta in the hands of a woman who agreed to nurse her, and then went to look for Ursula.</t473> <t474>Aureliano also went with them.</t474> <t475>The indigenous fishermen, through gestures, indicated to them that they hadn't seen anyone pass by since early morning.</t475> <t476>After three days of futile searching, the search party returned to the village.</t476>
<t477>In a few weeks, José Arcadio Buendía was completely disheartened.</t477> <t478>He took care of baby Amaranta like a mother.</t478> <t479>He bathed and changed the baby's clothes, and during the day, he took the baby to nurse four times, and at night he sang lullabies to put the baby to sleep, something Úrsula had never done.</t479> <t480>Once, Pilar Ternera volunteered to help with the household chores until Úrsula returned.</t480> <t481>Aureliano, whose strange intuition had already sensed that she was the culprit behind this misfortune, glared at her as she entered.</t481> <t482>Thus, he knew that in whatever form, she was guilty of his brother's departure and his mother's disappearance, and in this way, he silently and resolutely condemned her, causing the woman to no longer dare to set foot in the house.</t482>
<t483>As time passed, everything fell back into place.</t483> <t484>José Arcadio Buendía and his son did not realize when they resumed working in the laboratory, dusting, heating the test tubes, and then becoming engrossed in the preparation of alloys that had lain dormant under a layer of dust for several months.</t484> <t485>Even little Amaranta, snug in her wicker cradle, curiously observed the silent work of her father and brother in the room that had become strange due to the mercury fumes.</t485> <t486>After several months of Úrsula's disappearance, some strange events occurred one day.</t486> <t487>An empty glass jar that had been forgotten on a shelf for a long time suddenly became so heavy that it could not be moved.</t487> <t488>A pot of filtered water placed on the work table, without any fire underneath, suddenly started boiling and within half an hour, all the water had evaporated.</t488> <t489>José Arcadio Buendía and his son were startled by these phenomena and could not explain them, but they insisted that they were omens of the alloy that was about to be created.</t489> <t490>One day, little Amaranta's cradle started spinning on its own and moved around the room, much to the alarm of Aureliano, who rushed to stop it.</t490> <t491>But his father remained calm and unperturbed.</t491> <t492>He placed the cradle back in its original spot and tied it to a table leg, convinced that the long-awaited event was imminent.</t492> <t493>At that moment, Aureliano heard his father say:</t493>
<t494>- If you do not fear God, then fear metal instead!</t494>
<t495>Suddenly, Úrsula returned after nearly five months of being missing.</t495> <t496>She came back looking cheerful, youthful, wearing a dress that was unfamiliar to the villagers.</t496> <t497>José Arcadio Buendía, overwhelmed with joy and sorrow, could hardly resist hugging and kissing her. "Oh my, so that's how it is!", he exclaimed: "I knew it would happen" and he truly believed it, because during his solitary sessions in the room while concocting compounds, from the depths of his heart he had prayed for the eagerly awaited event to occur—not as the perfect result of alchemy, not as liberation from the furnace blowing fire to melt metals, not as the magic to turn the hinges and locks of the house into gold, but as the event that had just happened: Úrsula's return.</t497> <t498>But she did not share her husband's noisy joy.</t498> <t499>She kissed him out of habit as if she had been away for no more than an hour, then told him:</t499>
<t500>- Let's go outside!</t500>
<t501>When he went out and looked at the crowd, José Arcadio Buendía took a long time to come to his senses.</t501> <t502>They were not gypsies.</t502> <t503>They were men and women just like the villagers, with the same soft hair and brown skin, speaking the same language as him and lamenting the same sorrows.</t503> <t504>They brought with them donkeys laden with food, ox carts carrying tables and cooking utensils, which were simple and essential goods sold by vendors without any advertisement.</t504> <t505>They had come from the other side of the swamp, about two days' journey from Macondo, where villages regularly received mail from the post office and were familiar with machines that produced many benefits.</t505> <t506>Úrsula did not catch up with the gypsies, but she found the road that her husband had failed to discover in his unsuccessful quest for great inventions.</t506>
<t507>Chapter 3</t507>
<t508>Pila Tecnêra's son was brought to his grandparents' home two weeks after he was born.</t508><t509>Ursula reluctantly accepted him, as once again she had to yield to her husband's eccentricity, who persistently held the belief that his descendants should not be left to fend for themselves in life. However, she set the condition that the child would never know his true origins.</t509><t510>Although the child was named José Arcadio, the grandparents decided to avoid confusion by calling him simply Arcadio.</t510><t511>At that time, there were many social activities outside the village and a lot of work inside the house, so child care became a secondary concern.</t511><t512>The children were entrusted to Visitación, an indigenous girl who had come to the village with her brother, fleeing a plague of insomnia that had been ravaging their tribe for several years.</t512><t513>Both siblings were gentle, well-behaved, and hardworking, so Ursula trusted them to help with household chores.</t513><t514>Thus, Arcadio and Amaranta spoke the indigenous language before they knew how to speak Spanish, learned to sip lizard soup, and ate spider eggs without Ursula's knowledge, as she was busy with her promising candy animal business.</t514><t515>Macondo had completely changed.</t515><t516>Those who came with Ursula had learned to exploit the fertile land and the advantageous geographical position of Macondo compared to the swampy region. Consequently, the once desolate village quickly became a bustling town with shops, craft workshops, and a regular marketplace where the first Arabs, wearing pantoufles and earrings, came to trade fake crystal beads for long-tailed parrots.</t516><t517>José Arcadio Buendía did not have a moment of rest.</t517>
<t518>Excited by a reality that at this moment seemed more mystical to him than the vast world within his imagination, he lost all interest in his work at the alchemy lab, abandoning the metal substance that had been repeatedly heated and cooled for months to return to his energetic self who once decided to chart roads and build houses so that no one would enjoy more benefits than others.</t518><t519>He had high prestige among the newcomers to the village, to the extent that those who wanted to dig foundations for houses or fence their gardens had to seek his advice first, and he was elected by everyone as the first person to decide on the redistribution of the village's land.</t519><t520>When the gypsies returned with their magic tricks, this time their mobile stall had become a huge shop with games of chance, they were warmly welcomed because people thought that José Arcadio would return with them.</t520><t521>But José Arcadio did not return, and these gypsies did not bring the snake-man, whom Úrsula thought would be the only one who could tell her the truth about her son, so she did not allow the gypsies to stop and open their shop, and in the future, they would not be allowed to set foot in the village because they were condemned as messengers of debauchery and corruption.</t521><t522>However, José Arcadio Buendía showed great insight in thinking that the tribe of Melquíades, with their ancient knowledge and marvelous inventions that had greatly contributed to the growth of Macondo, should always be welcomed with open arms.</t522><t523>But the tribe of Melquíades, according to the words of the wanderers, had been wiped off the face of the earth for daring to surpass the limits of knowledge that God had decreed for mankind.</t523>
<t524>Escaping the torment of illusory desires, at least for the time being, José Arcadio Buendía managed to stabilize the village's way of life with discipline and labor, and allowed the birds that had enlivened the atmosphere with their songs since the village's founding to be released, replacing them with musical clocks installed in homes.</t524> <t525>These were precious clocks made of smoothly planed wood that the Arabs exchanged with the villagers for long-tailed parrots, and José Arcadio Buendía orchestrated them to run so precisely in unison that every half hour, the villagers were cheered by harmonious music that seemed to emanate from a single clock.</t525> <t526>José Arcadio Buendía was the one who, during those years, decided to plant almond trees along the roads instead of acacias, and he was also the one who discovered but never revealed the methods to make them thrive.</t526> <t527>Many years later, when the village of Macondo had become a settlement of wooden houses with tin roofs, the old almond trees still lined the ancient roads, covered in road dust, though by then no one knew who had planted them.</t527> <t528>While the father was busy organizing the communal affairs of the village and the mother was making chicken-shaped candies on sticks to sell at the market twice a day to supplement the family income, Aureliano was passionately absorbed in the abandoned laboratory, studying the craft of goldsmithing.</t528> <t529>He did it purely out of personal interest.</t529> <t530>In a short time, Aureliano grew so much that he could no longer fit into his older brother's clothes and had to wear his father's, but Visitación had to take in the sleeves and narrow the pant legs because he still did not have the robust build of his father and brother.</t530> <t531>Adolescence had taken away Aureliano's sweet voice and made him sullen, quiet, and solitary, but conversely, it had also returned to him the intense expressiveness that he had possessed since birth in his eyes.</t531> <t532>Aureliano was so intensely focused on his goldsmithing experiments that he hardly left the laboratory to eat.</t532> <t533>Worried about his son's extreme intellectual concentration, José Arcadio Buendía gave him the key to the door and some money, thinking that perhaps his son needed the company of a woman.</t533> <t534>But Aureliano used the money to buy hydrochloric acid to create a solution, and he made the keys shine with a layer of gold plating.</t534> <t535>Aureliano's peculiar traits were in harmony with Arcadio and Amaranta, who, though they had lost their baby teeth, still clung to the skirts of the Indian women and stubbornly refused to give up their native language for Spanish. "I have nothing more to complain about," Úrsula said to her husband. "Our children have inherited their parents' madness."</t535> <t536>While she lamented her unfortunate fate, feeling that her sons' eccentricities were indeed as terrifying as a pig's tail, Aureliano dreamily watched her:</t536>
<t537>- Someone will come to our house! - he told his mother.</t537>
<t538>Because you have often talked about omens, Ucsula, with her common sense, intended to dampen your spirits.</t538> <t539>Visitors to the house are a normal occurrence.</t539> <t540>Dozens of strangers pass through the village of Macondo without surprising anyone or announcing any secret.</t540> <t541>However, despite those common sense arguments, Aureliano still insisted on his prediction:</t541>
<t542>- I don't know who it is? - he said, - but the person coming here is currently on their way.</t542>
<t543>Indeed, Rebecca arrived on a Sunday.</t543> <t544>The little girl was not yet eleven years old.</t544> <t545>She had to struggle to travel from Manaure to here with the fur traders who were tasked with delivering her and the letter to José Arcadio Buendía's house, but even these traders could not clearly explain who had asked them to do so.</t545> <t546>All of her belongings consisted of a small trunk containing clothes, a wooden rocking chair, and a burlap sack in which her parents' bones were kept, which would often clatter, clatter, clatter when they bumped into each other.</t546> <t547>The letter addressed to José Arcadio Buendía was written with affectionate words expressing the feelings of someone who, despite being separated for a long time, still loved him and, out of a very close affection, felt obliged to ask his family to take care of this orphaned girl with no place to go. It mentioned that the girl was a close relative of Úrsula and thus also somewhat related to José Arcadio Buendía, though a bit more distantly, because she was the daughter of an unforgettable friend named Nicanor Ulloa and his noble wife named Rebecca Montiel.</t547> <t548>These are the people who are now under God's protection in His paradise, and their bones have been collected so that his family could bury them in the graveyard of Christ's flock.</t548>
<t549>The names mentioned as signatures in the letter were completely clear, but neither José Arcadio Buendía nor Úrsula remembered knowing anyone with those names, including the sender of the letter, and in the distant village of Manaure, there couldn't possibly be anyone with those names.</t549> <t550>Through the little girl, they also couldn't find any more detailed information.</t550> <t551>Since her arrival, the little girl sat on the rocking chair, sucking her thumb and with wide, fearful eyes, observing everyone, without showing whether she understood or didn't understand what people were asking her.</t551> <t552>The little girl wore a black-striped, worn-out diagonal fabric dress and varnished boots with holes, her hair tucked behind her ears with black ribbons.</t552> <t553>She also wore a sleeveless embroidered cloak, faded by sweat, and on her right wrist, she wore a wild animal's tooth set in a bronze base, which seemed like a protective amulet.</t553> <t554>Her skin was pale green, her belly round and swollen like a drum, all indicating that she was very sickly and had been starving for a long time.</t554> <t555>But when given food, she sat still, placed the bowl on her lap, and didn't taste it at all.</t555> <t556>Everyone assumed she was mute and deaf until the Indians asked her in their native language if she wanted water, and her eyes blinked as if she understood them, and she nodded to ask for water.</t556>
<t557>There was no other way, so the family had to adopt the little girl.</t557><t558>Everyone decided to call her Rebecca to match her mother's name because Aureliano had patiently sat in front of her reading all the saint names, but he didn't see any reaction, not even the slightest, on the girl's face.</t558><t559>At that time, the village of Macondo didn't have a cemetery, because until then, no one had died, so the family had to keep the sack containing the remains until there was a proper place for burial.</t559><t560>For quite a long time, they were scattered everywhere, found in the most unexpected places, often accompanied by the squawking of a scratching chicken.</t560><t561>It took a long time for Rebecca to get used to life in this house.</t561><t562>She sat on the rocking chair, sucking her thumb in the loneliest corner of the house.</t562><t563>Only the music from the clock caught her attention.</t563><t564>Every half hour, with startled eyes, she listened as if waiting for that music from some windy place.</t564><t565>For several days, the family couldn't get her to eat.</t565><t566>No one could understand how she didn't starve to death until the Indians, who knew everything because they quietly moved around the house, discovered that Rebecca didn't like to eat rice but instead liked to eat moist earth from the yard and wall soil that she scraped off with her fingers.</t566><t567>It was clear that her parents or whoever raised her had scolded her for this bad habit, so she ate secretly, fully aware that it was a wrongdoing.</t567><t568>She would sneak pieces of wall soil to eat when no one was looking.</t568><t569>From then on, people paid more attention to her.</t569><t570>They poured cow bile on the yard and smeared chili water on the walls, believing that these methods could curb the dangerous habit.</t570><t571>But the girl was clever and skillfully found ways to hide the soil to eat, forcing Ursula to use even more effective measures.</t571><t572>She mixed orange juice with rhubarb powder in a jar and left it out overnight, and the next morning at breakfast, she gave the girl a cup of it.</t572><t573>Even though no one told her that this was a special remedy for eating soil, she thought that any bitter liquid on an empty stomach would affect the liver.</t573><t574>Despite having rickets, Rebecca was so fierce and strong that they had to pin her down like a calf to give her the medicine, and they could hardly restrain her kicking legs or cover her mouth to stop the incomprehensible sounds accompanied by scratching and spitting, which, according to the Indians, were the most obscene words in their language.</t574><t575>Knowing this, Ursula had to resort to using a whip while giving her the medicine.</t575><t576>It will never be determined whether it was the rhubarb powder or the whippings, or both combined, that proved effective, but the fact was that within a few weeks, Rebecca began to show signs of recovery.</t576><t577>Rebecca joined the games of Arcadio and Amaranta, who welcomed her as an older sister, and she ate heartily, skillfully using the utensils.</t577><t578>She also quickly demonstrated that she spoke Spanish as fluently as the Indian language and had a knack for manual labor.</t578><t579>She sang along with the waltz music from the clock, improvising beautiful lyrics.</t579><t580>Soon, she was accepted as another member of the family.</t580><t581>Ursula loved her more than her own children, and the girl called Amaranta and Arcadio her siblings, Aureliano her uncle, and José Arcadio Buendía her grandfather.</t581>
<t582>So in the end, the girl truly deserved to bear the name Rebeca Buendía, a unique name of hers, and she proudly carried it throughout her life until her last breath.</t582>
<t583>One night, during the period when Rebecca was cured of her habit of eating dirt and was moved to sleep in the same room as the other children, the Indian girl who usually slept in the same room with them suddenly woke up and heard an unusual noise.</t583> <t584>The girl startled and sat up, thinking that some animal had entered the room, and at that moment she saw Rebecca sitting on the rocking chair, sucking her thumb with eyes shining like a cat's in the dark night.</t584>
<t585>Panic-stricken by the dangerous disease, worried about the misfortune of her fate, Visitacion recognized in those eyes the symptoms of an illness whose threat had forced them, her and her brother, to forever bid farewell to the ancient kingdom where they had once been princess and prince.</t585> <t586>It was the insomnia plague.</t586>
<t587>Cataurê, Visitaxiôn's brother, ran away from home that night.</t587><t588>His sister stayed because her fateful heart had told her that the deadly dangerous disease would follow her to the ends of the earth.</t588><t589>No one understood Visitaxiôn's anxiety.</t589><t590>"If we can't sleep, all the better," José Arcadio Buendía said cheerfully, "that way life will be more useful to us."</t590><t591>But the Indian girl explained to them that the most frightening thing about insomnia was not the inability to sleep, because the body does not feel tired, but that if not treated in time, it would certainly lead to an even more serious disease, which is amnesia.</t591><t592>That is, when the patient gets used to the state of insomnia, childhood memories will be erased from his mind, then the names and meanings of things, and finally his own identity and even the awareness of himself, will be erased from his memory, leaving everything to sink into an unprecedented state of idiocy. José Arcadio Buendía, feeling like laughing to death, remarked that the girl was talking about one of those diseases caused by the superstitions of the Indians.</t592><t593>However, Úrsula, to be safe, carefully isolated Rebeca from the children.</t593><t594>Weeks later, when Visitaxiôn's anxiety had subsided, José Arcadio Buendía spent a restless night in bed, unable to sleep.</t594><t595>Úrsula, who had also woken up, asked him what was wrong, and he replied, "Once again, I am thinking about Prudencio Aguilar, my dear."</t595><t596>Neither of the couple slept a minute, but the next day they felt so healthy that they forgot about the sleepless night.</t596><t597>At lunchtime, Aureliano startled everyone by saying that he felt fine even though he had stayed up all night gilding a hair clip, a gift he planned to give his mother for her birthday.</t597><t598>No one in the family showed any concern until the third day, at bedtime, when they still did not feel sleepy, and only then did everyone realize that they had not slept for more than fifty hours.</t598>
<t599>- The children are as alert as larks, - the Indian girl said with a firm belief in the unavoidable fate.</t599> <t600>- Once the epidemic spreads, no one can escape.</t600>
<t601>Indeed, the insomnia epidemic had spread to this place.</t601><t602>Thanks to learning from her mother, Ucsula knew about some plants with healing properties. She crushed the leaves of the aconite plant to extract the juice for everyone to drink, but it was ineffective; no one could sleep, and instead, everyone spent the entire day dreaming while awake.</t602><t603>In that state of wakeful dreaming, not only did people see the figures from their own dreams, but they also saw the figures from others' dreams.</t603><t604>It was as if the house was crowded with guests.</t604><t605>Sitting on the rocking chair placed in the kitchen corner, Rebecca dreamed of a man who looked very much like her, dressed in white linen clothes, with a gold button at his collar, bringing her a rose branch.</t605><t606>A woman with delicate hands walking beside him plucked a rose and pinned it in the girl's hair.</t606><t607>Ucsula understood that they were Rebecca's parents, but despite her efforts to recognize them, she had to admit that she had never seen them before.</t607><t608>Meanwhile, due to an unfortunate oversight that José Arcadio Buendía would never forgive himself for: the animal-shaped candies produced in the house continued to be sold at the market.</t608><t609>Children and adults delighted in sucking on the green chicken-shaped candies that had caught the insomnia, the pink fish-shaped candies that had caught the insomnia, the yellow horse-shaped candies that had caught the insomnia, so the dawn of the second day found the entire village awake.</t609><t610>At first, no one panicked.</t610><t611>On the contrary, everyone was delighted to be unable to sleep, because there was so much work to be done that they hardly had enough time.</t611><t612>They worked so much that soon there was nothing left to do, and at three in the morning, they all sat with folded arms, counting each chime from the clocks.</t612><t613>Those who wanted to sleep, not because they felt tired but because they missed their usual sleep, tried every necessary method to bring sleep to themselves, but all were ineffective.</t613><t614>They gathered to chat endlessly, to repeat a single funny story for hours and hours, to drive everyone crazy with the story of the Capon chicken, which was a never-ending game where the storyteller would ask if the listeners wanted to hear the story of the Capon chicken, and when they answered yes, he would say he didn't ask them to say yes but if they wanted to hear the story of the Capon chicken, and when they answered no, he would say he didn't ask them to say no but if they wanted to hear the story of the Capon chicken, and when they stayed silent, he would say he didn't ask them to stay silent but if they wanted to hear the story of the Capon chicken, and no one could leave because the storyteller would say he didn't ask them to leave but if they wanted to hear the story of the Capon chicken, and so it went on in a vicious cycle throughout the long nights.</t614>
<t615>When José Arcadio Buendía realized that the epidemic had spread throughout the village, he gathered the household heads to explain the insomnia disease as he understood it.</t615> <t616>The household heads unanimously agreed to take the necessary measures to prevent this calamity from spreading to other residents in the swamp area.</t616> <t617>So, they removed the small bells from the goats' necks that the Arabs had brought here in exchange for long-tailed parrots and placed them at the village gate, ready for those who ignored the gatekeeper's earnest warnings and pleas and insisted on visiting the village of Macondo.</t617> <t618>All strangers walking the streets of Macondo during that time had to ring the bells to inform the sick that they were healthy.</t618> <t619>During their stay, they were not allowed to eat or drink anything because it was beyond doubt that the disease spread through the mouth, and moreover, all food and drink had been contaminated with insomnia.</t619> <t620>In this way, the epidemic was confined within the village's boundaries.</t620> <t621>This decisive measure was the one used during the quarantine period, and it was maintained until the state of insomnia was considered a normal phenomenon, and life was organized so that business regained its usual rhythm, and no one had to worry about sleeping, considering it a useless habit.</t621>
<t622>Aureliano was the first person to find a remedy for memory loss.</t622> <t623>He discovered it by chance.</t623> <t624>As the person who had suffered from insomnia the longest in the village, he had trained to achieve a high level of skill in the craft of goldsmithing.</t624> <t625>One day, he was looking for a small anvil to thin metal and suddenly forgot where it was.</t625>
<t626>Your father told you to call it a tas.</t626> <t627>Aureliano immediately wrote the word tas on a piece of paper and stuck it on the anvil stand.</t627> <t628>That way, he believed he would not forget its name in the future.</t628> <t629>He did not realize that forgetting the name was the first sign of amnesia, but simply thought it was because the tool had a very difficult name to remember.</t629> <t630>But a few days later, he discovered that he could not remember the names of the tools in the laboratory.</t630> <t631>So he wrote their names on each object, so just reading the name would allow him to recognize them.</t631> <t632>When his father shared his worry about forgetting all the deep memories from his childhood, Aureliano explained his method to him, and José Arcadio Buendía then applied this method to everyone in the house, and later to all the villagers.</t632> <t633>Using a branch dipped in ink, he wrote the name of each object: table, chair, clock, door, wall, bed, frying pan.</t633> <t634>Then he went to the garden and wrote on each animal and plant: cow, goat, pig, hen, cassava, taro, plantain.</t634> <t635>By exploring the infinite possibilities of amnesia, he gradually realized that a day might come when people would recognize objects by their written names but would not remember their uses in life.</t635> <t636>So it was necessary to write more clearly.</t636> <t637>The sign hanging on the cow's neck was a typical example of how the people of Macondo used this method to combat amnesia. This is a cow; every morning it must be milked, and the milk must be boiled to mix with coffee to make coffee with milk.</t637> <t638>Thus, the villagers lived in a slippery reality that could not be etched into memory, fleetingly recognized by the words written on paper but disappearing when the meanings of the words were forgotten.</t638>
<t639>Right at the entrance to the village of Macondo, a sign was erected that read: Macondo, and on the central road of the village, an even larger sign was erected that said "God Exists".</t639><t640>To remember objects as well as human emotions, signs were hung in houses labeling each object and each type of emotion.</t640><t641>But this memory system required so much observation and mental effort that many people surrendered to the joy of an imaginary world, one they themselves invented, which was more of a world of consolation than a world of action for them.</t641><t642>Pilar Ternera contributed significantly to the spread of this deception when she sensed how to read the past in the cards, just as she had previously read the future through them.</t642><t643>Using this method, the insomniacs began to live in a world constructed of vague, uncertain images in the cards, where the father almost remembered himself as a tanned man who had just arrived here at the beginning of April, and the mother almost remembered herself as a young woman with jet-black hair, still wearing a gold label on her left hand, and where a birthday seemed to have just been celebrated last Tuesday, with the lark's song still echoing in the laurel tree.</t643><t644>Defeated by these comforting tricks, José Arcadio Buendía resolved to create a memory machine he had always wished for, to remember the marvelous inventions of the gypsies.</t644><t645>Every morning, this machine was supposed to be capable of reviewing all the knowledge acquired in life from beginning to end.</t645><t646>He imagined it as a self-rotating dictionary that a person sitting at the axis, using a crank, could make the knowledge of life appear before their eyes in just a few hours.</t646><t647>He wrote fourteen thousand index cards.</t647><t648>At that very moment, on the road from the swamp to the village, an eccentric old man appeared, holding a small bell that rang with the sad sound of a sleepy person, carrying a bulging suitcase tied carefully with ropes, pushing a small cart covered with dark patches of cloth.</t648><t649>He went straight to the house of José Arcadio Buendía.</t649>
<t650>When she opened the door for the old man, Visitacion did not recognize him and thought that the old man had come to sell something, not knowing that it was impossible to sell anything in this village, which was mired in a terrible, incurable disease of forgetfulness.</t650> <t651>He was a very elderly man.</t651> <t652>Although his voice was hoarse and intermittent, and his hands trembled as if denying the existence of objects, it was clear that he came from a land where people could still sleep and remember.</t652> <t653>Jose Arcadio Buendia found him sitting in the living room; fanning himself with a patched-up hat, his eyes engrossed in reading the handwritten papers pasted on the walls.</t653> <t654>He greeted the old man respectfully to avoid being rude, fearing that it might be an old acquaintance he had not seen in a long time and did not currently recognize.</t654> <t655>But the guest realized that he was not sincere.</t655> <t656>The old man felt forgotten by the host, but it was not a forgetfulness born of carelessness; it was a malicious and incurable forgetfulness due to an inability to concentrate memory, a forgetfulness he knew very well because it was the forgetfulness of death.</t656>
<t657>The old man opened the suitcase packed with indispensable items, then he took out a bag containing bottles. He gave José Arcadio Buendía a drink of a pleasantly scented liquid, and immediately his memory brightened.</t657> <t658>Tears blurred his eyes before he could see that he was in a room cluttered with labeled objects, before he could feel ashamed of the foolish things written on the walls with all sincerity and more, before he realized who the newcomer was in the fresh light of hope.</t658> <t659>That person was Melquíades.</t659>
<t660>While Macondo was celebrating the recovery of its memory, José Arcadio Buendía and Melquíades rekindled their old friendship, which had long been covered in the dust of time.</t660> <t661>The gypsy decided to stay in the village.</t661> <t662>Indeed, he had been in the realm of the dead and had fled back here because he could no longer bear the loneliness.</t662> <t663>Abandoned by his tribe, stripped of his supernatural powers, all of this seemed like a punishment for his loyalty to life. He had resolved to flee to this corner of the world, unnoticed by death, and devoted himself to the profession of daguerreotype photography.</t663> <t664>José Arcadio Buendía had never heard of this invention.</t664> <t665>But when he saw himself and his family eternally captured on a shimmering metal plate, he stood there in a daze.</t665> <t666>A photograph from that time, now tarnished, still exists, showing José Arcadio Buendía with graying curly hair, a brass button fastening the collar of his shirt, and an expression of solemn fear that made Úrsula laugh and describe him as "a terrified general."</t666> <t667>Indeed, José Arcadio Buendía was terrified on that December morning when the photograph was taken, because he thought that people would gradually disappear as his image appeared on the metal plate.</t667> <t668>By a strange reversal of habits, it was Úrsula who had planted this idea in his mind, and she, who had forgotten her previous discomforts, agreed to let Melquíades stay in their house, although she never allowed him to photograph her because (as she said) she did not want to exist in such a way to mock her grandchildren.</t668>
<t669>That morning, she dressed beautifully to visit the children, applied powder to them, and then gave each one a spoonful of marrow porridge so they could stand still for two minutes in front of Menkyadet's camera.</t669> <t670>In the photograph of the long-lost family, Aureliano, dressed in black wool, stood between Amaranta and Rebeca.</t670> <t671>He bore a melancholic look and sharp eyes that, many years later, remained unchanged when he faced the firing squad; at that time, he still had no premonition of his fate.</t671> <t672>He had become a skilled jeweler admired throughout the region.</t672> <t673>In the workshop, which was set up alongside Menkyadet's noisy laboratory, it seemed as if he had escaped into another era to work to the point where he could no longer hear himself breathe; meanwhile, his father and Menkyadet loudly debated Nostradamus's prophecies.</t673>
<t674>The words echoed amidst the clinking of bottles and cups containing solutions, and the misfortune of spilled acid and scattered silver spoons caused by occasional elbow bumps and slips.</t674> <t675>The wholehearted dedication to work, the correct concept guiding all his interests, had allowed him in a short time to earn a sum of money larger than what Ursula had earned from her delicious animal-shaped candies, but everyone was surprised that although he was already a grown man, he still hadn't known a woman.</t675> <t676>In truth, he had never been with anyone.</t676>
<t677>A few months later, old Francisco - The Man, a nearly two-hundred-year-old wanderer who often passed through Macondo to spread songs he composed himself, returned to the village.</t677> <t678>In these songs, old Francisco - The Man recounted in detail the events that happened along his journeys, from the village of Manaure to the remotest corners of the swampy regions, so if anyone wanted to send a message or announce an event, they could pay him two cents to include it in his program.</t678> <t679>Thanks to this, one night Úrsula listened to his songs with the hope that they would mention José Arcadio, and she accidentally learned of her mother's passing.</t679> <t680>He was called Francisco - The Man because in a singing contest he had defeated the devil, and besides, no one knew his real name.</t680> <t681>During the insomnia epidemic that plagued the village of Macondo, he quietly disappeared, and now, without any prior notice, he was back in Catarino's store. The whole village flocked to hear him to see what new things had happened in the world.</t681> <t682>On this occasion, accompanying him was a fat woman sitting on a rocking chair carried by four Indian porters, with a lonely-looking mixed-race girl holding an umbrella to shade her.</t682> <t683>That night, Aureliano went to Catarino's store. He saw old Francisco - The Man, like a petrified chameleon, sitting among the curious crowd listening to his tales...</t683> <t684>With a wheezing voice accompanied by an old accordion, which had been given to him by Mr. Oante Rahch on the island of Goadana.</t684> <t685>He sang to tell the news, while his large, rough, cracked feet from walking through sulfur mines kept stomping to keep the rhythm.</t685> <t686>The madam sat on a rocking chair, fanning herself at the back door where some men were coming and going.</t686> <t687>With a felt rose tucked behind his ear, Catarino sold glasses of guarapo - nearly everyone placed their hands on him right where they shouldn't.</t687> <t688>As the night wore on, the heat became unbearable.</t688> <t689>Aureliano sat and listened to the news until the end of the program but found nothing useful for his family.</t689> <t690>As he was preparing to go home, the madam pointed her finger, signaling to him:</t690>
<t691>- You should come in too, - she said, - it only costs two hào!</t691>
<t692>Aureliano put two dimes into the dish the woman held at her side and then stepped into the room without knowing what to do.</t692> <t693>The mixed-race girl was lying naked on the bed with breasts that looked like a dog's.</t693> <t694>That night, before Aureliano entered, sixty-three men had already passed through this room.</t694> <t695>The air in the room, thickened by the sweat and breath of so many people, had started to condense into a muddy substance.</t695> <t696>The girl stripped the soaking wet bed sheet and asked Aureliano to hold one end.</t696> <t697>It was extremely heavy.</t697> <t698>The two of them twisted the ends of the sheet until it was wrung dry.</t698> <t699>They tilted the bed, and sweat streamed down the tilted side, dripping onto the floor.</t699> <t700>Aureliano fervently wished that this task would never end.</t700> <t701>In theory, he knew the art of lovemaking, but his knees were weak, and despite his skin being covered in goosebumps and feeling feverish, he couldn't suppress the urgent need to pass gas.</t701> <t702>When the bed was made, the girl ordered him to undress, and he clumsily explained, "They told me to come in.</t702> <t703>They told me to put two dimes in the money dish and not to delay."</t703> <t704>The girl understood his anxiety. "If you put two more dimes at the exit, you can stay a few more minutes," she said gently.</t704>
<t705>Aureliano undressed, looking miserable with shame, unable to shake off the thought that his naked body could not compare to his brother's robust physique.</t705><t706>Despite the girl's attempts to be seductive, he still felt lonely, terribly lonely.</t706><t707>"I'll add two more cents," he said in a dejected voice.</t707><t708>The girl quietly thanked him.</t708><t709>Her back was bright red.</t709><t710>Her skin clung to her bones, revealing each rib, and her breath was rapid and erratic, full of exhaustion.</t710><t711>Two years ago, in a distant village, the girl had fallen asleep without extinguishing a candle, and when she awoke, she was right next to the fire.</t711><t712>The house where she lived with her grandmother, who had raised her since she was a baby, had turned to ashes.</t712><t713>Since then, her grandmother had taken her from village to village, forcing her to sleep with men for two cents each time to save up enough to compensate for the value of the burned house.</t713><t714>According to the girl's calculations, she still had more than ten years to go, offering herself every night to seventy men, because besides paying her grandmother, she also had to earn enough to cover travel expenses, food for both of them, and wages for the Indians who carried the rocking chair.</t714><t715>When the madam knocked on the door for the second time, Aureliano left the room without having done anything, feeling so pained he wanted to cry.</t715><t716>That night, he did not sleep, only thinking about the girl with a mix of love and pity.</t716><t717>He felt both love and a desire to protect the girl.</t717><t718>It was an intense feeling that could not be stopped.</t718><t719>At dawn, exhausted from lack of sleep and fever, he made a healthy decision: he would marry the girl to free her from her grandmother's control and to enjoy with her the nights of pleasure she had to share with seventy men.</t719><t720>But at ten in the morning, when he arrived at Catarino's store, the girl had already left the village.</t720>
<t721>Time gradually soothed Auréliano's naive intentions, but it deepened his sense of failure.</t721><t722>He buried himself in work.</t722><t723>He resolved to remain single for life, to bury the shameful thought of considering himself useless.</t723><t724>Meanwhile, Melquíades had finished recording on his films everything that could be captured in the village of Macondo and had left the darkroom to the passions of José Arcadio Buendía, who had decided to use it for a scientific experiment to prove the existence of God.</t724><t725>Through a complex process of presenting photographs taken at different locations in the house, he was convinced that sooner or later there would be a photograph of God, if God indeed existed, and at the same time, it would end all speculation about His existence.</t725><t726>Melquíades delved deeper into the teachings of Nostradamus.</t726><t727>He worked until dusk, suffocating in his faded felt coat, with his tiny sparrow-like hands, the rings on his fingers having lost their luster from times past, scribbling on sheets of paper.</t727><t728>One night, he thought he had found a prophecy about the future of Macondo.</t728><t729>It could become a radiant city, with enormous crystal buildings, where there would be no trace of the Buendía lineage.</t729><t730>"That is a mistake," José Arcadio Buendía angrily said.</t730><t731>"Those are not crystal buildings, but ice buildings, as I have seen in my dreams, and no matter how many centuries pass, there will always be a Buendía."</t731><t732>In that bizarre house, Úrsula was the one trying to maintain a normal atmosphere by expanding the business of making candy figurines, with an oven that in one night produced baskets and baskets of bread, and batches of puddings, cakes, and biscuits that within a few hours spread throughout the paths of the swampy region.</t732><t733>Though she was of an age to rest, she became even more active.</t733><t734>So busy with these profitable tasks, clothes seemed to place them in a new phase of life.</t736>
<t737>Completely contrary to what might await her in the future, Rebecca was the most beautiful girl.</t737> <t738>She had skin as white as celery, large and deep eyes, and dexterous hands that seemed to be weaving invisible threads on an embroidered tapestry.</t738> <t739>Amaranta, the younger girl, though slightly less graceful, had a natural elegance, an inner tranquility inherited from her late grandmother.</t739> <t740>Beside the two girls was Arcadio.</t740> <t741>Despite having inherited his father's stature, Arcadio was still just a little boy.</t741> <t742>He decided to learn the trade of a jeweler under the guidance of Aureliano, who, besides teaching the trade, also taught the boy to read and write.</t742> <t743>Immediately, Ursula realized that her house would soon be cramped, that her children were of marriageable age and would have children of their own, and due to lack of space, they would be forced to scatter.</t743> <t744>So she took the money she had saved over many years of hard work, made contracts with her clients, and began expanding the house.</t744>
<t745>She decided to build two living rooms, one of which would be fully equipped and more airy for daily use, and a dining room large enough to accommodate a big dining table with twelve seats for family and guests to dine together, nine bedrooms all with windows opening to the yard, and a long corridor with balconies holding pots of ferns and begonias, outside of which was a rose garden shielding from the harsh midday sun.</t745> <t746>She also decided to demolish the old kitchen to build two ovens; to dismantle the old corn warehouse where Pila Tecnêra had read José Arcadio's fortune through the cards, to construct a new warehouse twice as large, sufficient to store food supplies for many years.</t746> <t747>She decided to erect a male bathhouse and a female bathhouse, and at the end of the yard, a large stable, a chicken coop with wire mesh, a milking barn, and an aviary with open doors to welcome birds that could freely come and go. Followed by dozens of carpenters and masons, as if she had caught her husband's gold fever, Úrsula arranged the positions for light and ventilation, dividing the spaces without forgetting their boundaries.</t747> <t748>The house, originally built by the pioneering ancestors, suddenly became crowded with saws, chisels, and construction materials, and the tired, sweaty workers were asking everyone else not to disturb the objects, unaware that they themselves were the ones causing all the disruption.</t748> <t749>They went mad because of the bag of human bones that rattled, clattering everywhere they went.</t749> <t750>In the unpleasant scene of having to breathe the strong smell of lime and tar, no one understood why a house was rising from the ground, not only larger than any seen in the village but also the most hospitable and coolest ever in the entire swamp region.</t750> <t751>José Arcadio Buendía, who thought he would astonish the Almighty with the great changes, was the one who understood it the least.</t751> <t752>When the house was nearly complete, Úrsula pulled him out of his dream world to inform him that there was an order requiring the front walls of all houses to be painted blue instead of white, as the villagers preferred.</t752> <t753>She showed him the paper with the official order written on it.</t753> <t754>José Arcadio Buendía, still not understanding what his wife was saying, was trying to read the signature:</t754>
<t755>- Who is this person? - He asked.</t755>
<t756>- That's the inspector, - Ursula replied sadly.</t756> <t757>- People say that he is a new official sent by the government.</t757>
<t758>Apollinar Moscote, the magistrate, quietly arrived in the village of Macondo.</t758> <t759>He got off the vehicle and stayed at the Jacob Hotel, established by one of the first Arabs who came here to trade goods for long-tailed parrots.</t759> <t760>The next day, he rented a room that opened onto the street, just two blocks away from Buendía's house.</t760> <t761>He set up a table and a chair bought from Jacob, hung the national emblem he had brought with him on the wall, and placed a sign on the door that read: Magistrate.</t761> <t762>His first order of business was to instruct the houses in the village to be whitewashed in celebration of Independence Day. José Arcadio Buendía, holding a copy of the order, found him napping in a hammock strung up in a sparsely furnished and decorated room.</t762> <t763>"Are you the one who wrote this paper?" he asked.</t763> <t764>Apollinar Moscote, a mature man with a timid demeanor and a flushed face, replied that he was. "In the name of what did you write this?" José Arcadio Buendía continued to ask.</t764>
<t765>Đông Apôlina Môscôtê found a piece of paper stored in the desk drawer and showed it to him: "I have just been appointed as the Inspector of this village".</t765> <t766>Hôsê Accađiô Buênđya didn't bother to look at the appointment letter, and said:</t766>
<t767>- In this village, we do not issue orders with paperwork - he said calmly, - and just so you know, we do not need any Inspector, because there is nothing here that needs inspecting.</t767>
<t768>Faced with the firm attitude of Don Apolinar Moscote, who never raised his voice, José Arcadio Buendía recounted in detail how they had built the village, how they had divided the land, how they had opened roads, and how they had developed the necessary and profitable trades for life, without ever bothering the government and without anyone ever bothering the villagers.</t768>
<t769>"We live so peacefully that our village has never had anyone die from illness or old age," he said. "You see, our village doesn't even have a cemetery yet!"</t769> <t770>He was not at all distressed that the government had not helped the villagers.</t770> <t771>On the contrary, he was pleased to see that up to this point, the government had allowed the villagers to continue living that way, because they did not waste their efforts establishing the village only for others to come and tell them what to do. Don Apolinar Moscote, wearing a twill fabric coat as white as his trousers, never lost his calm demeanor.</t771>
<t772>- So if you want to stay here, like other ordinary residents, you will be warmly welcomed, - José Arcadio Buendía concluded, - otherwise, if you have come to cause trouble and force the villagers to whitewash their walls, you can pack your bags and go back to where you came from.</t772> <t773>Because my house must be as white as a dove.</t773>
<t774>Don Apolinar Moscote turned pale. He took a step back, gritting his teeth with an embarrassed look, and said:</t774>
<t775>- I inform you that I have a weapon.</t775>
<t776>José Arcadio Buendía did not know since when the strength of a young man capable of breaking a bull's horn had concentrated entirely in his hands.</t776> <t777>Grabbing Apolinar Moscote by the lapels of his cotton jacket, he lifted him to eye level and said:</t777>
<t778>- I'll do this, - he said, - because I want to carry it alive and not have to regret for the rest of my life carrying a dead body.</t778>
<t779>He kept carrying him along the main road until his hand got tired and he let go of the lapel, then he set him down.</t779> <t780>At that moment, the Inspector was already on the road outside the marsh.</t780> <t781>A week later, he returned with six barefoot soldiers, all dressed in rags, each carrying a musket, and an ox cart carrying his wife and seven daughters.</t781> <t782>After that, two more ox carts arrived carrying beds, chairs, tables, cabinets, trunks, and kitchen utensils.</t782> <t783>He lodged his family at the Jacob Hotel while looking to buy a house and resumed his office with guards outside.</t783>
<t784>The founders of the village of Macondo were ready to expel the invaders.</t784> <t785>Together with their grown sons, they came to support the determination of José Arcadio Buendía.</t785>
<t786>But he objected because, as he explained, Apôhna Môscôtê had returned here with his wife and daughters, and as a man, one should not embarrass another in front of their family.</t786> <t787>So he decided to handle the matter in a more amicable manner.</t787>
<t788>Aureliano went with him.</t788> <t789>At this time, he had a black mustache that curled up at the ends and a booming voice, which later became distinguishing features that made him stand out in the war.</t789> <t790>Without carrying any weapons and not obeying the guards' orders, the father and son walked straight into the Inspector's office.</t790>
<t791>Don Apolinar Moscote remained calm.</t791> <t792>He introduced to them his two daughters who happened to be there.</t792> <t793>Amparo, sixteen years old, with tanned skin like her mother's, and Remedios, approximately nine years old, a charming little girl with ivory skin and blue eyes.</t793> <t794>Both were graceful and well-mannered.</t794> <t795>As swiftly as they had entered, before being introduced by their father, the girls had already brought chairs for the guests to sit on.</t795> <t796>But both father and daughters remained standing.</t796>

<t798>Apollinar Moscote was bewildered, but José Arcadio Buendía did not give him time to respond: "We only ask two things of you," he said.
