<t803>- Those are the words of an enemy, - José Arcadio Buendía said.</t803> <t804>But with a bitter tone, he clarified:</t804>
<t805>- Because I want to make it clear for you to know: You and I will continue to be enemies.</t805>
<t806>That very afternoon, the soldiers had to leave in a hurry.</t806> <t807>A few days later, José Arcadio Buendía found a house for the Inspector's family.</t807> <t808>Everyone lived peacefully, except for Aureliano. The image of Remedios, the Inspector's youngest daughter, who by age could have been his own child, lingered painfully in his mind.</t808> <t809>It was a mechanical sensation, like a pebble in a shoe that hurts the foot with every step.</t809>
<t810>Chapter 4</t810>
<t811>A dance party was organized to celebrate the new house, white as a dove.</t811> <t812>Ursula had cherished that idea ever since the afternoon she saw Rebeca and Amaranta had become young ladies, and it could almost be said that the main reason for building the new house was the desire to provide the girls with a worthy place to receive guests.</t812> <t813>While carrying out the reconstruction of the house, Ursula toiled like a convict so that no obstacle could extinguish that brilliant, radiant purpose.</t813>
<t814>Therefore, before these tasks were completed, she had already sent for the purchase of decorations and food containers, all of which were expensive items.</t814> <t815>Among them was a marvelous object that had astonished the entire village and delighted the youth, which was the automatic piano.</t815> <t816>People transported its parts to the house.</t816> <t817>These parts were placed in several crates and were unpacked along with furniture made in Vienna, mirrors produced in Bohemia, dishes from the West Indies Company, Dutch tablecloths, and a series of oil lamps and candles, vases, floral silk wall hangings, and carpets.</t817> <t818>The import company had sent an experienced Italian specialist named Pietro Crespi to the house to assemble and tune the automatic piano, to teach the buyers how to use it, and especially to instruct them in dancing to the fashionable music printed on six rolls of tape.</t818>
<t819>Piêtrô Crêspi was a young man with red hair, one of the most handsome and educated individuals in Macondo, who took such pride in his appearance that, despite the sweltering heat, he always wore a tight-fitting silk suit and a thick woolen overcoat.</t819><t820>Sweating profusely, trying to maintain a clear distance from his hosts, he stayed for several weeks in a closed room, with a passion identical to Aureliano's in the jewelry workshop.</t820><t821>One morning, without opening the door, without calling upon any divine witness, he placed a music roll in the automatic piano, and pure, melodious music began to play, causing the harsh hammering and constant clattering of the wooden planks to suddenly cease in the startling silence.</t821><t822>Everyone hurriedly rushed to the living room.</t822><t823>José Arcadio Buendía became excited not because of the enchanting beauty of the music but because the automatic piano was playing the keys by itself, and he placed Melquiades' camera in the living room, hoping to capture a photograph of the invisible musician.</t823><t824>That day, the Italian maestro had lunch with the family.</t824><t825>Rebeca and Amaranta, who were serving, were both charmed by the gentle and graceful demeanor of the refined man as his ringless, pale green hands handled the silverware at the table.</t825><t826>In the dining room connected to the living room, Piêtrô Crêspi taught the two girls to dance.</t826><t827>Keeping time with a metronome, he taught them to move their feet without touching their bodies, under the endearing supervision of Úrsula, who never left the living room while her daughters were learning to dance.</t827><t828>During those days, Piêtrô Crêspi wore special, soft, tight-fitting clothes and a pair of dancing shoes.</t828><t829>"You have nothing to worry about," José Arcadio Buendía told his wife. "This fellow is just a hermaphrodite."</t829><t830>However, she continued to keep watch until her daughters' dance lessons were over and the Italian man had left Macondo.</t830><t831>So she began preparing for the celebration.</t831><t832>Úrsula made a list of the guests to be invited to the party, selecting only the descendants of the founders of Macondo, excluding the family of Pilar Ternera, who by then had two more illegitimate children.</t832><t833>In fact, it was a unique selection determined by friendships, as those chosen were not only close friends of the José Arcadio Buendía family since before the migration that led to the founding of Macondo but also because their descendants had been childhood friends of Aureliano and Arcadio.</t833><t834>Their daughters were the only girls who regularly visited to embroider with Rebeca and Amaranta.</t834><t835>Don Apolinar Moscote, a kind-hearted official whose duties were limited to maintaining two policemen armed only with batons and supporting them within his modest means, was merely an authority figure in name.</t835><t836>To ensure daily expenses, his daughters had to open a small shop where they made felt flowers, prepared guava jelly, and wrote love letters for the young men and women in the village.</t836><t837>Despite their humility and good manners, his daughters—beautiful and the most graceful dancers of the new dances in the village—could not find a way to be invited to the celebration.</t837>
<t838>While Úrsula and the girls opened the box to take out the utensils, the shiny bowls, dishes, cups, and hung on the walls the paintings of noble ladies standing on boats filled with caged flowers, and with these tasks, the mother and her daughters breathed new life into the polished rooms created by the masons, José Arcadio Buendía abandoned his quest to find the image of God, admitting the nonexistence of the deity, and dismantled the automatic piano to understand its mysteries.</t838> <t839>After many days of fumbling with the tangle of wires stretching out at one end only to coil back at the other, and a heap of surplus pegs and levers, José Arcadio Buendía managed to reassemble the automatic piano, but did so very poorly.</t839> <t840>He completed this task two days before the celebration.</t840> <t841>Never before had there been so much anxiety and bustling in this house as in those days.</t841> <t842>Yet the new lamps, freshly lit with tar, still shone brightly at the appointed day and hour.</t842> <t843>The house was wide open, still fragrant with the smell of pine resin and damp lime.</t843> <t844>The descendants of the founders of Macondo familiarized themselves with the hallways adorned with potted ferns and begonias, the serene rooms, the garden fragrant with roses, and then gathered in the living room before the machine, previously unknown, covered with a white cloth.</t844> <t845>Those who were familiar with the commonly used pianos in the villages around the swamp felt a bit uneasy.</t845> <t846>But the most bitter disappointment was Úrsula's when a music roll was placed in the automatic piano for Rebeca and Amaranta to start the dance, but the mechanism did not move at all.</t846>
<t847>Menkyađêt, now almost blind, tried to recall the skills from his vast repository of ancient knowledge to repair the automatic piano.</t847> <t848>Finally, purely by chance, José Arcadio Buendía managed to make a dead machine turn, and thus the music that initially sounded mournful began to flow like a stream of sound composed of incomprehensible notes.</t848> <t849>The piano hammers struck chaotically.</t849> <t850>They kept hitting the strings, which were arranged haphazardly, without any order, causing them to vibrate with a trembling, fearful sound.</t850>
<t851>But the stubborn descendants of the twenty-one brave souls who once traversed the forest heading west to find the sea, managed to overcome the difficult spots caused by changes in the music, and thus the dance lasted until morning.</t851>
<t852>Piêtrô Crêspi returned to repair the automatic piano.</t852>
<t853>Rebecca and Amaranta helped him arrange the order of the strings, and they joined him in laughing at the tricky parts of the waltzes.</t853> <t854>He displayed such a respectable and noble demeanor that Úrsula did not feel the need to supervise her daughters' dance lessons.</t854> <t855>The night before his departure, an impromptu dance with the newly repaired automatic piano was organized to bid him farewell.</t855> <t856>He and Rebecca performed the modern dances exceptionally well.</t856>
<t857>Accadio and Amaranta were no less graceful and lively in their movements.</t857><t858>But the performance had to be stopped because Pila Tecnera, who was outside the door with the curious onlookers, got into a fight with a woman who dared to comment that Accadio had a woman's buttocks.</t858><t859>At midnight, Pietro Crespi bid farewell to the family with an emotional speech, promising to return soon.</t859><t860>Rebecca saw him to the main door, and after closing all the doors and turning off the house lights, she went to her room to cry.</t860><t861>It was a mournful cry that lasted several days, the reason for which no one knew, not even Amaranta.</t861><t862>She was always a mysterious person, and that was not unusual.</t862><t863>Despite her seemingly open and polite exterior, she was actually a lonely person with an inscrutable heart.</t863><t864>She was a splendid adolescent girl, slender and fond of sitting on the wooden rocking chair she had brought to the house, which had been repaired many times and lacked armrests.</t864><t865>No one discovered that at her age she still had the habit of sucking her thumb.</t865><t866>Therefore, she never missed an opportunity to be alone in the bathroom and maintained the habit of sleeping facing the wall.</t866><t867>On rainy afternoons, while sitting and embroidering with a group of girlfriends in the hallway with begonias, she would suddenly lose track of the lively conversation, letting a tear of longing roll down her cheek and salt her tongue as she looked at the wet patches of earth and the small mounds of clay that worms pushed up in the garden.</t867><t868>When she began to cry, those secret pleasures, once defeated by orange juice mixed with rhubarb powder in previous years, resurfaced in an uncontrollable desire.</t868><t869>She ate earth again as she had before.</t869><t870>At first, she ate it almost out of curiosity, believing that the unpleasant taste of the earth would be an effective remedy against her desire.</t870><t871>And indeed, she could not stand the earth in her mouth.</t871><t872>But as her growing craving overcame her, she forced herself to endure it.</t872><t873>It was the old craving, the fondness for eating minerals, the eager joy of consuming primal food.</t873><t874>She stuffed pieces of earth into her pockets and secretly ate them one by one without being seen.</t874><t875>She ate them, feeling both a sense of blissful satisfaction and bitter resentment while teaching her girlfriends the most intricate embroidery stitches and talking about men who were unworthy of the sacrifices she made, such as eating wall lime for them.</t875><t876>These pieces of earth became less ancient and more distinct, representing the only man worthy of such self-destruction, as if the ground he trod with his elegant varnished boots somewhere else in the world transmitted to the girl the weight and warmth of his blood in the taste of minerals, leaving a bitter sensation in her mouth and a lasting sediment of peace in her heart.</t876>
<t877>One afternoon, without any particular reason, Amparo Moscote suddenly asked to visit the house.</t877> <t878>Amaranta and Rebeca, bewildered by the unannounced visit, reluctantly received her.</t878> <t879>They showed her the newly renovated house, let her listen to music from the automatic piano, and offered her oranges with biscuits.</t879> <t880>Amparo presented herself as a cheerful and well-mannered young lady, which moved Úrsula during the moments she sat with them to entertain the guest.</t880> <t881>After two hours, when the conversation had begun to wane, Amparo took advantage of Amaranta's distraction to hand Rebeca a letter.</t881> <t882>The girl only had time to see the line: "To the distinguished Miss Rebeca Buendía," written in neat handwriting, with blue ink and a spacing style that was both airy and pleasing to the eye, similar to the instructions for using the automatic piano. Then she carefully folded and tucked the letter into her bodice with her fingers.</t882> <t883>She looked at Amparo Moscote with a gaze that conveyed immense gratitude and silently vowed to fulfill the wish until her dying day.</t883>
<t884>The sudden friendship between Amparo and Rebeca awakened Aureliano's hopes. The longing for the girl Remedios tormented him incessantly, but he had no opportunity to see her again.</t884> <t885>When he strolled around the village with his old friends like Macrophico Visban and Herinendo Maquet - the sons of the founders with the same names - he eagerly searched for the girl in the tailor's shop with his eyes, but he only saw her sisters.</t885> <t886>Amparo Moscote's presence in his house felt like an omen.</t886> <t887>"She will come with her sister," Aureliano told himself.</t887> <t888>"The little sister will come."</t888> <t889>He repeated it many times with all his faith.</t889> <t890>Thus, one afternoon while making a goldfish in the workshop, he was certain that the girl had responded to the call of his heart.</t890> <t891>Indeed, shortly thereafter, he heard a clear, innocent voice, and when he looked up with a startled heart, he saw the girl in a pink gauze dress, wearing white boots, standing right at the door.</t891>
<t892>- Remedios, don't go in there! - Amparo Moscote called from the hallway.</t892> <t893>- They are working in there!</t893>
<t894>But Aureliano did not let the girl listen to her sister: He handed her the goldfish with a small ring in its mouth and said to her:</t894>
<t895>- Come in! Come in!</t895>
<t896>The little girl walked closer and asked him a few questions that Aureliano couldn't answer, because a sudden asthma attack prevented him.</t896> <t897>He wanted to be with the little girl forever, close to her ivory skin, close to her emerald green eyes, close to her voice that, with each question, called him "sir" with all the respect she still had for her father.</t897> <t898>Melquiades sat in the corner of the room next to the writing desk, jotting down unreadable symbols.</t898> <t899>Aureliano was furious with him.</t899> <t900>He could do nothing except tell Remedios that he would give her a goldfish, and she, horrified by the gift, quickly ran out of the workshop.</t900> <t901>That afternoon, Aureliano lost his composure, the deep calm he usually had while waiting for a chance to see the little girl.</t901> <t902>He neglected his work.</t902> <t903>In desperate attempts to concentrate, he called her name many times, but Remedios did not respond.</t903> <t904>He looked for her at her sisters' dress shop, during visits to her house, in her father's office, but he only saw her in the image steeped in his own terrible loneliness.</t904> <t905>For hours and hours, he sat with Rebeca in the living room, listening to waltzes played by the automatic piano.</t905> <t906>Rebeca listened to the waltzes because Pietro Crespi had taught her to dance to that music.</t906> <t907>And Aureliano listened to the waltzes because, quite simply, that music reminded him of Remedios.</t907>
<t908>The house was filled with love, Aureliano expressed his love in poetry, verses without beginning or end.</t908> <t909>He wrote them on the parchment given by old Melquiades, on the bathroom walls, on the skin of his own arms, and in those verses, he depicted a distorted Remedios: Remedios in the fleeting breeze at two in the afternoon, Remedios in the silent breath of roses, Remedios in the steaming bread fresh from the oven in the morning, Remedios everywhere and Remedios forever and ever.</t909> <t910>Standing by the window embroidering, Rebeca longed for love to arrive at four in the afternoon.</t910> <t911>She knew that the mailman's donkey only came to the village once every fortnight, but she always hoped, telling herself that by mistake it might come any day.</t911> <t912>But the opposite happened: one time the donkey did not come on the scheduled day.</t912> <t913>So the girl went mad with disappointment.</t913> <t914>In the middle of the night, she woke up to eat handfuls of earth from the flower garden.</t914> <t915>In her desperate desire to commit suicide, she cried bitterly, both in sorrow and anger, while chewing on soft worms and crunching the shells of snails.</t915> <t916>She vomited until morning. She collapsed from exhaustion.</t916> <t917>She lost her sanity, and her heart pounded restlessly without shame.</t917> <t918>Ursula, horrified, unlocked the chest and found at the bottom sixteen fragrant letters carefully tied with pink ribbons, along with skeleton leaves and dried flower calyxes hidden in old books, and the dried bodies of butterflies that would turn to dust at the slightest touch.</t918>
<t919>Aureliano was the only one who understood the boundless suffering.</t919>
<t920>That afternoon, while Úrsula was trying to treat Rebeca from her stupor, he, along with Magnífico Visbal and Hernando Márquez, went to Catarino's eatery. The eatery had been expanded with a row of wooden rooms, rooms for the single women who wore makeup.</t920> <t921>A band consisting of drums and accordion played songs by old Francisco - The Man who had disappeared from the village of Macondo years ago.</t921> <t922>The three friends drank guarapo.</t922> <t923>Magnífico and Márquez were contemporaries of Aureliano, but they were more worldly than him in the ways of revelry.</t923> <t924>Both of them drank freely with the girls sitting on their laps.</t924> <t925>One of those girls, the withered one with gold teeth, had actively flirted with Aureliano. But he did not like her.</t925> <t926>He realized that the more he drank, the more he missed Remedios intensely, but the tormenting longing was more bearable.</t926> <t927>He did not know when he started to feel light and buoyant.</t927>
<t928>He saw his friends along with the floating women swimming in the radiant reflected light, speaking words that seemed not to come from their mouths, making mystical movements that seemed disconnected from their gestures.</t928>
<t929>Mr. Catarinô placed his hand on his back and said, "It's almost eleven o'clock."</t929> <t930>Aurêlianô turned his face around and saw a large, distorted face with a felt flower tucked behind the ear, and he immediately lost his memory as he had previously suffered from amnesia. Then he remembered that: on a distant early morning in a completely unfamiliar room to him, where Pila Tecnêra was present, dressed in underwear, barefoot, with disheveled hair, holding an oil lamp illuminating his face, and she was half-believing, half-doubting in astonishment:</t930>
<t931>- Oh my God, is that you, Aureliano</t931><t932>!</t932>
<t933>Aureliano stood firm on his two feet, raising his head.</t933> <t934>He had forgotten how he got there but still clearly knew his purpose, because since childhood he had carried that purpose with him and kept it hidden in the deepest recesses of his heart.</t934>
<t935>- I came to sleep with you!</t935>
<t936>He wore clothes stained with mud and vomited food.</t936><t937>Pila Tecnêra, who was then living alone with her two youngest children, did not ask him a word.</t937><t938>She took him to bed.</t938><t939>With a wet cloth, she wiped his face, undressed him, and then she undressed herself, letting down the mosquito net so the little children wouldn't see if they woke up.</t939><t940>She was tired of waiting for a man to stay, then waiting for the men who had left, then hoping that all were mistaken due to the inaccuracy of the cards.</t940><t941>While waiting, her skin wrinkled, her breasts sagged, and her heart no longer burned with the fervor of old love.</t941><t942>She groped for Aureliano in the dark and kissed his neck with the affectionate tenderness of a mother: "Oh, my poor little boy."</t942><t943>Aureliano shuddered with a languid touch, noiselessly, he left behind the shores of pain and met Remedios, who had turned into a vast lake with no horizon, redolent of young animals and freshly ironed clothes.</t943><t944>When he found himself drifting aimlessly on that lake, he wept.</t944><t945>At first, they were sobs of pent-up, bitter anguish.</t945>
<t946>Then he awkwardly swam in a boundless source of water, feeling something both slightly painful and achingly about to burst inside his stomach.</t946> <t947>By flicking his head with her finger, she waited until his body expelled a grayish substance that left him exhausted.</t947> <t948>At that moment, Pila Tecnera asked him: "Who is that?".</t948>
<t949>Aureliano told her the name of that person.</t949> <t950>She let out a laugh, the kind of laugh that used to startle the pigeons but now did not wake the children.</t950> <t951>"Then you must marry the girl."</t951> <t952>She mocked him.</t952> <t953>But in that mockery, Aureliano found a quiet place of sympathy.</t953> <t954>When he left the room, he had left behind not only the uncertainty about his own strength but also the bitter weight that had been choking his heart for many months.</t954> <t955>Pilar Ternera suddenly promised him:</t955>
<t956>- I promise to go talk to the girl, - she said, - and you will see that bringing her to you is not difficult at all.</t956>
<t957>She fulfilled her promise at an inopportune moment because Buendía's house had lost the tranquility of the earlier days.</t957> <t958>When she discovered Rebeca's passion, which could not be kept secret because the girl kept screaming incessantly, Amaranta also became feverish.</t958> <t959>The girl also felt the painful thorn of lonely love.</t959>
<t960>Locking herself in the bathroom, the girl calmed the storm of a hopeless passion by writing passionate letters that she contentedly hid at the bottom of a chest.</t960> <t961>Úrsula barely had the strength to take care of her two sick daughters.</t961> <t962>During the long and delicate inquiries, she still could not understand what had led Amaranta to such a state of decline.</t962> <t963>Finally, in a moment of sudden insight, she broke the chest's lock and found the letters tied with a pink ribbon, swollen with fresh lilies, still smudged with tears, meant to be sent but never to be sent to Pietro Crespi.</t963> <t964>Weeping with indignation, she cursed the moment she got the idea to buy the automatic piano, banned the embroidery lessons, and declared mourning, a mourning without a dead person, for a long period until her daughters' disillusionment passed.</t964> <t965>José Arcadio Buendía, who had by then corrected his initial impression of Pietro Crespi and admired his skillful use of musical machines, intervened in the matter, but all his efforts were in vain.</t965> <t966>So when Pilar Ternera informed Aureliano that Remedios was ready to get married, he understood that this news would end all the distress for his parents.</t966>
<t967>But that very news put him face to face with the actual situation.</t967>
<t968>Invited to the clinic to attend a regular seminar, José Arcadio Buendía and Úrsula calmly listened to their son's expressions.</t968> <t969>When he learned the name of his son's lover, José Arcadio Buendía's face turned red with shame.</t969> <t970>"Love is a plague," he angrily thundered.</t970> <t971>"There are so many beautiful and respectable girls for you, yet you go and take the daughter of my enemy."</t971> <t972>But Úrsula approved of her son's choice.</t972> <t973>She expressed her affection and admiration for the seven Moscote sisters, all of whom were beautiful, hardworking, well-mannered, and well-educated.</t973> <t974>And she congratulated her son on his wise choice of a lifelong partner.</t974> <t975>Persuaded by his wife's enthusiasm, José Arcadio Buendía set one condition: Rebeca, who was suitable, would marry Pietro Crespi.</t975>
<t976>During a trip when there is time, Úrsula will take Amaranta to the city so that interacting with a different crowd will gradually help her feel better, and she will wake up from her delusion.</t976>
<t977>Rebecca recovered very quickly when she learned of the family's unanimous opinion and immediately wrote a cheerful letter to inform her lover of her parents' official decision, then she put it in the mailbox without needing an intermediary anymore.</t977>
<t978>Amaranta pretended to comply with her parents' decision and gradually recovered from the fever, but she kept telling herself that if Rebeca wanted to marry Pietro Crespi, she would have to step over her dead body.</t978> <t979>A week later, José Arcadio Buendía dressed in a dark wool suit, a collared shirt, and the chamois leather boots he had worn on the night of the new house celebration, went to ask for Remedios Moscote's hand in marriage for his son.</t979> <t980>The magistrate and his wife welcomed him with a mix of joy and worry, because they did not know the purpose of this sudden visit, and then they thought he might have mistaken the name of the chosen daughter.</t980> <t981>To avoid any misunderstanding, the mother woke Remedios and carried her into the living room.</t981> <t982>The little girl was still half-asleep.</t982> <t983>They asked her if she really wanted to get married, and she cried and replied that she just wanted everyone to leave her alone so she could sleep. José Arcadio Buendía, understanding the Moscotes' confusion, went home to explain everything to Aureliano.</t983> <t984>When he returned to the magistrate's house, the Moscotes had dressed according to protocol, rearranged the furniture, and replaced the flowers in the vase.</t984> <t985>Weary from doing something against his will and from the tight collar that was choking him, José Arcadio Buendía confirmed that Remedios was indeed the one Aureliano had chosen to marry.</t985>
<t986>"It's not going well at all," Apolinar Moscote said sadly.</t986> <t987>"We still have six daughters, all of whom are unmarried and of marriageable age, and they would all be delighted to become the proper wives of respectable and hardworking young men like the one in your family, but unfortunately, Aureliano only fancies the little girl who still wets the bed."</t987>
<t988>His wife, a woman who still retained a graceful charm, with slightly melancholic eyes and demeanor, had opposed his foolish words.</t988> <t989>When everyone finished drinking the fruit juice, the Moscotes couple happily accepted Aureliano's proposal. However, Mrs. Moscote requested to speak privately with Mrs. Úrsula. Partly out of curiosity, partly out of self-pity for being troubled by men's issues, but actually very fearful and emotional, Úrsula visited Mrs. Moscote the next day.</t989> <t990>Half an hour later, she returned with the news that Remedios was not yet of age.</t990> <t991>Aureliano did not consider this a major obstacle.</t991> <t992>He had waited long enough that he could wait as long as necessary until the girl reached puberty.</t992>
<t993>The house regained its peaceful and warm atmosphere, and it was only disrupted when Melquiades passed away.</t993> <t994>Although his death was an obvious event, the exact timing of it was unpredictable.</t994>
<t995>After returning for a few months, a rapid and intense aging process occurred in the old man’s body, to the extent that he was immediately regarded as one of those decrepit old men, wandering around like shadows in the room, heavily dragging his feet, mumbling loudly about the glorious memories of his life. In reality, old people were those who were not cared for or remembered by anyone until one day, they were found dead, stiff on their beds in the morning.</t995> <t996>At first, José Arcadio Buendía helped him, fascinated by the novelty of photography and the prophecies of Nostradamus.</t996> <t997>But gradually, he left him alone in his own lonely world, because communication between the two became increasingly difficult.</t997> <t998>He became more and more blind and deaf, as if he mistook the people talking to him for those he knew in the distant past of humanity, and he answered their questions with rambling sentences mixing various languages together.</t998> <t999>As if guided by direct intuition, he groped through the air as he walked.</t999> <t1000>Even though he had to weave between objects, he never knocked them over. One day, he forgot to put in his false teeth, which were still soaking in a cup of water next to his bedside.</t1000> <t1001>and he never used them again.</t1001> <t1002>When Úrsula decided to expand the house, she made him a private room right next to Aureliano’s jewelry workshop.</t1002>
<t1003>This room is far from the noise and kitchen work, with a window letting in abundant light into the room and a bookshelf filled with books almost tattered by dust and gnawed by cockroaches, scraps of paper densely covered with illegible writing, and a water jug containing false teeth that had tiny yellow flowers blooming on water lettuce, all arranged by Úrsula herself.</t1003> <t1004>The new room seemed to please old Melquíades because he was never seen leaving his room, not even during mealtimes when he was absent from the dining room.</t1004> <t1005>He only went to Aureliano's workshop and stayed there for hours and hours to write incomprehensible literature on parchment.</t1005> <t1006>It seemed as though these parchments would crack because they were made from a material as dry as puff pastry.</t1006> <t1007>There, he ate the meals brought by Visitación twice a day, although in his final days he did not like to eat and only consumed vegetables.</t1007>
<t1008>Not long after, he bore the lonely and sorrowful appearance of those who eat vegetarian.</t1008> <t1009>A layer of young moss, identical to the thick moss on his all-season coat that he never took off, had covered his skin, and his breath reeked of the farts of a sleeping animal.</t1009> <t1010>Aureliano also forgot him, as he was engrossed in writing his poems.</t1010> <t1011>But there were times when he thought he understood a few things the old man said during his aimless monologues, and he listened intently.</t1011> <t1012>In truth, the only thing he could distinguish in the old man's incomprehensible words was the endless repetition of the word Equinox and the name Alexander von Humboldt.</t1012> <t1013>Arcadio was closer to him when he helped Aureliano in the goldsmith's workshop.</t1013> <t1014>Melquiades responded to this effort to communicate by occasionally bursting out in long strings of Spanish that had little to do with the reality of life.</t1014> <t1015>However, there was one afternoon when he suddenly became cheerful for a moment.</t1015> <t1016>In later years, standing before the firing squad, Arcadio remembered his horror when he heard Melquiades read a few pages of the manuscript.</t1016> <t1017>Of course, he did not understand their meaning, but when he heard the old man read them aloud, he felt they sounded like the Pope's decrees.</t1017> <t1018>After that, for the first time in many years, he smiled and said in Spanish: "When I die, burn me in a fire of mercury for three days right in this room."</t1018> <t1019>Arcadio recounted this to José Arcadio Buendía, who then tried every way possible to get Melquiades to explain more clearly, but he only received a terse reply: "I have reached the level of immortality."</t1019> <t1020>When Melquiades' breath became foul, Arcadio took him to bathe in the river every Thursday morning.</t1020> <t1021>He seemed to get better.</t1021> <t1022>He took off his clothes and waded into the river to bathe with the children, and his mystical intuition guided him to avoid the dangerous rocky parts of the river.</t1022> <t1023>"We are all born from water," he once said.</t1023> <t1024>He lived like that for quite a long time, although no one saw him in the house except when he made a great effort to repair the automatic piano.</t1024> <t1025>When he went to the resurrection."</t1029> <t1030>He revived the forgotten water pipes and boiled a cauldron of mercury beside the corpse, which was increasingly covered in greenish blisters.</t1030> <t1031>Don Apolinar Moscote boldly reminded him that not burying a drowned body immediately would endanger the health of the villagers.</t1031> <t1032>"That's not true, because he is still alive."</t1032> <t1033>That was José Arcadio Buendía's reply, who carried out the full sixty-two hours of burning mercury beside the corpse, and when the greenish blisters on the body began to burst like popcorn with a popping sound, filling the house with an unpleasant smell.</t1033> <t1034>Only then did he allow the burial, but not in any ordinary way, but with the solemn ceremonies befitting the greatest benefactor of Macondo.</t1034> <t1035>It was the first and most moving funeral ever seen in the village, and it was only overshadowed by the funeral of the Great Mother nearly a century later.</t1035> <t1036>He was They held nine nights of mourning for him.</t1036> <t1037>In the crowd gathered outside to drink coffee, chat, and play cards, Amaranta found an opportunity to confess her love to Pietro Crespi, who had officially proposed to Rebeca a few weeks earlier and had set up a music and leather toy shop at the spot where the Arabs used to stay to trade trinkets for long-tailed parrots, and the villagers still called it Turkish Street. The Italian, with his golden curly hair that made women sigh with longing, did not have to think much to treat Amaranta like a mischievous little girl.</t1037>
<t1038>- I also have a younger brother, - he told the girl, - he will come to help me look after the store.</t1038>
<t1039>Amaranta felt hurt, and she furiously told Pietro Crespi that she was determined to stop her sister's wedding, even if they had to drag her dead body out of the house.</t1039>
<t1040>The Italian man was so moved by that tragic threat that he didn't dare to tell Rebecca.</t1040> <t1041>Amaranta's trip, always urged by Ursula, was prepared within a week.</t1041> <t1042>Amaranta did not resist, but when she kissed Rebecca goodbye, she whispered in her sister's ear:</t1042>
<t1043>- Don't celebrate too soon.</t1043> <t1044>Even if they take you to the ends of the earth, I will find a way to stop you from getting married, and if that's the case, I will have to kill you.</t1044>
<t1045>The house seemed to grow larger and emptier without Úrsula at home and with the death of Melquíades, but it seemed as if his shadow still flitted through the rooms.</t1045> <t1046>Rebeca took care of the household chores, while the Indian girl managed the candy shop.</t1046> <t1047>In the early evening, when Pietro Crespi, dressed in clothes scented with lavender, arrived, always bringing a toy as a gift, his beloved would receive him in the parlor with all the doors wide open to avoid any suspicion.</t1047> <t1048>This was an unnecessary precaution because the young Italian had shown himself to be so noble that he did not dare to hold the hand of the girl who would be his wife a year before their wedding.</t1048> <t1049>Those visits gradually filled the house with toys.</t1049> <t1050>Dancing dolls that moved with a wind-up key, music boxes, monkeys performing tricks on a wire, galloping horses, drumming clowns—this was a rich and marvelous collection of semi-mechanical toys that consoled José Arcadio Buendía in his sorrow over Melquíades' death and brought him back to the time when he was eager about alchemy.</t1050> <t1051>Thus, he lived in a world of dissected animals and machines taken apart to study and perfect a system of continuous movement based on the principles of the pendulum clock.</t1051>
<t1052>As for himself, Aureliano abandoned his work in the goldsmith's shop to teach little Remedios how to read and write.</t1052> <t1053>At first, the little girl demanded that the guest who often visited in the afternoons bring her doll parts, and she would be a reprehensible child if people made her sit in the room and entertain guests.</t1053> <t1054>But with perseverance and dedication, Aureliano managed to entice the little girl to the point where she would sit with him for hours, absorbing the lessons and using colored pencils to draw on a small board pictures of little houses with cows grazing in the yard and a round sun perched atop the mountain, radiating golden rays.</t1054>
<t1055>Only Rebecca herself suffered from Amaranta's threat.</t1055> <t1056>She understood very well the character, stubbornness, and deep hatred of her younger sister.</t1056> <t1057>Those things were making her afraid.</t1057>
<t1058>Hours spent sitting in the bathroom, she sucked her finger to curb the craving to eat dirt.</t1058> <t1059>She called Pila Tecnêra to her house for a fortune-telling session to ease her worries.</t1059> <t1060>After enthusiastically shuffling and arranging the cards, Pila Tecnêra entered a trance and read the fortune:</t1060>
<t1061>- I won't be happy until my parents are properly buried!</t1061>
<t1062>Rebecca shuddered.</t1062> <t1063>Like a dream, she remembered herself being very small, entering the house carrying a small trunk, a wooden rocking chair, and a sack whose contents she never knew.</t1063> <t1064>She remembered a bald gentleman, dressed in linen with a gold button on his shirt collar, who had nothing to do with the man in the card.</t1064> <t1065>She remembered a very young, glamorous lady with soft hands that smelled of perfume, unlike the rheumatic hands of the woman in the card; that lady had adorned her hair with flowers and taken her for a walk around the village with its green paths in the golden afternoon.</t1065>
<t1066>- I don't understand, - she said.</t1066>
<t1067>Pila Tecnêra was confused:</t1067>
<t1068>- You too, but the cards have spoken.</t1068>
<t1069>Rebecca was terrified, restless day and night when she thought about that prediction, and had to tell José Arcadio Buendía, who immediately scolded her, refusing to validate the card's prediction.</t1069> <t1070>But in reality, he silently searched the house for the sack of human bones: rummaging through trunks and chests, moving furniture, and turning over beds, but still couldn't find it.</t1070> <t1071>He remembered that he hadn't seen it since they rebuilt the house.</t1071> <t1072>He secretly called the masons to the house, and one of them confirmed that he had placed the sack of human bones into a wall cavity and plastered it over because it kept bothering him, distracting him from his work, but now he couldn't remember which room the wall belonged to.</t1072> <t1073>After a few days of searching, with their ears pressed against the walls, they found a faint clattering sound, clatter, clatter, clatter, whispering from within the wall.</t1073> <t1074>They broke through the wall and found the sack of human bones still intact.</t1074> <t1075>That very day, the two sets of human bones were buried in an unmarked grave right next to Melquíades' grave.</t1075> <t1076>José Arcadio Buendía returned home with a sense of relief, as if he had just shed a burden similar to the memory of Prudencio Aguilar that had once weighed heavily on his conscience.</t1076> <t1077>As he passed through the kitchen, he kissed Rebecca on the forehead and said:</t1077>
<t1078>- Shake off those pitiful thoughts, my child! - He said - From now on, you will be happy.</t1078>
<t1079>Rebecca's friendship opened the door for Pilar Ternera to come to the house.</t1079> <t1080>She had been banned by Úrsula since the day Arcadio was born.</t1080> <t1081>Like the noisy running of a herd of female goats, she came to the house at any time and eagerly did all the heavy work.</t1081> <t1082>Sometimes she was present in the jewelry workshop and with lively gestures helped Arcadio smooth out photo paper with affectionate feelings.</t1082> <t1083>Eventually, she made him feel awkward.</t1083> <t1084>That woman confused him.</t1084> <t1085>Her tanned skin, burnt smell, and giggles in the dark room made him breathe rapidly and hastily, constantly making him awkward, bumping into objects around him.</t1085>
<t1086>There were also times when Aureliano was present in the room, engrossed in his work, and Pilar Ternera leaned against the table, admiring his diligent and meticulous work.</t1086> <t1087>Suddenly, she went into a trance.</t1087> <t1088>Aureliano clearly realized that Arcadio was in the room.</t1088> <t1089>Before looking up and catching Pilar Ternera's eyes, revealing thoughts as clear as if they were laid bare under the midday sun, he asked:</t1089>
<t1090>- Alright! Tell me, what is it?</t1090>
<t1091>Pila Tecnêra licked her lips and then gave a sad smile:</t1091>
<t1092>- You are well-suited for war, - she said, - wherever you set your eyes, the bullet you shoot will reach there.</t1092>
<t1093>Aureliano raised his hand in agreement with that prediction.</t1093> <t1094>He refocused his thoughts on his work as if nothing had happened and spoke in a calm yet firm voice:</t1094>
<t1095>- I know, - he said, - that war will bear my name.</t1095>
<t1096>Finally, José Arcadio Buendía accomplished what he wanted to do.</t1096> <t1097>He connected the dancing doll to the clockwork mechanism, and it danced to the music for three consecutive days.</t1097> <t1098>This achievement made him happier than any of his previous exhausting endeavors.</t1098> <t1099>He did not eat.</t1099> <t1100>He did not sleep.</t1100> <t1101>Without Úrsula by his side to care for and look after him, he let himself drift into an imaginary world, which led him to a state of constant excitement.</t1101> <t1102>He spent long sleepless nights pacing back and forth in the room, muttering calculations, trying to apply the principles of the clock pendulum to ox carts, to plows, in short, to anything that could be set in motion.</t1102> <t1103>The fever from insomnia exhausted him so much that one early morning he could not recognize an old man with white hair who suddenly entered the bedroom with a bewildered gesture.</t1103> <t1104>It was Prudencio Aguilar. When he recognized Prudencio Aguilar, he was surprised to see that the dead also aged over the years.</t1104> <t1105>José Arcadio Buendía shivered with nostalgia. "Ah, Prudencio," he exclaimed, "how did you manage to come here from so far away?"</t1105> <t1106>After so many years in the realm of the dead, how intense was the longing for the living, how pressing was the need for companionship, how terrifying was the proximity of another death that already existed within the realm of the dead, which Prudencio Aguilar had just wished upon his worst enemy.</t1106> <t1107>Prudencio Aguilar had spent a long time searching for him.</t1107> <t1108>Prudencio Aguilar inquired about him through the dead souls in Riohacha, through the dead souls in the Uga valley, through the dead souls from the marshlands, but no dead soul gave him the correct answer, because Macondo at that time was still a village unknown to the dead souls.</t1108> <t1109>It was not until Melquíades arrived and pointed out a black dot on the multicolored maps of death.</t1109> <t1110>José Arcadio Buendía talked with Prudencio until dawn.</t1110> <t1111>A few hours later, exhausted from lack of sleep, José Arcadio Buendía entered Aureliano's workshop and asked him, "What day is it today?"</t1111> <t1112>Aureliano replied to his father that it was Tuesday. "I thought so too," José Arcadio Buendía said.</t1112>
<t1113>"But immediately I understood that today was still Monday, just like yesterday."</t1113> <t1114>"Look at the sky, look at the statues, look at the begonias."</t1114> <t1115>"Today is still Monday."</t1115> <t1116>Accustomed to such nonsensical words, Aureliano did not listen to him.</t1116> <t1117>The next day, on Wednesday morning, José Arcadio Buendía entered the workshop again.</t1117> <t1118>"This is a disaster," he said, "Look at the wind, listen to the buzzing of the sun, just like yesterday, and the day before."</t1118> <t1119>That night, Pietro Crespi found him to fly.</t1121> <t1122>And Pietro replied that it was impossible because a clock pendulum could lift any object into the air but could not lift itself.</t1122> <t1123>On the hundredth day, he was back <t1125>Aureliano scolded his father like a child, and he obediently listened.</t1125> <t1126>For six continuous hours, he inspected objects, trying to find some difference from the previous day to <t1130>So he grabbed the wooden bar used to block the door and with his inherent brutal strength, he smashed to pieces the alchemical laboratory equipment, the goldsmith's workshop, and the camera.</t1130> <t1131>Then, like a demon, he screamed in a language that was both resonant and incessant, yet utterly incomprehensible.</t1131> <t1132>He was ready to destroy the entire house when fortunately, Aureliano had called the neighbors for help.</t1132> <t1133>It took ten strong men to bring him down, fourteen to tie him up, and twenty to drag him to the chestnut tree in the middle of the yard, where they left him tied up, free to bark out strange words and spit out green phlegm. <t1138>Chapter 5</t1138>
<t1139>One Sunday in March, Aureliano Buendía and Remedios Moscote were married before the altar of God, which Father Nicanor Reyna had set up right in the Buendía family's living room.</t1139> <t1140>It was a period of intense activity lasting four consecutive weeks in the Moscote household, because little Remedios reached puberty before she had abandoned her childhood habits.</t1140> <t1141>Although her mother had explained the sudden changes of puberty to her, one February afternoon she suddenly panicked and cried out, showing her sisters, who were sitting and embroidering, a slip stained with a slimy chocolate-colored substance.</t1141> <t1142>The wedding date was set for a month later.</t1142> <t1143>There was hardly enough time to teach the girl how to wash herself, dress herself, and understand the essential tasks of family life.</t1143> <t1144>They made her urinate on heated bricks to cure her bedwetting.</t1144> <t1145>It was very difficult to convince her of the inviolability of the bridal chamber, because Remedios was both very frightened and very excited by the instructions to the point that she wanted everyone to talk about the wedding night in all its details.</t1145> <t1146>It was a tremendous effort, but on the day chosen for the wedding, the girl appeared as worldly-wise as any of her older sisters.</t1146>
<t1147>Mr. Apolina Moscote took his daughter by the arm and led her through the village streets adorned with flowers, resonating with the sound of firecrackers and joyful cranes.</t1147> <t1148>The little girl waved and smiled in thanks to those who wished her luck and happiness from their windows.</t1148>
<t1149>Aureliano, dressed in a black wool suit, wearing dark brown boots with spurs, the same boots he would wear years later when facing the firing squad, with a pale, ashen face and a throat tight with emotion, greeted his bride at the door and led her to the altar. Buendía.</t1155><t1156>Her joyous day did not come to pass.</t1156><t1157>According to an agreement with Úrsula, her wedding was also to be held on the same day as Aureliano's, but on Friday, Pietro At Aureliano's wedding, she sang a sad song she had composed specifically for her son's wedding.</t1159>
<t1160>Piêtrô Crêspi returned at midnight on Sunday to clean up the cigarette butts from the party after he had raced like the wind, causing five horses to collapse along the way, trying to make it back in time for his own wedding.</t1160> <t1161>The letter writer would never be discovered.</t1161> <t1162>Scolded by Úrsula, Amaranta wept bitterly, swearing on her life and before the altar of God, which the carpenters had not yet removed.</t1162>
<t1163>Father Nicanor Reyna - the person brought here by Apolinar Moscote to perform the wedding ceremony - was a patient man in his exceedingly dull and monotonous work.</t1163> <t1164>He had a sorrowful complexion that clung to his bones, a round, protruding belly, and the overly gentle, almost foolish demeanor of an old deity.</t1164> <t1165>He intended to return to his parish immediately after the wedding, but he was astonished by the excessively rustic and uncouth nature of the people of Macondo, who had proliferated abundantly, living blindly according to natural laws, refusing to baptize their children or give them saint names for the holidays.</t1165>
<t1166>Believing that this land needed God's seeds more than anywhere else, the priest decided to stay another week to convert this village into a village of the Lord, to conduct weddings for couples living together as they pleased, and to baptize those who were gravely ill.</t1166><t1167>But no one listened to the priest.</t1167><t1168>They told the priest that for many years they had lived without the guidance of a pastor, that they resolved their spiritual issues directly with God, and that they had completely abandoned their sinful habits.</t1168><t1169>Weary of preaching in the wilderness, Father Nicanô decided to build the largest church in the world, with life-sized statues and walls inlaid with colorful glass. Such a grand church would attract believers from as far as Rome, bringing glory to God right in the heart of this godless land.</t1169><t1170>The priest went around the village with a brass plate in hand to collect donations.</t1170><t1171>The villagers gave a lot, but the priest wanted more, because the church needed a bell whose sound could make the bodies of drowned people rise to the surface.</t1171><t1172>The priest pleaded so much that he became hoarse.</t1172><t1173>His bones ached as if ants were crawling inside them.</t1173><t1174>One Saturday, unable to get anything, not even fruits or food left at the door, the priest became fearful out of despair.</t1174><t1175>He hastily set up an altar in the middle of the square, and on Sunday, ringing a bell as if during a time of rampant insomnia, he went around the village urging everyone to attend the rustic mass.</t1175><t1176>Many people came out of curiosity.</t1176><t1177>Others came out of longing.</t1177><t1178>Some attended the mass to avoid having God see their neutral stance as personal opposition.</t1178><t1179>So at eight in the morning, half the village had gathered in the square to watch Father Nicanô, hoarse from pleading, reciting the Gospel.</t1179><t1180>At the end of the mass, as the attendees began to disperse, the priest raised his hands high to signal everyone to listen:</t1180>
<t1181>- Wait? Wait? - father said.</t1181> <t1182>- Now we will see an indisputable proof of the infinite power of God.</t1182>
<t1183>The little boy who earlier helped the priest conduct the mass now brought him a cup of thick, steaming hot chocolate, which the priest drank in one continuous gulp.</t1183> <t1184>After that, the priest took the handkerchief tucked in his wide sleeve to wipe his mouth, spread his arms wide, and closed his eyes tightly.</t1184> <t1185>Thus, Father Nicanô lifted himself twelve centimeters off the ground.</t1185> <t1186>It was an easily acceptable trick.</t1186>
<t1187>In a few days, the priest went to every house to remind people of the experiment of lifting oneself off the ground through the neural stimulation power of chocolate.</t1187> <t1188>Meanwhile, the altar boy had collected so much money in the sack that in less than a month, the construction of the church had begun.</t1188> <t1189>No one doubted the divine origin of that demonstration, except for José Arcadio Buendía, who, without moving, observed the chaotic crowd surrounding the chestnut tree one morning to once again see the priest lift himself off the ground.</t1189> <t1190>He almost just slightly stretched his legs on the chair and shrugged when Father Nicanor began to lift himself off the ground along with the chair he was sitting on.</t1190>

<t1192>Father Nicanor raised his hand and immediately the four legs of the chair lowered to stand on the ground.</t1192>
<t1193>- I deny it - he said. - In fact, this proves the existence of God without a doubt.</t1193>
<t1194>Thus it was known that the incomprehensible demonic language of José Arcadio Buendía was Latin.</t1194> <t1195>Father Nicanor took advantage of the opportunity, being the only one who could communicate with José Arcadio Buendía, to instill Christian faith into his deranged mind.</t1195> <t1196>Every afternoon, the priest would sit by the chestnut tree and preach in Latin, but José Arcadio Buendía, delighted, would not acknowledge the old worn paths nor the changing color of chocolate and demanded to see a picture of God, considering it as the ultimate proof.</t1196> <t1197>So Father Nicanor brought him badges and pictures and even a reproduction of a cloth embroidered with the image of Saint Veronica, but José Arcadio dismissed them as handicrafts without scientific basis.</t1197> <t1198>He was so stubborn that Father Nicanor had to abandon his intention of converting him to Christianity. <t1201>José Arcadio Buendía did not play, as he could never understand the meaning of a contest in which the two opponents had agreed on the principles.</t1201> <t1202>Father Nicanor, who had never seen anyone play chess in such a manner, also did not return to play checkers.</t1202> <t1203>Day by day, Father Nicanor admired José Arcadio Buendía's intelligence more and more.</t1203> <t1204>He asked him how he could be tied to the tree like that.</t1204>
<t1205>- This is very simple, - he replied - because I am mad.</t1205>
<t1206>From that point on, taking care of his faith, the pastor no longer visited him, resolutely starting the construction of the church immediately.</t1206> <t1207>Rebecca felt her hope rekindling.</t1207> <t1208>Her future depended on the completion of this work.</t1208> <t1209>She thought so because since that Sunday when Pastor Nicano had lunch at their house and the whole family gathered around the dining table to talk about the solemn and grand ceremonies in religious activities once the church was completed.</t1209> <t1210>"The luckiest person here is Rebecca," Amaranta said.</t1210> <t1211>And seeing that Rebecca did not understand what she meant, she explained with an innocent smile:</t1211>
<t1212>- Oh, it's because your wedding will coincide with the inauguration of the church.</t1212>
<t1213>Rebecca hurriedly blocked any comments about this matter.</t1213> <t1214>According to the construction progress, the church cannot be completed before ten years.</t1214> <t1215>Father Nicanor disagreed because he believed that with the newfound generosity of the faithful, he could make the optimists even more hopeful.</t1215> <t1216>In response to Rebecca's silent sulking, who had to leave her meal unfinished, Ursula agreed with Amaranta's idea and contributed a significant portion of her wealth to accelerate the construction progress.</t1216> <t1217>Father Nicanor believed that with another contribution like this, the house would be completed in less than three years.</t1217>
<t1218>From that moment on, Rebeca refused to speak to Amaranta, telling herself that Amaranta's provocative attitude was not as innocent as she had thought.</t1218> <t1219>"That is the least serious thing that could happen," Amaranta retorted to Rebeca in the fierce argument that took place that night.</t1219> <t1220>"So, for the next three years, I won't need to kill you."</t1220> <t1221>Rebeca accepted that challenge.</t1221>
<t1222>When Pietro Crespi learned that his wedding was postponed again, he fell into a state of despair.</t1222> <t1223>In contrast, Rebeca demonstrated her loyalty.</t1223> <t1224>"We will elope whenever you decide," she said.</t1224> <t1225>However, Pietro Crespi was not one to take risks.</t1225> <t1226>He lacked the passionate nature of a lover and was overly scrupulous, treating promises as treasures that could not be squandered.</t1226> <t1227>So Rebeca resorted to bolder tactics.</t1227> <t1228>A strange wind blew out the lamp in the living room, and Úrsula caught the two of them kissing in the dark.</t1228> <t1229>Pietro Crespi clumsily explained to her about the poor quality of modern candles that burned with tar and helped her set up a more reliable lighting system in the living room.</t1229> <t1230>But another time, the lamp was either filled with the wrong oil or the wick slipped.</t1230> <t1231>Úrsula caught Rebeca sitting on her lover's lap again.</t1231> <t1232>So she refused to listen to any more excuses.</t1232> <t1233>She assigned the Indian girl to watch over the candy store and sat in the rocking chair herself to supervise the lovers' conversations, determined not to be outdone by the old tricks she was familiar with from her own youth.</t1233>
<t1234>"Poor mother!", Rebecca said with all the resentment mixed with a bit of humor as she watched Ursula yawning in the tense atmosphere of the visit.</t1234> <t1235>"When I die, I might just go on this rocking chair."</t1235> <t1236>After three months of living in a love that was closely monitored, disheartened by the delays in the construction work that he visited every day, Pietro Crespi agreed to contribute the money Father Nicanor needed to complete the church building. Amaranta did not lose heart. While sitting and chatting with the sisters who often came to the house in the afternoons to embroider or knit on the porch, she tried to come up with new schemes.</t1236> <t1237>A mistake ruined the scheme she thought was the most effective.</t1237> <t1238>It was the plan to steal the mothballs that Rebecca had placed in her bridal gown before storing it in the chest in the bedroom.</t1238> <t1239>Amaranta would do it when there were less than two months left until the church construction was completed.</t1239> <t1240>But Rebecca was so eager for her wedding day to come that she wanted to prepare her bridal gown earlier than Amaranta had planned.</t1240> <t1241>When she opened the chest, then the wrapping paper, and finally the silk covering, she found the satin wedding dress, a a week.</t1243> <t1244>One rainy afternoon, Amparo, wearing a raincoat, entered the house to give the final fitting of the bridal gown for Rebecca, and Amaranta felt so furious she wanted to <t1248>That afternoon, while Rebecca was suffocating from the heat in the satin dress being fitted by Amparo, who was calmly pinning it to fit her body, Amaranta had pricked her fingers several times with the crochet hook.</t1248> <t1249>But with terrifying coldness, she decided that the day to poison Rebecca would be the Friday closest to the wedding day, and the method would be a drop of opium <t1250>A major obstacle, very brutal and also very unexpected, came crashing down, forcing the wedding to be postponed once again, with no idea of when it would finally take place.</t1250><t1251>Just one week before the wedding, suddenly at midnight, Remedios woke up vomiting and having diarrhea, her body drenched in the hot, foul-smelling contents from her stomach, and after three days, she died from poisoning by her own blood, carrying a twin fetus in her womb.</t1251><t1252>Amaranta tormented herself with guilt.</t1252><t1253>She had fervently prayed to God for something terrible to happen so that she wouldn't have to poison Rebeca, and thus she felt guilty for Remedios's death.</t1253><t1254>This was not the obstacle she had prayed for.</t1254><t1255>Remedios had brought a new, joyful breeze into the house.</t1255><t1256>She and her husband moved into the room next to the jewelry workshop, and she decorated their living space with all her childhood dolls and toys. Buendía.</t1259><t1260>She brought food to her father-in-law, diligently attended to him during his bathroom needs, bathed him with soap and scrubbing pads, kept his beard and hair free from lice, and
