<t1275>Aureliano was very fond of his resourceful father-in-law.</t1275> <t1276>"You'll end up as plump as he is," his friends told him. But the leisure that had once puffed up his cheeks and concentrated the light in his eyes did not increase his body weight nor did it disrupt his pride; on the contrary, it only accentuated the lines on his lips, a sign of deep thought and unshakable determination.</t1276> <t1277>The deep affection between him and his wife awakened the feelings of both families to such an extent that when Remedios announced she was pregnant, even Rebeca and Amaranta reconciled to weave a blue linen if it was a boy and a pink linen if it was a girl.</t1277> <t1278>She was the last person Arcadio thought of a few years later when he stood before the firing squad.</t1278>
<t1279>Ursula decided to mourn her for a year.</t1279> <t1280>She ordered all the doors to be closed, no one to enter or leave except in unavoidable circumstances, forbade loud talking, and personally hung Remedios' picture at the place where her body was laid out, placing an oil lamp that would burn continuously in front of the picture.</t1280> <t1281>Future generations, who never let the oil lamp go out, were puzzled by the picture of the girl in a puffed dress, white boots, and a bow in her hair, unable to reconcile that image with their own imagination of an aunt.</t1281> <t1282>Amaranta took in Aureliano José and raised him as her own son.</t1282> <t1283>He would be the one to share her loneliness and console her from the torment of her fervent prayers that had inadvertently caused the opium to fall into Remedios' coffee.</t1283> <t1284>In the evenings, Pietro Crespi, wearing a mourning band on his hat, would tiptoe into the house to quietly visit Rebeca, a Rebeca who seemed to be bleeding out in her black dress with sleeves that covered the backs of her hands.</t1284> <t1285>The mere thought of rescheduling the wedding was already considered highly improper, thus their romantic relationship became an eternal one, a weary love that no one wanted to tend to, as if the lovers who had turned off the lights to kiss in the dark had drifted apart by the will of death.</t1285> <t1286>Completely disoriented and having lost her sanity, Rebeca began eating earth again.</t1286>
<t1287>The mourning had been going on for quite some time, to the point where the family had to reorganize the prayer sessions at home.</t1287><t1288>At two in the afternoon on a quiet, unbearably hot day, someone suddenly pushed open the door leading to the main road, causing the doors to creak with such force that it shook the entire house where Amaranta and her friends were sitting and embroidering in the hallway, Rebeca was sucking her thumb in the bedroom, Úrsula was busy in the kitchen, Aureliano was diligently working in the goldsmith's workshop, and José Arcadio Buendía was sitting alone under the chestnut tree; everyone felt a shiver as if there was an earthquake.</t1288><t1289>A burly man came to the house.</t1289><t1290>His broad back seemed too large to fit through the door.</t1290><t1291>Around his neck, he wore a chain with a medallion of Saint Remedios the Virgin.</t1291><t1292>His chest and arms were covered in tattoos of cemetery images.</t1292><t1293>On his wrist, he wore a bronze statue of the crucified Jesus.</t1293><t1294>He had weather-beaten dark skin, short and shaggy hair like a mule's mane, a strong, protruding jaw, and sad eyes.</t1294><t1295>His belt was twice as thick as a horse's rein.</t1295><t1296>His short boots had spurs and were reinforced with iron.</t1296><t1297>And his presence gave everyone the shiver of an impending earthquake.</t1297><t1298>He walked through the living room, then the waiting room, carrying old bags in his hands.</t1298><t1299>Then, like a thunderclap, he appeared in the hallway where the begonias were placed, where Amaranta and her friends, who were sewing, were so startled that they raised their hands holding the needles.</t1299>
<t1300>"Hello," he said to the girls in a tired voice, then threw the bags onto the desk.</t1300> <t1301>After that, he hurriedly walked into the inner house. "Hello," he said to Rebeca, who was looking at him in panic as he passed by the bedroom window. "Hello," he said to Aureliano in the jewelry workshop, who was straining all five senses to listen.</t1301> <t1302>He went straight into the kitchen, and here for the first time, he paused at the end of a journey around the world. "Hello," he said.</t1302>
<t1303>Ursula stood still for a second, her mouth agape, staring straight into his eyes.</t1303> <t1304>She let out a cry and then jumped up to hug his neck, crying and shouting with joy.</t1304> <t1305>It was José Arcadio.</t1305>
<t1306>He returned as poor as when he left, to the point that Ursula had to give him two pesos to pay for the horse rental.</t1306> <t1307>He spoke Spanish mixed with sailor slang.</t1307> <t1308>When people asked him where he had been, he answered, "Over there."</t1308> <t1309>He hung a hammock in the room reserved for him and slept for three days straight.</t1309> <t1310>When he woke up, after eating sixteen boiled eggs, he went straight to Catarino's eatery. There, his massive body sparked lively and curious discussions among the women.</t1310> <t1311>He organized a party with music and strong liquor for everyone.</t1311> <t1312>He challenged five men to arm wrestle him simultaneously.</t1312> <t1313>"No way," they said when they realized they couldn't budge his arm, "because you have the crucified Jesus helping you"... Catarino, who still didn't believe in his strength, bet him twelve pesos if he could lift his counter.</t1313> <t1314>José Arcadio lifted the counter, balanced it on his head, and carried it out to the middle of the street.</t1314> <t1315>It took eleven strong men to carry it back to its original place.</t1315> <t1316>In the lively, festive atmosphere, José Arcadio stood on the counter, showing off his muscular body covered in tattoos with blue and red writing in various languages.</t1316> <t1317>Turning to the women surrounding him, with insatiable greed, he asked who would pay more.</t1317> <t1318>The wealthiest woman gave him several dozen pesos.</t1318> <t1319>So he organized a raffle with tickets costing ten pesos each.</t1319> <t1320>It was an exorbitant price because the most popular woman could only earn eight pesos a night.</t1320> <t1321>But all the women accepted the price.</t1321> <t1322>They wrote their names on fourteen tickets, mixed them up, put them all in a hat, and then each woman drew a ticket.</t1322> <t1323>When only two tickets were left, he approached the women who were about to draw.</t1323>
<t1324>- Pay an extra five dollars per ticket, - José Arcadio urged, - and then I'll split it between the two of you.</t1324>
<t1325>He lived that way.</t1325> <t1326>He had circled the earth sixty-five times, living with exiled sailors.</t1326> <t1327>The women who slept with him that night in Catarino's shop had taken him stark naked to the dance hall so everyone could see his body, not a single millimeter without a tattoo: tattoos in the front, tattoos in the back, tattoos from his neck down to his toes.</t1327> <t1328>He could not integrate into family life.</t1328>
<t1329>He slept all day, and at night he stayed up gambling and testing his strength in the poor worker's neighborhood. On rare occasions, Úrsula managed to get him to sit at the dinner table.</t1329> <t1330>He came across as a likable person, especially when recounting his adventurous tales in distant lands.</t1330> <t1331>He had been shipwrecked and drifted for several weeks on the Japanese sea, surviving by eating the salted and sun-cooked flesh of a friend who had died of sunstroke, which had turned into a sweet-tasting jerky.</t1331> <t1332>One scorching afternoon in the Bay of Bengal, his ship defeated a sea dragon, inside which they found a helmet, belt buckles, and the weapons of an ocean explorer.</t1332> <t1333>In the Caribbean Sea, he had seen the ghost of the pirate Victor Hugo's ship, with its tattered sail and worm-eaten hull, forever lost on its way to the island of Guadeloupe.</t1333> <t1334>Sitting at the table, Úrsula sobbed as if she were reading letters never sent to her, in which José Arcadio recounted his triumphs and failures. "Here, the houses are so spacious, my child," she wept, "and there's so much food that we have to throw it to the pigs.</t1334> <t1335>But you're never home for me to rely on..."</t1335> <t1336>Yet deep down, she couldn't accept that the boy taken away by the gypsies had become this crude man who devoured half a suckling pig at lunch and whose hot breath withered the plants.</t1336> <t1337>The other family members thought similarly of him.</t1337> <t1338>Amaranta couldn't hide her disgust whenever he burped at the table.</t1338> <t1339>Arcadio, who never knew of his father-son relationship with José Arcadio, barely responded to his attempts to win his son's affection.</t1339> <t1340>Aureliano tried to revive the old days when they shared a room and reminisced about their childhood pranks, but José Arcadio had forgotten everything, as his sailor's life, filled with countless memories, had saturated his mind.</t1340> <t1341>Only Rebeca submitted to him from the very first moment.</t1341> <t1342>That afternoon, seeing him pass by her window, she thought Pietro Crespi was just a plump man compared to the extremely robust José Arcadio, whose volcanic breath filled the house.</t1342> <t1343>She found excuses to be near him.</t1343> <t1344>There were times when José Arcadio shamelessly ogled her body and said, "You are very womanly, my dear."</t1344> <t1345>Rebeca could no longer control herself.</t1345> <t1346>She resumed eating earth and wall lime with the same craving as before and sucked her thumb until it became calloused.</t1346> <t1347>She vomited a green liquid mixed with clotted blood.</t1347> <t1348>All night she lay awake, trembling with fever, battling her longing, waiting for dawn; the whole house shook with José Arcadio's footsteps as he returned.</t1348>
<t1349>One afternoon, while the whole family was taking a nap, she couldn't bear it any longer and sneaked into José Arcadio's bedroom.</t1349> <t1350>She saw him awake, wearing shorts, lying on a hammock tied with large iron rings and ship ropes.</t1350> <t1351>His muscular bare body moved her so much that she instinctively stopped and intended to leave.</t1351> <t1352>"Sorry"… she stammered, "I didn't know you were here".</t1352> <t1353>But she lowered her voice immediately so as not to wake anyone else.</t1353> <t1354>"Come in," he said.</t1354> <t1355>Rebeca obediently complied.</t1355> <t1356>She stood right next to the hammock, sweating coldly, feeling her stomach knotting up, while José Arcadio caressed her wrist with his five fingers, then her groin, then her buttocks, whispering: "Oh, little one.</t1356> <t1357>Oh, little one."</t1357> <t1358>The girl had to muster all her strength to avoid dying when a storm-like but very gentle force lifted her by the waist, undressed her, and laid her down like a small bird.</t1358> <t1359>She only had time to thank God for her existence before her consciousness was submerged in the soft, ecstatic pleasure that overwhelmed the pain, making her writhe in the steaming hammock.</t1359> <t1360>The hammock, like a blotting paper, immediately absorbed the blood from her virginity.</t1360>
<t1361>Three days later, they held the wedding ceremony during the mass at five in the afternoon.</t1361> <t1362>The day before that, Arcadio had gone to Pietro Crespi's shop.</t1362> <t1363>He found Pietro Crespi teaching a student to play the guitar and did not discreetly call him aside to talk.</t1363> <t1364>"I'm marrying Rebeca," he said.</t1364> <t1365>Pietro Crespi turned pale, handed the guitar to a student, and dismissed the class in the middle of the lesson.</t1365> <t1366>When only the two of them were left in the room filled with musical instruments and wind-up toys, Pietro Crespi said:</t1366>
<t1367>- Rebecca is your sister!</t1367>
<t1368>- To me, that doesn't matter, - José Arcadio replied.</t1368>
<t1369>Piêtrô Crêspi took out a lavender-scented handkerchief to wipe the sweat from his forehead.</t1369>
<t1370>- Taking it that way is against the laws of nature, he explained, - besides, the law strictly prohibits such a marriage.</t1370>
<t1371>- Oh, I don't care about your so-called natural order, - said José Arcadio.</t1371> <t1372>- I'm here to let you know so you won't bother Rebeca with questions.</t1372>
<t1373>Although his rude behavior had to disappear when he saw Pietro Crespi with tears in his eyes.</t1373> <t1374>"Now, here's the deal," he changed his tone, "if you still have feelings for my family, then Amaranta will be yours."</t1374>
<t1375>In the Sunday sermon, Father Nicanor demonstrated that José Arcadio and Rebeca were not biological siblings.</t1375>
<t1376>Ursula never forgave what she considered to be excessive rudeness, and when they returned from the church, she barred the newlyweds from entering the house.</t1376> <t1377>To her, they were now like the dead.</t1377> <t1378>So they bought a small house in front of the cemetery and moved to their new home without taking any furniture except for José Arcadio's hammock.</t1378>
<t1379>On their wedding night, Rebecca was bitten on the foot by a scorpion that had somehow gotten into her slipper.</t1379> <t1380>She was left speechless, but that didn't make their honeymoon any less enjoyable.</t1380> <t1381>The neighbors were startled by the screams that woke the entire neighborhood up to eight times in one night and three times in one afternoon, and they prayed that this excessive frenzy wouldn't disturb the dead resting peacefully in the tranquil cemetery nearby.</t1381>
<t1382>Aureliano was the only one worried about them.</t1382> <t1383>He bought some supplies and provided them with financial assistance until José Arcadio took a more realistic view of life and began to clear the unclaimed land adjacent to the yard.</t1383> <t1384>In contrast, Amaranta never ceased to hold a grudge against Rebeca, even though life had brought her unexpected joy.</t1384> <t1385>That joy was Pietro Crespi, who, with a calm and noble demeanor hiding his heartbreak, continued to have lunch with the family every Tuesday at Ursula's suggestion, as she still didn't know how to ease her embarrassment.</t1385> <t1386>Still wearing the black ribbon on his hat as a sign of his closeness to the family, he enjoyed expressing his admiration for Ursula by bringing her exotic gifts: Portuguese sardines, Turkish delight, and on special occasions, even a precious Manila shawl.</t1386> <t1387>Amaranta, with affectionate feelings, pampered him.</t1387> <t1388>She guessed his preferences, meticulously mended every loose thread on his sleeves, and to celebrate his birthday, she hemmed and embroidered their initials on a dozen handkerchiefs.</t1388> <t1389>On Tuesdays, after lunch, while Amaranta sat in the hallway, he always stayed by her side, chatting cheerfully.</t1389> <t1390>One Tuesday afternoon, when no one suspected what was about to happen, it happened: Pietro Crespi proposed to her.</t1390> <t1391>The girl continued to embroider calmly.</t1391> <t1392>Waiting for the heat in her ears to subside, she replied in an adult tone:</t1392>
<t1393>- Whatever, Crêspi, but let's wait until we really understand each other.</t1393> <t1394>There's no need to rush.</t1394>
<t1395>Ursula became confused.</t1395> <t1396>Although she had great respect for Pietro Crespi, she still couldn't determine whether, from a moral standpoint, her decision was good or bad, especially since this happened right after Pietro Crespi's prolonged and tumultuous relationship with Rebeca.</t1396> <t1397>However, she accepted that love, considering it an event that didn't require consulting others because at that time, no one shared her worries.</t1397> <t1398>Aureliano, who was now the authoritative man in the family, made her even more bewildered with his assertive and incomprehensible opinion:</t1398>
<t1399>- Now is not the time to discuss marriage.</t1399>
<t1400>In the following months, Úrsula finally understood that the statement was the only sincere answer that Aureliano could have given at that moment.</t1400> <t1401>It was not only a response to the matter of marriage but also to any issue unrelated to the war.</t1401> <t1402>When facing the execution squad's rifles, even he did not fully understand why a series of ironic coincidences had led him to that situation.</t1402> <t1403>The death of Remedios did not lead him to a terrifying agitation.</t1403> <t1404>Rather, it was merely a silent anger that gradually seeped into a quiet and lonely despondency, similar to the despondency he felt during the days he resolved to remain single.</t1404>
<t1405>He immersed himself in work again but still kept the habit of visiting his father-in-law to play dominoes: "Aureliano, get married," his father-in-law told him.</t1405> <t1406>"I have six daughters for you to choose from."</t1406> <t1407>Once, perhaps the event occurred before the election day, Don Apolinar Moscote returned to the village from the province, full of anxiety about the political situation of the country.</t1407> <t1408>The Liberals were ready to plunge into the fight.</t1408> <t1409>Since Aureliano still had vague notions about the differences between the Conservatives and the Liberals at that time, his father-in-law explained the general outlines to him.</t1409>
<t1410>Free men, he said, are Masons, wicked people who support hanging priests, advocate equal marriage and divorce, recognize the equality of illegitimate and legitimate children, and favor dividing the country into states that are federated to strip local powers and concentrate them in the supreme government.</t1410><t1411>In contrast, the Royalists, who have received power directly from God, protect the established public order and family morals, are the defenders of Christ, protect the foundations of power, and do not allow the country to be divided into free states.</t1411><t1412>Due to humanitarian sentiments, Aureliano sympathized with the liberal attitude of recognizing the rights of illegitimate children, but in any case, he could not understand why people had to go to war over intangible matters.</t1412><t1413>He felt his father-in-law was being extreme when he requested his superiors to send six soldiers under the command of a sergeant to Macondo, a village without political passions, to protect the election.</t1413><t1414>The soldiers not only came to the village, but they also went house to house, confiscating hunting weapons, machetes, and even kitchen knives, before distributing to men aged twenty-one and over a green ballot with the names of the Royalist candidates and a pink ballot with the names of the Liberal candidates.</t1414><t1415>Before the election day, Apolinar Moscote himself read a decree prohibiting the sale of liquor and gatherings of more than three people unless they were from the same household for forty-eight hours starting from midnight on Saturday.</t1415><t1416>The election proceeded smoothly.</t1416><t1417>As early as eight o'clock on Sunday morning, a wooden box guarded by six soldiers was placed in the square.</t1417><t1418>People voted freely, just as Aureliano could see for himself when he, along with his father-in-law, stood guard all day to ensure that no one could vote more than once.</t1418><t1419>At four in the afternoon, a drum roll in the square signaled the end of the election day, and Apolinar Moscote sealed the ballot box with a cross-marked tape bearing his signature.</t1419><t1420>That night, while playing dominoes with Aureliano, he ordered the sergeant to open the box and count the votes.</t1420><t1421>The number of pink and green ballots was roughly equal, but the sergeant left only ten pink ballots and replaced the rest with green ones to make up the difference.</t1421><t1422>Then the sergeant resealed the ballot box with a new label, and the next morning it was hurriedly taken to the provincial capital.</t1422><t1423>"The Liberals will rise up in civil war," Aureliano said.</t1423><t1424>Apolinar did not take his eyes off his domino tiles.</t1424><t1425>"If you mean because of the ballot change, they won't rise up," he said, "some pink ballots were left to keep them from complaining."</t1425><t1426>Aureliano understood the disadvantages of the opposing side.</t1426><t1427>"If I were a Liberal," he said, "I would rise up precisely because of this ballot change."</t1427><t1428>His father-in-law looked at him over his glasses.</t1428>
<t1429>- Oh Aureliano, - he said, - if you belong to the Liberal party, even if you are my son-in-law, you will never be able to witness the changing of the vote here.</t1429>
<t1430>In reality, what caused public outrage was not the election results but the fact that the soldiers did not return the weapons. A group of women spoke with Aureliano, asking him to request his father-in-law to return their kitchen knives.</t1430> <t1431>Don Apolinar Moscote, with utmost caution, explained to him that the soldiers had taken the confiscated weapons as evidence that the Liberals were preparing for war.</t1431> <t1432>The brazenness of that response shocked him.</t1432> <t1433>He did not comment, but one night, when Hernando Márquez and Magnífico Visbal were discussing the trouble over the knives with some friends, they asked him which side he belonged to: Liberal or Royalist. Without hesitation, he replied:</t1433>
<t1434>- If I have to choose between the two factions, I will side with the Liberals, - he said, - because the Royalists are swindlers.</t1434>
<t1435>The very next day, at the request of his friends, he went to see Dr. Alirio Noguera to have him examine his liver pain.</t1435><t1436>He didn't even know why he was doing it.</t1436><t1437>Dr. Alino Noguera had come to the village of Macondo a few years ago, bringing with him a cabinet of tasteless pills and an unconvincing advertisement sign: Fight poison with poison.</t1437><t1438>In reality, it was a joke.</t1438><t1439>Behind the mild exterior of a mediocre doctor was the face of a terrorist who wore knee-high boots to cover the scars on his ankles, marks from five years in prison.</t1439><t1440>From the very first federalist adventure, he had been arrested, escaped to Curaçao disguised in the clothes he hated most in the world: a priest's cassock.</t1440><t1441>After a ballots in the box were from the enthusiastic youth. "The only thing that works," he said, "is violence."</t1449><t1450>Most of Aureliano's friends were eager to sweep away the royalist regime, but none dared to involve him in the plan, not only because of his relationship with his father-in-law but also because of his solitary and indifferent nature.</t1450><t1451>Moreover, they knew he had voted green as directed by his father-in-law.</t1451><t1452>So it was merely a coincidence that revealed his political thoughts, and it was a completely curious impulse that led him to the absurd idea of visiting the doctor to treat a non-existent ailment.</t1452><t1453>In the cramped room reeking of cheap camphor, there was a man who looked exactly like a moldy iguana wheezing. Before he could ask anything, the doctor pulled him to the window and examined his lower eyelid.</t1453><t1454>"It's not there," Aureliano said, following his friends' instructions.</t1454><t1455>Pressing his finger on his abdomen where his liver was, he said, "This is where it hurts so much I can't sleep."</t1455><t1456>So Dr. Noguera closed the window, claiming it was too bright, and in simple terms explained to him why eliminating the royalists was a patriot's duty.</t1456><t1457>For several days, Aureliano carried a tube of pills in his shirt pocket.</t1457><t1458>Every two hours, he would take out the tube, pour three pills into his palm, and pop them all into his mouth, letting them dissolve slowly on his tongue.</t1458><t1459>Apolinar Moscote mocked his faith in the method of treating illness with illness, but those plotting rebellion recognized him as one of their own.</t1459><t1460>Almost all the sons of Macondo's founders were in the secret organization, but none knew exactly how the actions they were preparing would unfold.</t1460><t1461>However, on the day the doctor revealed the secret to Aureliano, he volunteered to join the uprising.</t1461><t1462>Although he agreed at the time that the royalist regime had to be quickly destroyed, the doctor's plan of action disgusted him.</t1462><t1463>Dr. Noguera was a great fan of individual terrorism.</t1463><t1464>His method of action involved coordinating a series of individual acts so that in a large-scale national uprising, the royalist officials and their entire families, especially their children, would be exterminated to eradicate the royalist lineage.</t1464><t1465>Of course, Apolinar Moscote, his wife, and daughters were all on the terrorist list.</t1465>
<t1466>- You are neither a Liberal nor anything else - Aureliano said calmly, - you are a murderer, nothing more, nothing less.</t1466>
<t1467>- In that case, - the doctor said calmly, - please return the tube of medicine to me.</t1467> <t1468>You don't need it anymore.</t1468>
<t1469>Six months later, Aureliano learned that the doctor did not believe he was a man of action, had no hope for him, considering him a sentimental person, overly gentle and lonely.</t1469> <t1470>They tried to monitor him because they feared he would reveal the rebellion plans.</t1470> <t1471>To put them at ease, Aureliano promised not to say a word.</t1471> <t1472>But the night they came to assassinate the Moscote family, they found him standing guard at the entrance.</t1472> <t1473>He showed such a decisive attitude that the assassination plan had to be postponed indefinitely. <t1476>At lunchtime, he talked with Arcadio, who was now a robust young man, and noticed that the young man increasingly showed eager enthusiasm for the impending civil war.</t1476> <t1477>Arcadio had kindled a fervent admiration for the idea of freedom in the school, where he had gathered not only older students but also young children who could barely speak.</t1477> <t1478>The young man spoke about shooting Father Nicanor, turning the church into a school, and recognizing sexual freedom.</t1478> <t1479>Aureliano was worried about his nephew's violent ideas.</t1479> <t1480>He advised him to be cautious and discreet.</t1480> <t1481>Ignoring his uncle's sensible arguments and practical thoughts, Arcadio openly criticized his uncle's cowardice.</t1481> <t1482>Aureliano waited and waited.</t1482> <t1483>Finally, at the beginning of December, Úrsula suddenly rushed into the goldsmith's workshop in a panic:</t1483>
<t1484>- Civil war has broken out!</t1484>
<t1485>Indeed, the civil war had been raging for three months.</t1485> <t1486>The curfew law was enforced nationwide.</t1486> <t1487>Don Apolinar Moscote was the only one who knew in time that the fighting had broken out, but he didn't reveal it even to his wife, while a platoon of soldiers had rushed in to occupy the village and set up a garrison.</t1487> <t1488>The soldiers quietly entered <t1496>All matters in the village were decided by an army captain.</t1496> <t1497>Every morning, this captain personally collected extraneous taxes to fund the maintenance of public security.</t1497> <t1498>Four soldiers under his command looted the home of a woman bitten by a rabid dog and beat her to death with rifle butts right in the middle of the street.</t1498> <t1499>Two weeks after the <t1502>- Where do we get weapons from?</t1502>
<t1503>- Take it from them, - Aureliano replied.</t1503>
<t1504>At midnight on the third day, during a fierce battle, twenty-one men under thirty years old, under the command of Aureliano, all armed with kitchen knives and pointed iron bars, suddenly ambushed the guards, seized their weapons, and shot dead the captain and four soldiers who had killed the innocent woman right in the yard.</t1504>
<t1505>That night, as the order to disarm the execution squad echoed, Accadio was appointed as the commander of military and civil affairs of the village.</t1505> <t1506>The militia members who had wives barely had time to bid farewell to their spouses before leaving, leaving the women to fend for themselves.</t1506>
<t1507>They left at dawn, cheered on by the entire village, having escaped the horrors, to join the armed forces of General Victorio Medina, a revolutionary.</t1507> <t1508>According to the latest news, he is operating in the Manaure area. <t1509>"Please stay calm," he said to his father-in-law, "the new government, with their word of honor, will ensure the safety of you and the entire family."</t1509> <t1510>It was very difficult for Don Apolinar Moscote to recognize the revolutionary, wearing high boots and a gun at his hip, as the same person who used to play dominoes with him until nine in the evening.</t1510>
<t1511>- Is this a joke, Aureliano? - he exclaimed.</t1511>
<t1512>- I'm not joking, - Aureliano said, - this is a real war.</t1512> <t1513>Please don't call me just Aureliano anymore, because I am now Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1513>
<t1514>Chapter 6</t1514>
<t1515>Colonel Aureliano Buendía launched thirty-two armed uprisings and failed completely.</t1515> <t1516>Having relations with seventeen girls in different villages, he fathered seventeen sons, all of whom were killed one by one in a single night before the eldest turned thirty-five. He survived fourteen assassination attempts, seventy-three ambushes, and a firing squad.</t1516> <t1517>He survived poisoning by strychnine powder mixed in his coffee in a dose sufficient to kill a horse.</t1517> <t1518>He refused the Order of Merit awarded by the President of the Republic.</t1518> <t1519>He became the Commander-in-Chief of the revolutionary armed forces, wielding authority from one country to another, and made the government wary, but he never allowed anyone to photograph him.</t1519> <t1520>He declined the pension due to him after the war and lived to old age thanks to the goldfish he produced in his jewelry workshop in the village of Macondo. Although he always led his army in battle, the only wound on his body was self-inflicted after signing the Neclandia truce, ending twenty years of civil war.</t1520> <t1521>He shot himself in the chest with a revolver, and the bullet exited through his back without harming any vital organs.</t1521> <t1522>The only thing that remained from twenty years of civil war was a street in Macondo named after him.</t1522> <t1523>However, according to his declaration a few years before dying of old age, even that was not what he had hoped for on the morning he and twenty-one strong young men set out to join the armed forces of General Victoriano Medina.</t1523>
<t1524>- We are leaving Macondo to you, - that is all he said to Arcadio before setting off.</t1524> <t1525>- we are pleased to leave it to you.</t1525> <t1526>So try to take care of it so that when we return, we find it better.</t1526>
<t1527>Accadio responded to that advice in his own way.</t1527> <t1528>He tailored a military uniform with marshal's epaulets and fringes, modeled after the illustrations in Menkyadet's book, and strapped to his waist the gold-inlaid sword of the executed captain.</t1528> <t1529>He placed two cannons right at the village gate, dressed his former students—who had been driven mad by his fiery proclamations—in uniforms, and had them swagger around the village armed, giving strangers the impression that this was an impregnable military position.</t1529> <t1530>It was a double-edged game because it prevented the government from daring to attack the square for ten months, but it also meant that when the government did order an attack, the military used such overwhelming force that they crushed the resistance within half an hour.</t1530>
<t1531>From the very first day of exercising his power, Accadio revealed himself to be an arrogant person.</t1531> <t1532>Every day, he would read orders until four in the afternoon to command and arrange everything according to his will.</t1532> <t1533>He enforced military conscription on those aged eighteen and above, declared the requisition of any animals still wandering the streets after six in the evening, and required elderly men to wear a red armband.</t1533>
<t1534>Imprisoning Father Nicanor in the monastery with the threat of shooting him and forbidding him from performing mass and ringing the bells unless it was to celebrate the victory of the Liberal faction.</t1534> <t1535>To ensure that no one doubted the seriousness of his policies, he ordered a firing squad to practice shooting at a scarecrow in the square right in front of the public.</t1535> <t1536>At first, no one took it seriously.</t1536> <t1537>It was just the students playing at being adults.</t1537> <t1538>But one night, when Arcadio entered Catarino's restaurant, the trumpet player greeted him with a mocking fanfare that made the crowd burst into laughter, so he ordered the man to be shot for disrespecting authority.</t1538> <t1539>For those who dared to oppose him, he ordered them to be handcuffed and locked in a jail that was once a classroom, feeding them only bland bread and water. "You are a murderer," Ursula scolded him whenever she learned of a new act of cruelty by her grandson.</t1539> <t1540>"If Aureliano knew, he would kill you, and I would be the first in this village to feel satisfied." But her scoldings were in vain.</t1540> <t1541>Arcadio continued to tighten the screws of his oppressive machine until he became the bloodiest authority Macondo had ever seen. "Now we suffer from the struggle," Don Apolinar Moscote said on several occasions: "This is the Paradise of the Liberals."</t1541> <t1542>Arcadio knew.</t1542> <t1543>Leading a squad of soldiers, he broke into houses, smashed furniture, beat the daughters, and dragged Don Apolinar Moscote along.</t1543> <t1544>Just as Arcadio was about to personally order the firing squad to shoot Don Apolinar Moscote, Ursula, after angrily shouting and waving a tarred leather whip to part the crowd, made a commotion in the barracks yard: "You scoundrel, have some respect!" Before Arcadio could react, she lashed him with the whip, "You murderer.</t1544> <t1545>You are too insolent," she screamed.</t1545> <t1546>"You might as well kill me too, you scoundrel.</t1546> <t1547>That way, I won't have to cry in shame for having raised a traitor."</t1547> <t1548>She continued to whip him mercilessly, chasing him to the end of the yard, making him squirm like a snail.</t1548> <t1549>Don Apolinar Moscote stood petrified, tied to the post where the scarecrows had previously been blown to bits by the firing squad's bullets.</t1549>
<t1550>The boys of the execution squad scattered because they were afraid Ursula would beat them to death.</t1550> <t1551>But she didn't even look at them.</t1551> <t1552>Leaving Arcadio in his disheveled, ragged military uniform, groaning in pain and anger, she went to untie Don Apolinar Moscote and led him home.</t1552> <t1553>Before leaving the barracks, she also released the political prisoners.</t1553>
<t1554>Since that time, she has been the ruler of Macondo village.</t1554> <t1555>She restored the Sunday mass, abolished the wearing of red armbands, and disbanded the violent patrols.</t1555> <t1556>But during her moments of rest, she still lamented her unfortunate fate.</t1556> <t1557>She felt so lonely that she had to seek out her forgotten husband under the chestnut tree. "My dear, look at our plight, isn't it tragic," she said to him as the July downpours threatened to topple the palm-leaf hut. "Look at the deserted house, our children scattered across the world, and once again it's just the two of us together like in the beginning."</t1557>
<t1558>José Arcadio Buendía was perpetually lost in a deep unconscious state, unresponsive as stone to her laments.</t1558> <t1559>In the early days of his madness, he would use incomprehensible Latin phrases to communicate his daily essential needs to everyone.</t1559> <t1560>During brief moments of lucidity, when Amaranta brought him food, he would inform her of his greatest discomforts, and he obediently took mustard seed medicine and allowed her to perform bloodletting.</t1560> <t1561>But during the period when Úrsula lamented beside him, he had completely lost the ability to interact with reality.</t1561> <t1562>He sat in the chair while she bathed him, one part at a time.</t1562> <t1563>As she bathed him, she told him stories about the household.</t1563> <t1564>"Aureliano has been at war for four months now, and we still haven't received any news about him," she said while scrubbing him with soap. "José Arcadio has returned; he is a real man, taller than even you, and covered in tattoos."</t1564> <t1565>"He came back only to bring shame to our family."</t1565> <t1566>However, she noticed that her husband seemed saddened by the bad news.</t1566> <t1567>So she changed her tone and began to lie to him, "Don't believe what I just said," she said, while sprinkling ashes over his excrement to scoop it up. "God wants José Arcadio and Rebeca to marry, and now they are living very happily."</t1567> <t1568>Then she became so sincere in her lies that she herself felt comforted by her own deceptions. "Arcadio has become a respectable young man," she said, "very brave and very handsome in his uniform with a sword at his side."</t1568> <t1569>All these words were spoken as if to a dead person, for José Arcadio Buendía was serene, very serene, beyond all worries and concerns.</t1569> <t1570>But she did not stop.</t1570> <t1571>She found him so gentle, so much like everyone else, that she untied him.</t1571> <t1572>He remained in the chair, as if the restraints were no longer necessary, because it seemed that a force stronger than any visible bonds had tied him to the chestnut tree.</t1572> <t1573>Around August, when the winter began to drag on, Úrsula was able to give him some news:</t1573>
<t1574>- Hey, luck is still with us, - she told him, - Amaranta and that Italian guy who fixed the automatic piano are going to get married.</t1574>
<t1575>Indeed, Amaranta and Pietro Crespi had a deep friendship.</t1575><t1576>Both were lovingly cared for by Úrsula.</t1576><t1577>This time she felt it was unnecessary to sit and watch over the young couple as they conversed.</t1577><t1578>It was a love in the twilight.</t1578><t1579>The Italian man, with an ivory white gardenia flower standing out on his lapel, would come to the house in the afternoon and translate Petrarch's sonnets into Spanish for Amaranta.</t1579><t1580>The two of them would sit in the hallway, thick with the scent of roses and begonias.</t1580><t1581>He read poetry, she wove lace, unaware of the shocking events and bad news from the front, until the mosquitoes bit them so fiercely that they had to run into the living room.</t1581><t1582>Amaranta's gentle affection, with her subtly tender words, was like an invisible spider web that gradually enveloped her beloved, so much so that by eight o'clock in the evening, he had to use his pale, ringed fingers to untangle himself and go home.</t1582><t1583>The two of them had made a wonderful album with postcards sent from Italy to Pietro Crespi.</t1583><t1584>These were photographs of lovers in secluded gardens, decorated with drawings of hearts pierced by arrows and golden ribbons entwining pairs of doves. "I know this park in Florence," he said, as he flipped through the postcards.</t1584><t1585>"As soon as one extends a hand, the birds immediately swoop down to eat."</t1585><t1586>Sometimes, in front of a nursery, nostalgia transformed the smell of mud and rotten fish in the canals of Venice into a gentle floral fragrance.</t1586><t1587>Amaranta sighed, laughed, and dreamed of a second homeland filled with magnificent young men and women speaking the language of children, in ancient cities whose former glory now only lingered in the form of cats prowling among the ruins.</t1587><t1588>After crossing the vast ocean in his quest, after mistaking it for the passionate moments with Rebeca, Pietro Crespi had now found love.</t1588><t1589>Joy brought with it wealth, and now his shop occupied nearly an entire block.</t1589><t1590>It was a gigantic, magical display case that restored Florentine alarm clocks, which, each time they struck the hour, would ring out a chorus of bells, sold Chinese powder boxes that delighted the fingers when opened, and offered every imaginable musical instrument and wind-up toy one could dream of.</t1590>
<t1591>Bruno Crespi, his youngest son, sat watching over the store because he was not yet capable of handling the music class.</t1591> <t1592>Thanks to Pietro Crespi, the Turkish street quickly transformed the splendid display of precious goods into a serene and melodious place to forget the brutal actions of Arcadio and the perpetual gloom of war.</t1592> <t1593>When Úrsula decided to
