"I can't wait any longer," he told her.</t1605> <t1606>"Let's get married next month."</t1606> <t1607>Amaranta did not tremble when her hand touched his cold hand.</t1607> <t1608>She slowly withdrew her hand from his.</t1608> <t1609>Like an evasive animal, she resumed her work.</t1609>
<t1610>- Stop being so naive, Crespi, - she laughed.</t1610> <t1611>- Even if I die, I won't marry you.</t1611>
<t1612>Piêtrô Crêspi could not control himself.</t1612> <t1613>He cried uncontrollably, no longer feeling any shame, nearly crushing his fingers out of frustration.</t1613> <t1614>But he still could not sway her. "Don't waste any more time hesitating," that was all Amaranta could say.</t1614> <t1615>"If you truly love me deeply, then please do not come to this house anymore."</t1615> <t1616>Ursula felt as if she were going mad with shame.</t1616> <t1617>Piêtrô Crêspi used all his skills to plead, lowering himself to unimaginable levels.</t1617> <t1618>He cried like a child on Ursula's chest all night, who might have had to sell her soul to comfort him.</t1618>
<t1619>On rainy nights, people would find him wandering around the house with a silk umbrella, hoping to catch a glimpse of the light in Amaranta's room.</t1619> <t1620>He had never dressed as elegantly as he did then.</t1620> <t1621>His magnificent hair, like that of an emperor after a storm of emotions, had taken on a strange appearance.</t1621> <t1622>He intercepted Amaranta's friends, the girls who usually came to embroider on the porch, to ask them to persuade Amaranta.</t1622> <t1623>He no longer had the heart to take care of the shop.</t1623> <t1624>All day long, he stayed at home writing tedious letters to send to Amaranta along with dried flowers and butterflies.</t1624> <t1625>But she returned them unopened.</t1625> <t1626>Shutting himself in his room, he sat for days on end playing the lute.</t1626> <t1627>One night, he sang.</t1627> <t1628>Macondo awoke in a special kind of astonishment, feeling chosen by the gods, as the sound of the lute resonated mournfully, not from this world, accompanied by a voice filled with such love that it seemed impossible to exist on this earth.</t1628> <t1629>It was then that Pietro Crespi saw light coming from the windows of every house in the village except Amaranta's room.</t1629> <t1630>On November second, All Souls' Day, his younger brother opened the shop and found all the lights on, all the music boxes open, all the clocks stopped at an eternal hour, and amidst this chaotic scene, he saw Pietro Crespi sitting at the writing table in the back room with his fingers cut and soaked in a basin of cochineal dye.</t1630>
<t1631>Ursula decided to keep her brother's body in her house for the funeral rites.</t1631><t1632>Father Nicanor refused to perform the baptism and bury him in the holy ground.</t1632><t1633>Ursula confronted the priest. "Neither you nor I can understand that this man is a saint," she said.</t1633><t1634>"Against your wishes, I will bury him right next to Melquiades' grave."</t1634><t1635>She did exactly what she said.</t1635><t1636>With the support of the entire village, she organized a solemn funeral for him.</t1636><t1637>Amaranta did not step out of her room.</t1637><t1638>Lying on her bed, she clearly heard Ursula's crying, the footsteps and the sobbing murmurs of the crowd, the eerie wails of the hired mourners echoing into the house, and then the quiet, solemn scene laden with the scent of trampled flowers.</t1638><t1639>Ursula abandoned her.</t1639><t1640>That very afternoon, Amaranta went into the kitchen, thrust her hand into the glowing embers, and left it there until she no longer felt pain but only the acrid smell of her own flesh burning, yet her mother did not even lift her eyes to pity her daughter.</t1640><t1641>It was an effective remedy for the torment of remorse.</t1641><t1642>For several days, with her burnt hand soaking in a bowl of egg whites, she walked around the house, and as the egg whites healed the burns, it seemed they also healed the burns in her heart.</t1642><t1643>The only external trace left of the tragedy was the black bandage on her burnt hand, which she had to wear until her death.</t1643>
<t1644>Through the official order to mourn Pietro Crespi, Arcadio particularly showed himself to be a noble person.</t1644> <t1645>Úrsula explained this phenomenon as the return of a lost sheep.</t1645> <t1646>But she was greatly mistaken.</t1646> <t1647>She had lost Arcadio not just since he wore the military uniform but long before that.</t1647> <t1648>She thought she was raising him like a son, like she raised Rebeca, neither too indulgently nor too harshly.</t1648> <t1649>However, Arcadio was a lonely and frightened child during the time of the insomnia plague, in the circumstances where Úrsula was busy getting rich, José Arcadio Buendía was insane, Aureliano was taciturn, and Amaranta and Rebeca were hostile to each other.</t1649> <t1650>Aureliano taught him to read and write, but his mind was elsewhere, as if he was turning him into a stranger.</t1650> <t1651>He gave him clothes that were about to tear for Visitación to mend to fit.</t1651> <t1652>Arcadio suffered with shoes that were too big, with mended pants, and with his womanly hips.</t1652> <t1653>He never confided in anyone except Visitación and Cataure in their language.</t1653> <t1654>In fact, Melquíades was the only one who cared about him.</t1654> <t1655>He recited incomprehensible elegies to him and explained the art of photography to him.</t1655> <t1656>No one knew how he cried silently over Melquíades' death and tried to revive him by studying his writings without success.</t1656> <t1657>The school, where he worked diligently and was respected by everyone, and later the authority given to him with decisive orders and the glorious military uniform, those things freed him from his previous bitterness.</t1657> <t1658>One night, right in Catarino's store, someone dared to say to him: "You are not worthy of your name!".</t1658> <t1659>Contrary to what everyone expected, Arcadio did not order that person to be shot:</t1659>
<t1660>- Very pleased, - he said.</t1660> <t1661>- Fortunately, I'm not a Buendía.</t1661>
<t1662>Those who knew his origins through this argument all thought that he had understood.</t1662> <t1663>In reality, he still had no idea about his origins.</t1663> <t1664>Pila Tecnêra, his mother, who had once aroused his lust in the darkroom, was an irresistible craving for him, just as she had been initially for José Arcadio and later for Aureliano. Although she had lost her former charm and infectious laughter, he still sought her out and found her in the acrid smoke emanating from her body.</t1664> <t1665>But before the outbreak of hostilities, one afternoon when she came to the school later than usual to find her youngest son, Arcadio was waiting for her in the lounge, which later became a prison cell.</t1665> <t1666>While the child was playing outside in the yard, he lay in the hammock waiting for her, trembling with renewed desire and knowing that she would come.</t1666> <t1667>She did come.</t1667> <t1668>Arcadio grabbed her wrist, intending to pull her into the hammock with him.</t1668>
<t1669>"I give up! I give up!", Pila Tecnêra said, trembling with fear.</t1669> <t1670>"You don't know that I also want to please you very much.</t1670> <t1671>But with God as my witness: I cannot satisfy you."</t1671>
<t1672>With inherited strength, Accadio wrapped his arms around her waist and felt the world tilt when he touched her skin.</t1672> <t1673>"Stop it, don't pretend to be all high and mighty...", he said.</t1673> <t1674>"In the end, everyone knows I'm a prostitute."</t1674>
<t1675>Pila Tecnera could not overcome the shame that the tragic fate had bestowed upon her.</t1675>
<t1676>- The children will disappear, - she said.</t1676> <t1677>- It's best if you leave the door open tonight.</t1677>
<t1678>That night, lying on the hammock, his body burning as if on fire, Arcadio waited for her.</t1678><t1679>He did not sleep, waiting and listening to the sounds of the late night and the cries of the night heron.</t1679><t1680>The longer he waited, the more hopeless he felt, thinking he had been "fooled."</t1680><t1681>Just as his desire began to cool and his anger flared up, the door suddenly opened.</t1681><t1682>Months later, standing before the firing squad, Arcadio recalled the staggering footsteps in the classroom, the collisions with the benches, and finally, a shadowy figure emerging in the dim room and the pounding heart that was not his own.</t1682><t1683>He reached out his hand and encountered another hand wearing two rings on the same finger of a person about to drown in darkness.</t1683><t1684>He felt his blood pulsing through his veins, his misfortune beating erratically, and his hand, with a lifeline cut short by death on the thumb mound, was damp with sweat.</t1684><t1685>Thus, he immediately understood that this woman was not the one he had been waiting for, because she did not emit the expected burnt smell but rather the scent of brigantine, because she had wrinkled breasts, a firm and round navel like a grape seed, and a body trembling with the fear of someone inexperienced in life.</t1685><t1686>She was a virgin and had an almost unbelievable name: Santa Sofía de la Piedad.</t1686><t1687>Pilar Ternera paid her fifty pesos, half of the money she had saved all her life, to do what she was doing.</t1687><t1688>Arcadio had seen her many times while tending his parents' grocery store, and he had never paid attention to her because she was not yet in her prime.</t1688><t1689>But from that day on, he clung to her like a cat.</t1689><t1690>She came to the school during lunch breaks with the complicity of her parents, who had received the rest of Pilar Ternera's savings.</t1690><t1691>Later, when the government troops expelled them from the school, the two made love among the butter barrels and corn sacks in the warehouse.</t1691><t1692>During the time Arcadio was appointed Military and Civil Governor of Macondo, they had a daughter together.</t1692>
<t1693>The only close relatives who knew about it were José Arcadio and Rebeca.</t1693> <t1694>They were the ones with whom Arcadio at that time maintained intimate relations not only based on kinship but also on complicity.</t1694> <t1695>The marital yoke completely subdued José Arcadio.</t1695> <t1696>Rebeca's strong character, her gluttonous appetite, and her insatiable greed drained the robust strength of her husband, forcing him, who was lazy and a playboy, to become a hardworking giant.</t1696>
<t1697>He has a clean and tidy house.</t1697> <t1698>Every morning, Rebecca opens the doors wide, and the wind from the cemetery rushes through the windows and then sweeps out through the side door, leaving behind the sulfurous smell of the dead, causing the walls to turn pale and the tables, cabinets, and beds to darken.</t1698> <t1699>The hunger for land, the clattering sound of the bones of her parents, and her impatience with Pietro Crespi's overly calm demeanor had faded into the past.</t1699> <t1700>All day, she sat embroidering by the window, indifferent to the echoes of war.</t1700> <t1701>Only when the earthenware pots began to rattle in the cupboard did she get up to reheat the food.</t1701> <t1702>She did this before the appearance of the mud-splattered, sniffing dogs and the burly man in spurred boots, carrying a double-barreled musket: "This man sometimes carried a deer on his shoulders, but almost always brought back a string of rabbits or wild ducks… One afternoon, right when he took power, Arcadio suddenly came to visit them.</t1702> <t1703>When they left the house, they did not see him, but he had shown such warmth and friendliness that the couple invited him to stay and taste the freshly cooked game meat.</t1703>
<t1704>It was only when drinking coffee that Arcadio clearly stated the purpose of his visit: he had received a complaint against José Arcadio. The complaint alleged that José Arcadio plowed his own field and encroached on neighboring lands, broke down fences, herded his cattle into others' pens, and used force to seize the most fertile farms around.</t1704> <t1705>For those farmers who were not dispossessed because their lands were poor, he forced them to pay taxes.</t1705> <t1706>Every Saturday, he used hunting dogs and a double-barreled musket to collect taxes.</t1706> <t1707>He did not deny these accusations.</t1707> <t1708>He asserted his rights over the lands he had seized, which had been distributed by José Arcadio Buendía during the village's founding, and he believed he could prove that his father had been insane since that time because he had given away wealth that rightfully belonged to his family.</t1708> <t1709>It was an unnecessary argument because Arcadio had not come to adjudicate right or wrong but merely to negotiate.</t1709>
<t1710>As for himself, he established a law office to oversee private property, which would provide José Arcadio with the documents to legalize the lands he had seized, and in return, he had to allow the local government the right to collect taxes.</t1710> <t1711>The two agreed with each other.</t1711> <t1712>In later years, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía examined the land deeds, he found that all the land from José Arcadio's yard to the horizon, including the village cemetery, belonged to his brother; and that during the eleven months of his rule, Arcadio had collected not only taxes but also money from people who wanted to bury their loved ones on José Arcadio's estate.</t1712>
<t1713>A few months later, Úrsula finally learned about the matter that had become public knowledge, because the townspeople had hidden it from her to spare her further distress.</t1713> <t1714>She began to suspect him. "Arcadio is building a house," she proudly asserted to her husband while trying to shove a spoonful of pumpkin soup into his mouth.</t1714> <t1715>However, she unconsciously sighed: "I don't know why, but I feel uneasy about all these things."</t1715> <t1716>And later, when she found out that not only had Arcadio finished building the house but had also bought furniture made in Vienna, she became even more convinced that he was embezzling public funds, something she had previously suspected.</t1716>
<t1717>"You are the disgrace of our family," she scolded him like that at the end of the Sunday mass, when she saw him playing cards in the new house.</t1717> <t1718>Arcadio ignored her words.</t1718> <t1719>It was only then that Úrsula learned he had a six-month-old daughter and that Santa Sofía de la Piedad, the woman he was living with without being married, was pregnant again.</t1719>
<t1720>Ursula intended to write a letter to Colonel Aureliano Buendía, no matter where he was, to inform him of everything happening at home and in the neighborhood.</t1720> <t1721>But the tumultuous events at that time not only hindered all her intentions but also made her regret having gathered them.</t1721> <t1722>War, which until then had been just a word to describe an empty and stagnant situation, had materialized into a tragic reality.</t1722> <t1723>At the end of February, an old woman with white hair riding a broomstick horse arrived in the village of Macondo.</t1723> <t1724>She looked so gentle that the patrols let her into the village without questioning her, just as they had not questioned the peddlers who often passed through the villages around the swamp.</t1724> <t1725>She went straight to the barracks, where Arcadio received her right in what used to be the classroom and was now the soldiers' resting place, where hammocks were rolled up and hung on hooks, backpacks were piled up in corners, and rifles, carbines, and hunting guns were scattered all over the floor.</t1725> <t1726>The old woman saluted in a military manner before introducing herself:</t1726>
<t1727>- I am Colonel Gregory Stevenson.</t1727>
<t1728>He brings bad news.</t1728> <t1729>The last strongholds of the Liberal forces, according to him, are being destroyed.</t1729> <t1730>Colonel Aureliano Buendía, who is being pursued by the Royalist army on the outskirts of the city of Riohacha, has entrusted him with the mission to speak with Arcadio, advising him to surrender the square to the government forces under the condition that they honorably promise to ensure the safety and property of the Liberals.</t1730>
<t1731>Accađiô looked with suspicious eyes at the strange messenger, who could be mistaken for a frightened old woman fleeing.</t1731>
<t1732>- So may I ask if you have any documents with you? - he asked.</t1732>
<t1733>- Unfortunately, - the messenger replied, - I didn't bring it with me.</t1733> <t1734>It is understandable that under the current conditions, it is really difficult to carry anything on one's person that might arouse suspicion.</t1734>
<t1735>While speaking, he took a goldfish out of his chest pocket and placed it on the table.</t1735> <t1736>"I believe this will be more than sufficient," he said.</t1736> <t1737>Accadio confirmed that it was one of the goldfish made by Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1737> <t1738>But someone had either bought it before the war broke out or had stolen it, and thus it no longer held the value of a transit pass.</t1738> <t1739>At this point, the messenger was forced to violate military secrecy to reveal his true identity.</t1739> <t1740>He recounted that his mission was to go to Curaçao to recruit all the exiles from the entire Caribbean region, purchase enough weapons and military supplies to plan a landing by the end of the year.</t1740> <t1741>Although he trusted the plan, Colonel Aureliano Buendía did not agree that it was the right time to carry out such futile sacrificial activities.</t1741> <t1742>But Accadio was ruthless.</t1742> <t1743>He imprisoned the messenger to verify his background and decided to defend the square to the last breath.</t1743>
<t1744>No need to wait long.</t1744><t1745>News of the Liberal faction's failure became more and more certain.</t1745><t1746>In the early hours of a late March night, amidst early downpours, the heavy, quiet atmosphere of the previous weeks suddenly dissipated with the eerie sound of a cornet and the violent explosion of a cannon that brought down the church bell tower.</t1746><t1747>In reality, Arcadio's resolve to resist was a mad idea.</t1747><t1748>He had no more than fifty poorly armed men with no more than twenty bullets per gun.</t1748><t1749>But among the resistance fighters, his former students, who were once eager with his grand declarations, were determined to fight to the last moment for a lost cause.</t1749><t1750>In the midst of the chaotic sounds of running boots, conflicting orders, earth-shaking cannon blasts, random gunfire, and meaningless cornet calls, the imprisoned Colonel Stevenson requested to speak with Arcadio.</t1750><t1751>"Spare me the shame of dying in prison dressed in women's clothes," he said to him. "If I must die, I will die fighting."</t1751><t1752>Arcadio ordered a gun with twenty bullets to be given to him and allowed him, along with five others, to defend the barracks, while he and his command went to the front line of the resistance.</t1752><t1753>The barricades were broken, and the village defenders had to fight an uneven gun battle in the streets.</t1753><t1754>First, they fought with rifles until they ran out of bullets, then with pistols against rifles, and finally in hand-to-hand combat.</t1754><t1755>Facing imminent defeat, some women, wielding sticks and butcher knives, poured into the streets.</t1755><t1756>In that chaotic scene, Arcadio encountered Amaranta in her nightgown, holding two old pistols of José Arcadio Buendía, frantically searching for him.</t1756><t1757>Arcadio handed his gun to an officer who had lost his weapon in the battle, then slipped into a nearby alley with Amaranta to take her home.</t1757><t1758>Ursula stood at the doorway, waiting, despite the gunfire and a stray bullet that punched a hole in the front wall of the neighboring house.</t1758><t1759>The rain had stopped, but the main road was muddy like soap foam, and people had to feel their way in the darkness. Arcadio left Amaranta with Ursula and intended to face two government soldiers coming down the street to search.</t1759><t1760>The pistols, having been in the drawer for too long without oil, did not fire.</t1760><t1761>Shielding Arcadio with her body, Ursula tried to pull him back to her house.</t1761>
<t1762>- Come back immediately! For God's sake, come back home immediately - she shouted at him. - Stop this madness.</t1762>
<t1763>The soldiers fired bullets at them.</t1763>
<t1764>- Old woman, let go of that person, - a soldier shouted, - or we will not be responsible for your death.</t1764>
<t1765>Accadio pushed Ursula towards the house, while he raised his hands in surrender.</t1765>
<t1766>Shortly thereafter, the gunfire ceased abruptly and the church bells began to toll.</t1766> <t1767>The resistance was crushed in less than half an hour.</t1767>
<t1768>Not a single person from Akkad survived the attack, but before dying, they were all paraded in front of three hundred government soldiers.</t1768> <t1769>The last fortress to be destroyed was the military camp.</t1769>
<t1770>Before it was attacked, Colonel Gregory Stevenson, who was imprisoned, released all the prisoners and ordered his subordinates to go out and fight on the streets.</t1770> <t1771>The change in the combat position was as flexible as his, and the precise trajectory of the twenty bullets he fired from different windows gave the impression that the barracks were well-defended, forcing the attackers to use cannons to bring it down.</t1771> <t1772>The captain commanding the battle was astonished by the sight of the collapsed walls, with only one man, wearing nothing but shorts, dead, and a gun with no bullets being held by a severed arm.</t1772> <t1773>That man had thick, curly hair resting on his shoulders and wore a necklace with a goldfish pendant around his neck.</t1773> <t1774>When the captain used his boot to turn the man's face up for a clearer look, he was startled and exclaimed, "Holy crap," he said.</t1774> <t1775>Several other officers also rushed over.</t1775>
<t1776>- Look, where does this man come from? - the captain said to them.</t1776> <t1777>- That is Gregorio Stevenson.</t1777>
<t1778>At dawn, Accadio was shot in front of the cemetery wall after the military council held an emergency meeting.</t1778> <t1779>In the last two hours of his life, he still couldn't understand why the fear of death that had tormented him since childhood had vanished.</t1779> <t1780>With a cold, calm demeanor, not at all concerned with proving his newly found courage, he listened to the lengthy accusations.</t1780>
<t1781>He thought of Úrsula, who at this hour was probably drinking coffee with José Arcadio Buendía under the chestnut tree.</t1781> <t1782>He thought of the eight-month-old daughter who still had no name and the child that would be born in the coming August. He thought of Santa Sofía de la Piedad, who had marinated a deer last night for Saturday's lunch, remembered her long hair falling over her shoulders, and her long eyelashes that looked like they were fake.</t1782> <t1783>He thought of his people without the slightest regret.</t1783> <t1784>In the fairness that life demanded, he began to understand how much he actually loved those he had once hated.</t1784> <t1785>The president of the military council began his final speech just as Arcadio suddenly remembered that two hours had already passed.</t1785> <t1786>"Although the positions assigned to him were insignificant," the president said, "his aggressive, irresponsible, and cruel attitude led his subordinates to useless deaths, which is enough to sentence him to death."... At the cracked school where he first felt the certainty of power, just a few meters from the classroom where he first experienced bewildering love, Arcadio encountered the ridiculous form of death.</t1786> <t1787>In reality, what mattered to him now was not death but life, and thus, the feeling he experienced when sentenced to death was not fear but longing.</t1787> <t1788>He remained silent until asked what his final wish was.</t1788>
<t1789>- Tell my wife, - he replied in a loud voice, - to name our daughter Ucsula, - he thought and confirmed: yes, Ucsula, the name of her grandmother.</t1789> <t1790>And tell my wife that if the child to be born is a boy, name him José Arcadio, but not after his uncle, rather after his grandfather.</t1790>
<t1791>Before they took him to the wall at the cemetery, Father Nicanor intended to baptize him. "I have no regrets," Arcadio said, and followed the execution squad's orders after drinking a cup of black coffee.</t1791> <t1792>The commander of the execution squad, a man who specialized in carrying out death sentences, had a name very fitting for his profession: Captain Roque Carnicero. On the way to the cemetery, under a light drizzle, Arcadio noticed that a dawn Wednesday was beginning to glow on the horizon.</t1792> <t1793>Nostalgia dissolved in the mist and left behind a terrible curiosity.</t1793>
<t1794>Only when he was ordered to lean against the wall did Arcadio see Rebeca with her wet hair, wearing a pink floral dress, opening the door of the house wide.</t1794> <t1795>He silently prayed that Rebeca would recognize him.</t1795> <t1796>Indeed, at that moment, Rebeca looked towards the cemetery wall and suddenly stood there, stunned and horrified.</t1796> <t1797>She barely had time to signal a farewell to Arcadio.</t1797> <t1798>Arcadio responded with a similar gesture.</t1798> <t1799>At that moment, the smoking barrels of the guns fired at Arcadio, and he clearly heard each word of the chant recited by old Melquíades and felt the staggering footsteps of Santa Sofía de la Piedad, the virgin maiden, echoing in the classroom, and felt the cold hardness of ice on his nose, which he had noticed in the nostrils of Remedios' corpse.</t1799> <t1800>"Ah, the b... - he just had time to think, - I forgot to say that if it's a girl, name her Remedios."</t1800> <t1801>At that moment, he felt the fear that had tormented him his entire life.</t1801> <t1802>The captain ordered the guns to fire.</t1802> <t1803>Almost at the same time, Arcadio managed to puff out his chest and lift his head, not understanding where the stream of hot, thick liquid that was burning his muscles was coming from:</t1803>
<t1804>- You old goat, - he shouted - Long live the Liberal Party!</t1804>
<t1805>Chapter 7</t1805>
<t1806>The war ended in May.</t1806> <t1807>Two weeks before the government issued an official announcement, in a boastful statement, they promised to mercilessly punish the leaders of the uprising.</t1807> <t1808>It was at that moment that Colonel Aureliano Buendía was captured while disguised as an Indian shaman, just as he was about to cross the western border of the country.</t1808> <t1809>Of the twenty-one men who followed him in the war, fourteen died in battle, six were wounded, and only one remained with him in the final defeat.</t1809> <t1810>That person was Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t1810> <t1811>Through a special decree, the news of his capture was announced in the village of Macondo.</t1811>
<t1812>"He is still alive… - Úrsula informed her husband.</t1812> <t1813>"Let us hope that his enemies have mercy."</t1813> <t1814>After three days of crying, one afternoon while stirring a pot of milk candy over the fire, she clearly heard her son's voice right next to her ear.</t1814> <t1815>"It's Aureliano," she screamed as she ran to the chestnut tree to inform her husband: "I don't know what kind of omen this is, but he is alive and we will see him soon."</t1815> <t1816>She believed that omen to be true.</t1816> <t1817>She ordered the floor to be cleaned and the furniture to be rearranged.</t1817> <t1818>A week later, a source without origin, not based on any decree, heartbreakingly confirmed that omen.</t1818> <t1819>Colonel Aureliano Buendía was sentenced to death, and the sentence would be carried out in the village of Macondo to intimidate the populace.</t1819> <t1820>At seven twenty on a Monday morning, Amaranta was dressing Aureliano José when she heard a distant commotion and a chilling cornet blast, and a minute later Úrsula burst into the room.</t1820> <t1821>"They are bringing him back," she said.</t1821> <t1822>The soldiers had to struggle, using their rifle butts to push through the chaotic crowd.</t1822> <t1823>Úrsula and Amaranta ran to the end of the street, pushed through the crowd, and there they saw Aureliano Buendía.</t1823> <t1824>He looked like a beggar: his clothes were in tatters, his hair and beard were unkempt, he was barefoot, his hands were tied behind his back with a rope attached to the saddle of the officer's horse.</t1824> <t1825>Along with him, the soldiers were also escorting Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, who was just as ragged and miserable as he was.</t1825> <t1826>The two men were not sad at all.</t1826> <t1827>They seemed to be excited in front of the crowd that was shouting and cursing the most depraved soldiers.</t1827>
<t1828>- Oh my child! - in the noisy scene filled with screams, Ursula called out to her child and slapped the soldier who was trying to stop her.</t1828>
<t1829>The officer's horse jumped up.</t1829> <t1830>At this moment, Colonel Aureliano Buendía stopped, trembling, and avoided his mother's outstretched arms that were about to embrace him, giving her a stern look:</t1830>
<t1831>- Mother, please go home, - he said.</t1831> <t1832>- Please ask the authorities for permission to visit me in prison.</t1832>
<t1833>He looked at Amaranta standing two steps behind Ucsula, smiled at her, and asked, "What happened to your hand?".</t1833> <t1834>Amaranta raised her bandaged hand. "Burned," she replied and pulled Ucsula back to avoid being trampled by the horse.</t1834> <t1835>The soldiers fired their guns into the air.</t1835> <t1836>The special guard unit surrounded the two prisoners and rode off, dragging them back to the military camp.</t1836>
<t1837>That afternoon, Úrsula went to the prison to visit Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1837> <t1838>She had intended to go through Don Apolinar Moscote to get permission to visit, but he had been stripped of all his power by the dictatorial soldiers.</t1838> <t1839>Father Nicanor was being laid low by a liver fever.</t1839> <t1840>The parents of Colonel Gerineldo Márquez, who had not been sentenced to death, tried to visit their son but were beaten with rifle butts by the soldiers and driven away.</t1840> <t1841>In the face of the hopelessness of finding intermediaries to help her, and moreover convincing herself that her son would be shot at dawn the next day, Úrsula wrapped up a bundle of supplies to bring to him, and went straight to the barracks by herself.</t1841>
<t1842>- I am the mother of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, - she introduced herself.</t1842>
<t1843>The guards stopped her. "Whatever you want, I'm going in," Ursula told them.</t1843> <t1844>"If you have orders to shoot, then go ahead and start all at once."</t1844> <t1845>She pushed one of the soldiers aside and stepped into the old classroom where a group of shirtless soldiers were busily cleaning their guns.</t1845> <t1846>An officer in field uniform, with rosy skin and glasses as thick as bottle bottoms, moving leisurely, ordered the guards to withdraw.</t1846>
<t1847>- I am the mother of Colonel Aureliano Buendía, - Úrsula repeated.</t1847>
<t1848>- You mean to say, - the officer, correcting her with a charming smile, - you are the mother who gave birth to Mr. Aureliano Buendía.</t1848>
<t1849>In the gentle speech of the officer, Úrsula recognized the soft intonation of the highland people, the dandies.</t1849>
<t1850>- Sir, just as you said.</t1850> <t1851>- She sincerely admits it.</t1851> <t1852>- Are you willing to let me visit it?</t1852>
<t1853>There was an order from the higher-ups not to allow anyone to visit the prisoners sentenced to death.</t1853><t1854>But this officer dared to take responsibility for letting her visit her son for fifteen minutes.</t1854><t1855>Ursula showed the officer the items she had in her package: a clean set of clothes to change into, the boots her son wore on his wedding day, and milk candies she had set aside from the day she received the omen.</t1855><t1856>She met Colonel Aureliano Buendía in the cell, lying on a single bed, with his arms spread wide because his armpits were swollen.</t1856><t1857>He had been allowed to shave.</t1857><t1858>His thick, curled mustache accentuated his high cheekbones even more.</t1858><t1859>It seemed to Ursula that her son looked paler than when he left, taller, and lonelier than ever.</t1859><t1860>He knew all the details of what had happened at home: Pietro Crespi's suicide, Arcadio's arrogance and execution, José Arcadio Buendía growing weaker under the chestnut tree.</t1860><t1861>He knew that Amaranta had decided to remain single for life to raise Aureliano José and that the child was very well-behaved, moreover intelligent, learning to read and write as soon as he learned to speak.</t1861><t1862>From the moment she stepped into the cell, Ursula felt overwhelmed by her son's maturity, his sense of command, and the aura of authority radiating from his skin.</t1862><t1863>She was surprised to see him know everything. "You already know, mother, I'm very good at guessing," he joked.</t1863><t1864>Then, with a serious demeanor, he added, "This morning, when they took me away, I had the feeling that all of this had already happened."</t1864>
<t1865>Indeed, while the people were angrily shouting around him, he concentrated his thoughts and was surprised at the village's aged appearance over the past year.</t1865> <t1866>The almond trees spread out their tattered leaves.</t1866> <t1867>The houses, once painted green, then red, and then green again, began to take on an indescribable color.</t1867>
<t1868>- What are you waiting for? - Ucsula sighed.</t1868> <t1869>- Time is passing by.</t1869>
<t1870>- That's right, - Aureliano admitted, - but not much, mother.</t1870>
<t1871>In this form, the long-awaited visit, for which both had prepared questions and anticipated answers, once again became an everyday conversation.</t1871> <t1872>When the guard announced that the permitted time for the conversation was over, Aureliano lifted the mat and took out a sweat-stained notebook.</t1872> <t1873>It contained his poems.</t1873> <t1874>Some were poems he had written when he loved Remedios and had taken with him when he left, and some were poems he had written during moments of rest after battles, filled with confusion.</t1874> <t1875>"Promise me you won't let anyone read them," he said, "burn them in the oven tonight."</t1875> <t1876>Úrsula promised him, then she prepared to bid him farewell with a kiss.</t1876>
<t1877>- Mom brought you a revolver, - she whispered.</t1877>
<t1878>Colonel Aureliano Buendía looked around and saw that the guard was not there.</t1878> <t1879>"It won't do you any good, mother," he whispered. "Alright, just leave it here, or else they'll search you when you leave."</t1879>
<t1880>Ursula took a pistol out from her chest pocket and he hid it under the mat.</t1880> <t1881>"Now there's no need for farewells," he calmly said.</t1881> <t1882>"Mother, you don't need to beg anyone and you don't need to lower yourself before anyone.</t1882> <t1883>Just consider that they have already shot me dead a long time ago."</t1883> <t1884>Ursula bit her lip to keep from crying out.</t1884>
<t1885>- Apply hot bricks to the swollen areas under your arms, - she said.</t1885>
<t1886>She turned halfway around and then left the cell.</t1886> <t1887>Colonel Aureliano Buendía stood still, looking pensive, until the door closed.</t1887> <t1888>Then he lay down on the bed, spreading his arms wide: Ever since he entered adolescence, when he began to perceive his own omens, he had thought that death must be heralded by a definitive, unmistakable sign, one that could not be altered, but with only a few hours left before he was to die, no such sign had come to him.</t1888>
<t1889>One day, a stunningly beautiful woman entered the Tucurinca barracks, asking the guards for permission to visit him.</t1889> <t1890>They allowed her to enter because they were accustomed to the fanaticism of some mothers who often sent their daughters to the bedrooms of the most famous warriors, as these men claimed, to improve the breed.</t1890> <t1891>That night, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had just finished writing a poem about a man lost in the rain when the girl entered the room.</t1891> <t1892>He turned his back to her to place the paper in a locked drawer where he kept his poems.</t1892> <t1893>And then he felt the presence of death.</t1893> <t1894>He grabbed the gun from the drawer without turning around.</t1894>
<t1895>- Please, don't shoot! - He said.</t1895>
<t1896>When he turned back, with a gun in hand ready to fire, the girl had already lowered her gun, flustered and unsure of what to do.</t1896><t1897>In this way, he had escaped four out of eleven ambushes.</t1897><t1898>On the contrary, there was one person who could never be caught, who infiltrated the revolutionary camp and stabbed Colonel Macgriffico Visban, his closest friend, to death while he was lying shivering on the bed waiting for the fever to break.</t1898><t1899>Just a few meters away, while sleeping on a hammock in the same room, he was unaware of anything.</t1899><t1900>His efforts to systematize the omens all became futile.</t1900><t1901>Suddenly, the omens all appeared at once in a brilliant flash of superior intelligence, but they were ungraspable because they were like an absolute but fleeting belief.</t1901><t1902>Sometimes they were so obvious that only after they had come true did he consider them as omens.</t1902><t1903>At other times, they appeared clearly but did not come true.</t1903><t1904>Usually, they were just sudden, crude punches of superstition.</t1904><t1905>But when he was sentenced to death and when he was asked to express his final wishes, he had no difficulty affirming the omen he recognized in his response:</t1905>
<t1906>- I request that the sentence be carried out in Macondo, - he said.</t1906>
<t1907>The judge was annoyed.</t1907>
<t1908>- Buendía, don't hope to escape death, - he said to him, - it's merely a tactic to buy time.</t1908>
<t1909>- It seems that you do not want to carry out this sentence in Macondo, - the colonel said - but that is my final wish.</t1909>
<t1910>Since then, he no longer received any omens.</t1910> <t1911>On the day Ursula visited him in prison, after much contemplation, he came to the conclusion: perhaps this time his death would not be foretold because it did not depend on fate but on the will of the executioners.</t1911> <t1912>That night, he stayed awake, writhing in pain with two festering, aching armpits.</t1912> <t1913>But just before dawn, he clearly heard footsteps in the hallway.</t1913>
<t1914>"They are coming," he said to himself, and suddenly he thought of José Arcadio Buendía, who at that moment, in the early dismal morning, was also sitting under the chestnut tree thinking of him.</t1914>
<t1915>Not feeling fear, nor longing, he only felt furious that this artificial death had not allowed him to see with his own eyes the final result of so much unfinished work.</t1915> <t1916>The door opened, and a guard stepped into the room to bring him a cup of coffee.</t1916> <t1917>The next day at this exact time, he still lived in that feeling, irritated because of the pain in his armpits, and everything happened exactly the same way.</t1917> <t1918>On the fifth day, he also shared the joy of eating milk candy with the guards, wore a clean outfit that was a bit tight for his body, and put on a pair of varnished boots.</t1918> <t1919>On the sixth day, they still hadn't shot him.</t1919>
<t1920>In reality, they did not dare to carry out the sentence.</t1920> <t1921>The people's defiance forced the military authorities to consider that executing Colonel Aureliano Buendía would have serious political repercussions not only in Macondo but throughout the entire swamp region, so they had to seek advice from the provincial authorities.</t1921> <t1922>On Saturday night, Captain Roque Carnicero and several officers went to Catarino's bar. Only one woman, almost compelled, dared to take him to her room.</t1922> <t1923>"None of the sisters dare to sleep with a man they know is going to die," the girl confessed to him.</t1923> <t1924>"No one knows how things will turn out, but the whole village says that the officer who shoots Colonel Aureliano Buendía, as well as all the soldiers in the firing squad, will sooner or later be killed one by one, even if they flee to the ends of the earth."</t1924> <t1925>Captain Roque Carnicero relayed this to several officers, who then reported it to their superiors.</t1925> <t1926>On Sunday, although no one could prove anything definitively, and although no military action could disrupt the heavy silence of those days, the entire village knew that the officers were trying every possible way to evade the duty of carrying out the sentence.</t1926> <t1927>On Monday, a telegram arrived with the official order: "The execution must be carried out within twenty-four hours of receiving this message."</t1927> <t1928>That night, the officers placed seven slips of paper with their names into a kepi, and the ill-fated Captain Roque Carnicero drew the slip of the one who had to carry out the sentence: "Bad luck cannot be avoided," he said with a bitter, silent resignation.</t1928> <t1929>"Born the son of a whore, I will die the son of a whore..."</t1929> <t1930>At five in the morning, by drawing lots, he formed the firing squad, gathered them in the camp courtyard, and woke the condemned man with a fateful phrase:</t1930>
<t1931>- Let's go, Buendía, - he urged him, - our time has come.</t1931>
<t1932>- Ah, so that's how it is, - the colonel said, - I was dreaming that I was being blown up by boils.</t1932>
<t1933>Rebeca Buendía, ever since she heard the news that Aureliano would be shot, frequently woke up at three in the morning.</t1933> <t1934>She sat silently in the dark room, watching the cemetery wall through the half-open window, while the bed she sat on shook with the rhythm of José Arcadio's snoring. For a whole week, she patiently waited as she had once waited for letters from Pietro Crespi.</t1934> <t1935>"They won't shoot him here," José Arcadio told her. "They will shoot him right in the prison at midnight so no one knows who is leading the firing squad, and then they will bury him right there."</t1935> <t1936>Nevertheless, Rebeca continued to wait.</t1936> <t1937>"They are so foolish that they will definitely shoot him here," she said.</t1937> <t1938>She believed in herself so much that she had already prepared how to open the door and how to wave farewell to him.</t1938> <t1939>"They won't take him along the main road," he kept insisting, "because they only have six cowardly soldiers while knowing full well that the townspeople are ready to do anything."</t1939> <t1940>Ignoring her husband's logic, she still sat by the window looking out at the cemetery wall.</t1940>
<t1941>- Then we will see that they are such fools, - she said.</t1941>
<t1942>At five o'clock in the morning on Tuesday, José Arcadio had finished his coffee and released the hunting dogs just as Rebeca closed the door and struggled to hold onto the bedpost to keep from falling. "They've taken him out already," she sighed.</t1942> <t1943>"Oh, how handsome he is."</t1943>
<t1944>José Arcadio stuck his head out the window, looking at the trembling young man in the dawn light, wearing clothes that had once been his own when he was young. The young man stood with his back against the wall, hands on his hips because his armpits were so swollen that he couldn't let his arms hang down.</t1944><t1945>"It's really ridiculous," Colonel Aureliano Buendía muttered.</t1945><t1946>"It's so ridiculous that it takes six sick men to kill one unarmed person."</t1946><t1947>He kept repeating it with such anger that it seemed he was boiling with rage, and Captain Roque Carnicero also felt pity because he thought the colonel was praying to God. When the firing squad aimed their guns at him, his anger materialized into a bitter mucus that settled on his tongue, lulling him to sleep and forcing him to close his eyes.</t1947><t1948>At that moment, the glorious dawn light vanished, and he saw himself as a little boy wearing short pants with a bow tied at his neck, and he saw himself in a radiant afternoon leading himself into the theater, and he saw the block of ice.</t1948><t1949>When he heard the shout, he immediately thought it was the final order for the firing squad.</t1949><t1950>He opened his eyes wide.</t1950><t1951>With a chilling curiosity, he awaited the bullets to pierce him but only saw Captain Roque Carnicero raising his hands, and saw José Arcadio crossing the street brandishing a shotgun ready to fire.</t1951>
<t1952>- Please don't shoot, - the captain said to José Arcadio - you have come by the command of the Almighty.</t1952>
<t1953>From that point, a new war began.</t1953> <t1954>Captain Roque Carnicero, along with six of his subordinates, followed Colonel Aureliano Buendía to liberate the revolutionaries, General Victorio Medina, who had been sentenced to death in Riohacha.</t1954> <t1955>They thought they could save time by quickly crossing the mountain range along the path José Arcadio Buendía had taken to establish the village of Macondo, but within less than a week, they unanimously agreed that it was an impassable route.</t1955> <t1956>Thus, they had to undertake a perilous journey along the mountain slopes with the scant ammunition of the soldiers in the execution squad.</t1956> <t1957>They rested on the outskirts of the villages.</t1957> <t1958>One of them, disguised and carrying a golden fish, entered the village in broad daylight to meet with the waiting Liberal faction members.</t1958>
<t1959>The next morning, these people went hunting and would never be seen returning.</t1959> <t1960>When they saw the city of Rioacha from a mountain bend, General Victorio Medina had already been executed.</t1960> <t1961>The men under Colonel Aureliano Buendía immediately proclaimed him Commander of the Revolutionary Armed Forces of the Caribbean coast with the rank of General.</t1961> <t1962>He accepted the position but refused the rank, setting for himself the condition that he would not accept the rank until his army had overthrown the Royalist regime.</t1962> <t1963>Exactly three months later, he had gathered more than a thousand troops but was defeated by government forces.</t1963>
<t1964>The survivors fled towards the eastern border of the country.</t1964> <t1965>Another time, it was known that they had landed at Cabo de la Vela from the Antidat archipelago, yet a government communiqué was sent through the postal service and reprinted in the form of cheerful notices spread throughout the country, announcing the death of Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t1965> <t1966>But two days later, another troubling telegram, almost simultaneous with the previous one, reported a new uprising in the southern plains. Thus began the legend of Colonel Aureliano Buendía's uncanny abilities.</t1966> <t1967>Similar and different news spread, announcing his victory in Vidanuéva, his defeat in Goacamadan, that he had been killed by the Indians in Colombia and Venezuela, that he died in some village in the swamp region, and once again he rose up in Urumita.</t1967> <t1968>The leaders of the Liberal Party, meanwhile negotiating their participation in the parliament, considered him an adventurous rogue who did not represent the party.</t1968> <t1969>The national government classified him as a bandit and offered a reward of five thousand pesos for his capture.</t1969> <t1970>After sixteen failed armed uprisings, Colonel Aureliano Buendía, with two thousand Indian soldiers armed from the Goahira region, launched an attack, and the Royalist guard, caught by surprise while sleeping, had to abandon the city of Riohacha and flee.</t1970> <t1971>There, he established his General Headquarters and declared a total war against the regime.</t1971> <t1972>The first reaction from the government that he received was a threat to execute Colonel Gerineldo Márquez within forty-eight hours if he did not withdraw all his armed forces to the eastern border.</t1972> <t1973>Colonel Roque Carnicero, now his chief of staff, solemnly handed him the telegram.</t1973> <t1974>But he revealed his joy upon reading the telegram.</t1974>
<t1975>- Very good, - he exclaimed, - Macondo now has a telegraph station.</t1975>
<t1976>His answer was decisive.</t1976> <t1977>For three months, he waited to establish his General Headquarters in Macondo.</t1977> <t1978>If by then he had not seen Colonel Gerineldo Márquez alive, he would execute all the captured officers without trial, starting with the generals, and at the same time, he would order his subordinates to proceed in the same manner, executing Royalist officers until the war ended.</t1978> <t1979>Three months later, when he triumphantly marched into Macondo, the first embrace he received on the road from the swamp to the village was from Colonel Gerineldo Márquez.</t1979>
<t1980>The house was crowded with children.</t1980> <t1981>Ursula welcomed Santa Sofia de la Piedad along with her first daughter and the twin children born five months after Arcadio's execution into her home.</t1981>
<t1982>Contrary to the final wishes of the condemned man, she named his first daughter Remedios.</t1982> <t1983>"I believe that is what Arcadio wanted to say," she said.</t1983> <t1984>"We will not call her Ursula because with that name she would suffer her whole life."</t1984>
<t1985>There were two twin children left, one named Arcadio Segundo and the other Aureliano Segundo.</t1985> <t1986>Amaranta took on the responsibility of looking after all these children.</t1986> <t1987>She arranged small chairs in the living room and accepted some neighborhood children to form a beginner's class.</t1987> <t1988>When Colonel Aureliano Buendía returned to the village, amidst the booming sound of fireworks and the resounding church bells, a children's choir came to his house to welcome him with songs.</t1988> <t1989>Aureliano José, tall like his grandfather, wearing a revolutionary officer's uniform, saluted him with a military gesture.</t1989>
<t1990>During the conversation, joyful stories were mixed with sad ones.</t1990><t1991>A year after Colonel Aureliano escaped, José Arcadio and Rebeca moved into the house that Arcadio had built.</t1991><t1992>No one knew that he had disrupted the execution.</t1992><t1993>The couple built a warm and welcoming home in their new house, situated in the most beautiful corner of the main square, nestled under the shade of an ancient almond tree with three redbird nests.</t1993><t1994>The house had a main entrance for guests and four windows to let in light.</t1994><t1995>Rebeca's friends, including the four Moscote sisters who were still unmarried, resumed their gatherings to embroider, a tradition that had never been interrupted in the past years, in the corridor adorned with begonia pots.</t1995><t1996>José Arcadio continued to enjoy the benefits of the lands he had seized, with deeds recognized by the Royalist government.</t1996><t1997>Every afternoon, he was seen returning on horseback, a double-barreled shotgun slung over his shoulder, followed by fierce hunting dogs, with a string of wild rabbits hanging from his saddle.</t1997><t1998>One September afternoon, before a threatening storm, he returned home earlier than usual.</t1998><t1999>He greeted Rebeca, who was in the dining room, tied the dogs in the yard, hung the string of rabbits in the kitchen to be cleaned and marinated later, and then went to the bedroom to change clothes.</t1999><t2000>Later, Rebeca explained that when her husband entered the bedroom, she was in the bathroom and knew nothing of what happened.</t2000><t2001>It was a hard-to-believe explanation, but no clearer one was available, and no one could think of any other reason for Rebeca to kill the man who had brought her happiness.</t2001><t2002>That might be the only mystery that was never solved in the village of Macondo.</t2002>
<t2003>Just as José Arcadio closed the door to his room, a gunshot rang out, shaking the entire house.</t2003> <t2004>A stream of blood flowed out from under the door, crawled across the living room, went out onto the street, continued to flow along the rugged roads, climbed over steps and obstacles, crawled steadily along the Turkish road, turned left onto one street then right onto another before making a carpenter's square turn right in front of the Buendía house, slipped under the closed door into the house, clung to the wall to avoid staining the carpets, continued to crawl through another room, made a wide loop to avoid the dining table, crawled along the hallway past the sugar-recovery pots and slipped under the chair where Amaranta was teaching arithmetic to Aureliano José without being seen, then disappeared as it entered the corn storage, and reappeared in the kitchen where Úrsula was cracking thirty-six eggs to make a cake.</t2004>
<t2005>- Oh, Virgin Mary, - Ursula screamed loudly.</t2005>
<t2006>The blood flowed backward, and to find its source, she crawled through the corn shed, followed the corridor lined with begonias where little Aureliano José was loudly reciting three times three is nine, passed through the dining rooms and two living rooms, then went straight out to the main road, turned left and right to enter Turkey Street, not remembering that she was still wearing the apron she used in the kitchen and the two house slippers, then she went out to the square, entered a house she had never been in before, pushed open the bedroom door, and was almost overwhelmed by the smell of gunpowder, and she found José Arcadio lying face down on the floor, his body covering the boots he had just taken off, and saw the blood trickling from his right ear.</t2006><t2007>There was no visible wound on his body, nor could the gun that had fired be found.</t2007><t2008>Nor could the acrid smell of gunpowder be washed off the corpse.</t2008><t2009>First, they bathed him with soap and scrubbing pads, then soaked him in vinegar and salt water, then used ash and lemon juice, and finally placed him in a tub of bleach for six hours straight.</t2009><t2010>They scrubbed him so much that his tattoos began to fade.</t2010><t2011>When they resorted to embalming him with pepper, dill, bay leaves, and simmering the corpse over a low flame for a whole day, the body began to fall apart, forcing them to bury him immediately.</t2011><t2012>They laid the corpse in an oversized coffin, two and a half meters long and one meter wide, lined with tin inside and tightly sealed with steel screws.</t2012><t2013>Despite the thorough washing and embalming, the corpse still emitted a burnt smell along the way as the funeral procession passed.</t2013>
<t2014>Cha Nicanô, with his round, liver-pained belly like a drum, stood on the bed sprinkling holy water on him.</t2014> <t2015>Although in recent months people had built graves with concrete slabs placed on top and scattered ashes, sawdust, and lime powder, the cemetery continued to reek of gunpowder, so much so that in later years, engineers from the Banana Company had to pour a concrete cover over the grave.</t2015> <t2016>Immediately after the coffin was taken away, Rêbêca shut the door and buried herself alive in the house, wearing a wrinkled coarse cloth outfit that no worldly desire could tear.</t2016> <t2017>During the period of the appearance of the demon Juđiô Erăngtê, Macônđô experienced a terrible heatwave that caused birds to crash through the wire mesh and die in the bedrooms; she went out once, very old, wearing old silver-white boots and a hat adorned with tiny flowers.</t2017> <t2018>The last time anyone saw her alive was when she shot dead a thief trying to break into her house.</t2018> <t2019>Since then, no one except Arhêmđa, her servant and confidante, had direct contact with her.</t2019> <t2020>There was a time when it was known that she wrote letters to the Bishop, whom she called her cousin, but it was also rumored that she never received a reply.</t2020> <t2021>The villagers had forgotten her.</t2021>
<t2022>Although returning victorious, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was not satisfied with the visible results.</t2022> <t2023>The government troops withdrew from the towns without any resistance, which sowed an illusion of an irreversible victory among the newly liberated populace.</t2023> <t2024>But the revolutionaries, especially Colonel Aureliano Buendía, understood the truth more accurately.</t2024> <t2025>Despite maintaining five thousand men under his command at that time and holding two coastal provinces, he was acutely aware that his forces were being pushed towards the sea and were in such a politically complicated situation that when he ordered the reconstruction of the church bell tower, which had been destroyed by artillery, Father Nicanor, lying on his sickbed, exclaimed: "This is a joke: the defenders of the Christian faith have shelled the church, while the Freemasons are ordering its reconstruction."</t2025> <t2026>For hours and hours, he stayed in the telegraph room, talking with the commanders of other towns, seeking a way out.</t2026>
<t2027>But every time he left the room, he increasingly confirmed his feeling that the war was coming to an end.</t2027> <t2028>When the new victory news of the Freedom fighters was delivered and loudly announced, he calculated the results achieved on the maps and understood that his army was retreating deeper into the forest to fight tuberculosis and jungle mosquitoes, moving further away from the city. "We are wasting time," he lamented to the officers.</t2028> <t2029>"We will continue to waste time while those bastards in the Freedom party are begging for a seat in parliament."</t2029> <t2030>In sleepless nights lying on the hammock hung in the very room that used to be a death row cell, he recalled the images of lawyers dressed in black with collars turned up to cover their ears and sleeves hanging down to cover their hands, stepping out of the President's mansion in the freezing cold of the night towards dawn, shivering, quickly slipping into the mournful coffee gardens at dawn to ponder over what the President meant when he said yes, or perhaps what he meant when he said no, and to search for what the President was thinking when he said something entirely different.</t2030> <t2031>Meanwhile, he swatted mosquitoes, endured the sweltering heat of thirty-five degrees, and felt increasingly closer to the dreadful morning when he would have to order his soldiers to plunge into the sea.</t2031>
<t2032>One restless night, Pila Tecnêra sang with the soldiers in the courtyard.</t2032> <t2033>He asked her to predict his future through the cards.</t2033> <t2034>"Keep your mouth shut," that was all Pila Tecnêra clearly saw after arranging the cards and gathering them back.</t2034>
<t2035>"I don't know what that means, but the omen is very clear: keep your mouth shut"… Two days later, someone replaced him and ordered an orderly to prepare a cup of coffee without sugar, then this orderly passed the order to another person, who then passed it to another until the cup of coffee without sugar, having gone through many hands, was brought to Colonel Aureliano Buendía's residence.</t2035> <t2036>Although he hadn't called for coffee, since it was already there, the colonel drank it.</t2036> <t2037>That cup of coffee contained enough strychnine to kill a stallion.</t2037> <t2038>When they brought him home, he was stiff, convulsed, with his tongue sticking out between his teeth.</t2038> <t2039>Úrsula fought with death to save him.</t2039> <t2040>After making him vomit to cleanse his stomach, she covered him with heated bed sheets and gave him egg whites to drink for two days until his weak body regained its normal temperature.</t2040> <t2041>On the fourth day, he was out of danger.</t2041> <t2042>Contrary to his wishes, due to Úrsula and his officers' insistence, he stayed in bed for another week.</t2042> <t2043>Only then did he learn that his poems had not been burned.</t2043> <t2044>"That night, mother didn't want to rush," Úrsula explained to him.</t2044> <t2045>"That night, when I went to light the stove, I thought it best to wait until your corpse was brought home."</t2045>
<t2046>Living among Remedios' dolls surrounding him, in the condition of gradually recovering health, Colonel Aureliano Buendía, through his poems, reminisced about the most vibrant periods of his life.</t2046> <t2047>He began writing poetry again. For many hours, on the sidelines of the prominent events of a war with no future, he affirmed his experiences right at the brink of death.</t2047> <t2048>Thus, his mind became increasingly clearer to the point where he could turn it inside out to examine it.</t2048> <t2049>One night, he asked Colonel Gerineldo Márquez:</t2049>
<t2050>- Tell me, why do you fight, my friend?</t2050>
<t2051>- Why are you asking me that, - Colonel Hêrinênđô Mackêt replied.</t2051> <t2052>- Because of the great Liberal party.</t2052>
<t2053>- I'm really happy because I know my goal in fighting, - he replied.</t2053> <t2054>- As for myself, until now I have only known that I fight for pride.</t2054>
<t2055>- That's terrible, - Colonel Hêrinênđô Mackêt replied.</t2055>
<t2056>The warning from Hernando Márquez delighted Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2056> <t2057>"Of course," he said.</t2057> <t2058>"But in any case, it's better not to know why you're fighting." He looked into his friend's eyes, smiled, and clarified:</t2058>
<t2059>- Or as if you had fought for something that meant nothing to anyone.</t2059>
<t2060>It was precisely that pride that prevented him from contacting the armed groups in the interior, while the leaders of the Liberal Party did not publicly make statements to dispel the notion that he was a bandit leader.</t2060> <t2061>However, he knew that if he could overcome those obstacles, he would immediately break the dangerous siege of war.</t2061> <t2062>His recovered health allowed him to think.</t2062> <t2063>So he asked Ursula not only for the remaining gold but also for the considerable capital she had accumulated. <t2446>Colonel Gerineldo Márquez was the first to perceive the futility of war.</t2446> <t2447>As the supreme commander in Macondo, he maintained regular telegraphic conversations twice a week with Colonel Aureliano Buendía.</t2447> <t2448>Initially, these exchanges confirmed the flow of the bloody conflict, where at any given moment its contours allowed for the establishment of a destination point and the projection of future directions.</t2448> <t2449>Although he never allowed himself to become entangled in intimate emotional matters, even with his closest comrades, Colonel Aureliano Buendía now maintained a familiar manner of speaking that allowed the person on the other end of the line to recognize him.</t2449> <t2450>Many times, he extended these telegraphic conversations beyond the allotted time, letting them drift into idle chatter.</t2450> <t2451>However, as the war expanded and intensified, his image gradually faded into a nebulous and unreal world.</t2451> <t2452>The clicks and clacks of his telegraph key became increasingly distant and vague, and they coalesced into words that gradually lost all meaning.</t2452> <t2453>At this point, Colonel Gerineldo Márquez merely listened, feeling uncomfortable with the sensation that he was communicating via telegraph with a stranger from another world.</t2453>

<t2455>Colonel Herinendo Marquez had completely lost touch with the war.</t2455> <t2456>What had once been a practical action, an uncontrollable passion of youth, had now become for him an old story, an empty void.</t2456> <t2457>The only place for him to escape that loneliness was Amaranta's sewing room.</t2457> <t2458>Every afternoon he would visit her.</t2458> <t2459>He liked to watch her hands as she folded the fabric on the sewing machine table, while Remedios the Beauty turned the crank.</t2459> <t2460>For hours and hours they would be together, not saying a word.</t2460> <t2461>While Amaranta was content with maintaining the intense flame of his love, he could not understand the purpose of that heart.</t2461> <t2462>When she learned that he would return, Amaranta felt a breathless joy.</t2462> <t2463>But when she saw him mingling with Colonel Aureliano Buendia's noisy guard as they entered the house, saw him worn out from his overseas struggles, saw him aged by time and neglect, saw him sticky with sweat mixed with road dust, smelling of animal odor, saw him unattractive with his arm immobilized in a sling, she was so disappointed she wanted to die. "Oh my God," she said, "this is not the man I had been waiting for."</t2463> <t2464>However, the next day, he came to the house looking more dapper: shaved and bathed, his mustache smelling of lavender, and his arm no longer in a sling.</t2464> <t2465>He brought her a prayer book bound in hard cover inlaid with mother-of-pearl.</t2465>
<t2466>- Oh, how strange men are, - she said because she didn't know what else to say.</t2466> <t2467>- Their whole life is a struggle against the priests, yet they go and give away religious books.</t2467>

<t2479>- Let's forget each other forever, - she said to him, - because we have missed our chance.</t2479>

<t2590>In that long, endless night, when Colonel Gerineldo Márquez remembered the beautiful afternoons in Amaranta's sewing room, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was tormented and restless, trying to tear apart the long, interminable night, the tough shell surrounding his loneliness.</t2590> <t2591>The only happy period of his life, since that distant afternoon when his father took him to see the ice, had taken place in the gold workshop, where time passed as he diligently cast little gold fishes.</t2591> <t2592>He had to ignite thirty-two civil wars, escape death many times, and seek glory like a pig rooting through garbage for food, only to discover nearly ten years later the meaning of the proverb "ignorance is bliss," an inherent trait of gullibility.</t2592>

<t2601>Even while speaking, he still couldn't imagine that starting a war is always much easier than ending it.</t2601> <t2602>He needed a year of bloody fighting to force the government to make peace proposals beneficial to the insurgents and another year to persuade his own faction to unanimously accept those conditions.</t2602> <t2603>He had gone to unwanted extremes of crime to suppress the uprisings of his own officers, who continued to fight stubbornly against the betrayal of the victory of the rebellion, only to end up helping the enemy forces completely dominate them.</t2603> <t2604>Never had he been as fierce a warrior as he was then.</t2604> <t2605>The clear realization that he was ultimately fighting for his own liberation and not for abstract ideas or slogans that politicians could manipulate according to specific circumstances, instilled in him a new, exhilarating enthusiasm.</t2605> <t2606>Colonel Hernando Márquez, who fought for defeat with all the zeal and loyalty he had once fought for victory, opposed his useless fear.</t2606> <t2607>"Don't worry," he said.</t2607> <t2608>"Dying isn't as easy as people think."</t2608> <t2609>In his case, that was true.</t2609> <t2610>The firm belief that his death would be foretold endowed him with a strange invulnerability, an immortality so certain that he couldn't be injured in dangerous battles and ultimately allowed him to achieve a defeat far more difficult, bloody, and costly than achieving a victory.</t2610>
<t2611>During nearly twenty years of war, Colonel Aureliano Buendía was often at home, but the hurried state each time he returned, the noisy military apparatus that followed him across the country, as well as the mythical aura that enveloped his image, and even the image of him in that mythical aura, made no one feel close to him, including Úrsula herself. <t2624>The standing army has the duty to protect the house.</t2624> <t2625>He returned in a state of being escorted, scorned, and accused of reigniting the war with the intention of selling it at a higher price.</t2625> <t2626>He shivered with fever and cold, and once again, his armpits were swollen.</t2626> <t2627>Six months before hearing the news of an armistice, Ursula had opened and cleaned the room of the couple, then burned incense in every corner, thinking that he would return to spend his old age beside Remedios' long-unused dolls.</t2627> <t2628>But in reality, in the last two years, he had repaid life for what he had enjoyed, including his share of old age.</t2628> <t2629>As he passed the jewelry workshop that Ursula had meticulously prepared, he did not recognize even the bunch of keys already inserted in the lock.</t2629> <t2630>He felt nothing at all, not even the major or minor damages left by the passage of time in the house, and that after being away for so long, if a person still retains their memories intact, it must be seen as a disaster.</t2630> <t2631>He did not feel the slightest sorrow over the peeling lime on the walls, the cobwebs in every corner, the dust covering the begonias, the termite holes in the beams, or the moss on the windowsills.</t2631> <t2632>He sat on the porch, still wearing his boots and wrapped in his coat, as if waiting for the rain to stop, and sat like that all afternoon watching the rain fall on the begonias.</t2632> <t2633>At that moment, Ursula realized that it had been a very long time since she had him in this house.</t2633>
