<t2691>With the elder, he gave a distracted smile, spread his hand with five fingers wide open, then without saying a word, he left his house to face the shouts, curses, and imprecations that followed him all the way to the village gate.</t2691> <t2692>Ursula bolted the door tightly with the intention of never opening it for the rest of her life.</t2692> <t2693>"We will rot to death inside," she thought.</t2693> <t2694>"We would rather let the ashes cover us in this house without men than let these wretched villagers enjoy watching us cry"...</t2694> <t2695>That entire morning, she searched every hidden corner to find a keepsake of her son but could not find anything.</t2695>
<t2696>The signing ceremony was held at a location about a few dozen kilometers from Macondo, right under the shade of a giant kapok tree, where the town of Neclandia was later established.</t2696> <t2697>The government representatives, party members, and the army surrendering their weapons were served by a noisy group of nuns in white robes, who looked like a flock of doves startled by the rain.</t2697> <t2698>Colonel Aureliano Buendía arrived at the location riding a mud-spattered donkey.</t2698> <t2699>He did not shave, and he was in more pain from the swelling under his arm than from the crushing defeat of his dreams, for he had reached the ultimate point of all belief, which surpassed both glory and the longing for glory.</t2699> <t2700>As per his request, the ceremony had no music, no fireworks, no ringing bells, no cheers, and no display of any kind that could disrupt the mournful nature of the armistice signing.</t2700> <t2701>A traveling photographer, having taken a picture of him—a portrait that could have been preserved—was forced to destroy the entire roll of film without checking each frame.</t2701>
<t2702>The ceremony almost took just enough time for the ink to dry on the signatures.</t2702> <t2703>The officers, still loyal to Colonel Aureliano Buendía until the last moment, sat around a rough wooden table placed in the center of the old circus tent, where the delegates were seated.</t2703> <t2704>Before signing the document, the representative of the President of the Republic intended to read aloud the surrender document, but Colonel Aureliano Buendía managed to protest in time.</t2704> <t2705>"We shouldn't waste time on formalities," he said, then proceeded to sign without reading the documents again.</t2705> <t2706>One of his officers then broke the heavy silence that had settled in the circus tent.</t2706>
<t2707>- Colonel, - the person said, - please, Colonel, could you make sure we are not the first ones to sign?</t2707>
<t2708>Colonel Aureliano Buendía paused.</t2708> <t2709>The document had made a round on the table in such absolute silence that one could read the signatures thanks to the scribbled pen marks on the paper.</t2709>
<t2710>The first signature spot is still blank.</t2710> <t2711>Colonel Aureliano Buendía is ready to sign.</t2711>
<t2712>- Colonel, - another officer of his said at that moment, - you still have enough time to sign, sir.</t2712>
<t2713>Without losing his composure, Colonel Aureliano Buendía signed the first copy.</t2713> <t2714>He had not yet signed the final copy when a young rebel colonel appeared at the door of the tent, holding the reins of a female mule loaded with two chests.</t2714> <t2715>Despite his youth, he exuded a bitter calmness.</t2715> <t2716>He was the treasurer of the revolutionary forces in the Macondo area. This young man had endured six grueling days on the road, dragging the starving mule to arrive in time for the signing of the armistice agreement.</t2716> <t2717>With the leisurely manner of someone who is disappointed, the young man unloaded the two chests, opened them wide, and then placed seventy-two gold bricks on the table, one by one.</t2717> <t2718>No one remembered that such a vast fortune still existed.</t2718> <t2719>In the tumultuous final years of the war, when the central command was disintegrating and the revolution had degenerated into a bloody brawl among the commanders, it was difficult to assign any responsibility to anyone.</t2719> <t2720>The rebels' gold, which had been melted into lumps and then coated with baked mud, was in fact not under anyone's control.</t2720> <t2721>Colonel Aureliano Buendía ordered the seventy-two gold bricks to be listed among the assets surrendered by the revolutionaries to the government, and concluded the signing ceremony without allowing anyone to deliver a speech.</t2721> <t2722>The thin, pale young man continued to stand in front of him, his amber eyes staring directly into his.</t2722>
<t2723>- And what else, young man? - Colonel Aureliano Buendía asked.</t2723>
<t2724>The young colonel gritted his teeth:</t2724>
<t2725>- The documents! - the young man said.</t2725>
<t2726>Colonel Aureliano Buendía handed him the certificate written by his own hand.</t2726> <t2727>After that, he drank a glass of lemonade and ate a biscuit distributed by the nuns; then he went to one of the tents prepared for him in case he wanted to rest.</t2727> <t2728>There, he took off his shirt and sat on the edge of a bed.</t2728> <t2729>At three fifteen in the afternoon, he pointed the muzzle of the revolver at the circle drawn on his chest with red medicine by the health officer and pulled the trigger. At that exact time in Macondo, Úrsula opened the lid of the pot of milk on the stove, surprised at how long it was taking to boil, and found it full of worms.</t2729>
<t2730>- They have killed Aureliano, - the old man exclaimed in shock.</t2730>
<t2731>The old woman looked out into the yard, obediently following her habit of solitude, and immediately she saw José Arcadio Buendía, drenched in rain and looking much older and sadder than when she had last seen him.</t2731> <t2732>"They killed him for treason," Úrsula judged, "and no one even bothered to close his eyes."</t2732> <t2733>As night fell, through her tears she saw golden round discs glowing orange, whizzing through the sky like lightning bolts, and she thought it was a sign of death.</t2733> <t2734>She still sat under the chestnut tree, her head resting on her husband's knee as she sobbed, while they brought her Colonel Aureliano Buendía, his eyes wide open with anger, wrapped in a cloak stained with dried blood.</t2734>
<t2735>He was not in any life-threatening danger.</t2735> <t2736>The bullet went straight through his chest to the point where the doctor threaded a medicated string from his chest to his back.</t2736> <t2737>"This is my masterpiece," he said to him with a pleased expression.</t2737> <t2738>It was the only spot where a bullet could pass through without causing any damage to any internal organs.</t2738> <t2739>Colonel Aureliano Buendía saw himself surrounded by miserable nuns chanting mournful hymns to pray for his soul to find peace in eternal rest, and he immediately regretted not shooting himself in the mouth as he had planned, and he had refrained from doing so only to mock Pila Tecnéra's prediction.</t2739>
<t2740>- If I still had power, - he said to the doctor - I would shoot you without any questioning.</t2740> <t2741>Not because you saved my life but because you made me a laughingstock to the world.</t2741>
<t2742>The failed suicide attempt restored his once-faded prestige.</t2742> <t2743>Those who had spread rumors that he sold out the war for a house with four walls made entirely of gold bricks affirmed that the suicide attempt was a noble act and thus declared him a martyr.</t2743> <t2744>Afterward, when he refused the Order of Merit awarded by the President of the Republic, even his mortal enemies came one by one to parade through his bedroom, urging him not to recognize the terms of the armistice agreement and to ignite a new war.</t2744> <t2745>Moved by the broad support of his old comrades-in-arms, Colonel Aureliano Buendía did not dismiss the possibility of fulfilling their wishes.</t2745> <t2746>On the contrary, there were moments when he seemed so enthusiastic about the idea of a new war that Colonel Gerineldo Márquez thought it would be declared at the slightest provocation.</t2746> <t2747>Indeed, the provocation came when the President of the Republic refused to grant war pensions to veterans of both the Liberal and Conservative sides until each individual case was reviewed and the war pension law was passed by Congress.</t2747> <t2748>"This is an abuse," Colonel Aureliano Buendía fumed, "People will die of old age while waiting for a response." </t2748> <t2749>For the first time, he left the rocking chair that Úrsula had bought for his convalescence and paced the room, reading a decisive telegram addressed to the President of the Republic.</t2749> <t2750>In this telegram, he denounced the government's initial violations of the Neclandia armistice agreement and threatened to declare war if the pensions were not resolved within fifteen days.</t2750> <t2751>His stance was so correct that it garnered the support of everyone, even the approval of the Conservative veterans.</t2751> <t2752>But the government's only response was to increase the number of guards at his door, ostensibly to protect the house and strictly prohibit all visits.</t2752> <t2753>Similar measures were applied nationwide to generals under house arrest.</t2753> <t2754>It was a timely, brutal, and effective campaign, so much so that two months after the armistice, when Colonel Aureliano Buendía had fully recovered, his closest comrades-in-arms were either dead, exiled, or had completely submitted to the public house arrest.</t2754>
<t2755>In December, Colonel Aureliano Buendía stepped out of the bedroom and just a glance at the hallway was enough for him to stop thinking about the war.</t2755> <t2756>With a vitality seemingly impossible for her age, Úrsula did everything to rejuvenate the house.</t2756> <t2757>"Now they will know who I am," she said when she was certain her son would live.</t2757> <t2758>"In this town, there will be no house more luxurious, more open to the four winds than this house of mad people."</t2758> <t2759>She ordered the house to be cleaned, whitewashed, replaced the furniture, refurbished the garden, planted new flowers, and opened all the doors: the main door and the windows, to let the brilliant summer light flood into the bedrooms.</t2759> <t2760>She decided to stop mourning the successive deaths of her loved ones, and she herself began to wear colorful, youthful dresses instead of the old, shabby ones.</t2760> <t2761>Music once again emanated from the automatic piano, making the house more lively.</t2761> <t2762>Upon hearing that music, Amaranta deeply missed Pietro Crespi, remembered the ivory-yellow gardenia on his lapel, and the lavender scent that emanated from him.</t2762> <t2763>And within her withered heart blossomed a pure feeling, cleansed by time.</t2763> <t2764>One afternoon, while tidying up the living room, Úrsula asked the house guards to lend a hand.</t2764> <t2765>The young commander of the guard allowed it.</t2765> <t2766>Gradually, Úrsula asked them to help with other tasks as well.</t2766> <t2767>She invited them to meals, gave them clothes and shoes, and taught them to read and write.</t2767> <t2768>When the government ceased the guard duty, one of those soldiers stayed to serve her for many years.</t2768>
<t2769>The New Year's festival dawned brightly with the young commander of the guard, driven mad by the reckless behavior of Remedios the Beauty, dying of love right next to her window.</t2769>
<t2770>Chapter 10</t2770>
<t2771>In his later years, lying on his deathbed, Aureliano Segundo remembered the rainy June afternoon when he walked into the bedroom to see his firstborn son for the first time.</t2771> <t2772>Although the child was weak and often cried, bearing no resemblance to anyone in the Buendía family, he did not have to think hard to come up with a name for him.</t2772>
<t2773>- We will call him José Arcadio, - he said.</t2773>
<t2774>Fernanda del Carpio, the beautiful wife he married last year, agreed to name the baby that way.</t2774><t2775>As for herself, Úrsula could not hide her vague pain. In the long history of the family, the practice of naming children the same from generation to generation allowed her to draw conclusions that were almost confirmed.</t2775><t2776>While those named Aureliano were melancholic yet very intelligent, those named José Arcadio were impulsive and bold; but all bore the mark of a tragic fate.</t2776><t2777>The only cases that were difficult to distinguish were those of José Arcadio Segundo and Aureliano Segundo.</t2777><t2778>In their childhood, both were so alike and mischievous that even Santa Sofía de la Piedad could not tell the twin brothers apart.</t2778><t2779>On the day of the naming ceremony, Amaranta put a saint medallion with their names engraved on each and dressed them in different colored clothes marked with their initials; but when they went to class, they swapped names, clothes, and medallions.</t2779><t2780>The teacher, Mencho Escalona, who had gotten used to identifying José Arcadio Segundo by his blue and a medallion with the name José Arcadio Segundo.</t2780><t2781>From then on, it was impossible to know exactly who was who.</t2781><t2782>Even when they had grown up and life had made them different, Úrsula continued to wonder if at some point they had made a mistake in their confusing game, they would have changed a lot.</t2782><t2783>Even in their youth, they were like two synchronized machines.</t2783><t2784>They woke up at the same time, felt the need to go to the bathroom at the same hour, suffered from the same ailments, and had identical dreams.</t2784><t2785>At home, everyone believed that their synchronized actions were a deliberate attempt to confuse people, but no one understood their true nature until one day Santa Sofía de la Piedad gave one of them a glass of lemonade, and later the other one said it lacked sugar.</t2785><t2786>Indeed, she had forgotten to put sugar in the lemonade.</t2786><t2787>Santa Sofía de la Piedad recounted this to Úrsula.</t2787><t2788>"All the children in this family are like that," she said, not at all surprised.</t2788><t2789>"They are crazy from birth."</t2789><t2790>Time passed and eventually ended the confusion of identities.</t2790><t2791>The boy in the confusing games, still called Aureliano Segundo, had the robust body of his grandfather, while the other, still called José Arcadio Segundo, had the slender build of the colonel, and the only thing they shared was the family's characteristic loneliness.</t2791><t2792>Perhaps the swapping of appearances, names, and characteristics was what made Úrsula suspect that from their childhood, they had been shuffled like a deck of cards.</t2792><t2793>The clear difference between the two was evident during the most intense period of the war when José Arcadio Segundo asked Colonel Gerineldo Márquez to take him to watch the executions.</t2793>
<t2794>Contrary to Ucsula's thoughts, his wish was fulfilled.</t2794> <t2795>On the contrary, Aureliano Segundo felt weak at the thought of being present at the execution.</t2795> <t2796>The boy only liked staying at home.</t2796> <t2797>At the age of twelve, he asked Ucsula what was in the closed room.</t2797> <t2798>"Just papers," she replied, "those are Melquíades' books and the strange things he wrote in his later years."</t2798> <t2799>The answer that should have reassured him only further stimulated his curiosity.</t2799> <t2800>He earnestly begged to be allowed to see, passionately promising her that he would not damage anything in there, to the point that Ucsula couldn't resist and had to give him the key.</t2800> <t2801>No one had entered the room since they carried Melquíades' corpse away; and a rusty iron lock that had long since seized up had been installed on the door.</t2801> <t2802>However, when Aureliano Segundo flung the windows open, a familiar beam of light seemed to illuminate the room as it always had; the room was not covered in dust, there were no cobwebs, but on the contrary, everything was clean, perhaps even more meticulously swept and polished than on the day of the funeral, the ink in the bottle was not dry, rust had not dulled the shine of the metal tools; even the embers in the stove where José Arcadio Buendía boiled mercury were still glowing.</t2802> <t2803>On the shelves were books bound in light brown cardboard resembling tanned human skin and even handwritten pages with fresh ink.</t2803> <t2804>Although the room had been closed for many years, the air there seemed cleaner than in other parts of the house.</t2804> <t2805>Everything was so new that a few weeks later when Ucsula brought in a bucket of water and a broom to sweep the floor, she found she didn't need to do anything.</t2805> <t2806>Aureliano Segundo was engrossed in reading a book.</t2806> <t2807>Although the book had lost its cover and had no title, the boy enjoyed the story of a woman sitting at a table picking up each grain of rice with chopsticks; the story of a fisherman borrowing a lead weight from his neighbor to fasten to his fishing net and the fish he later returned to the neighbor carrying a pearl in its belly; the story of a magic lamp that granted all wishes and flying carpets.</t2807>
<t2808>With an excited look, the boy asked Ursula if these stories were true, and she replied that they were, that a long time ago, the gypsies brought magic lamps and flying carpets to the village of Macondo.</t2808>
<t2809>- The thing is, - he sighed, - the world is becoming increasingly impoverished, and therefore, those things are no longer seen.</t2809>
<t2810>After finishing the book with many stories left incomplete due to missing pages, Aureliano Segundo turned to translating the handwritten manuscripts.</t2810> <t2811>It was an impossible task.</t2811> <t2812>The letters looked like clothes hanging on a wire, and overall it resembled sheet music more than a piece of literature.</t2812> <t2813>One scorching afternoon, while deciphering the manuscripts, the boy felt he was not alone in the room.</t2813> <t2814>Melquiades was sitting there, with his back to the window, hands resting on his knees.</t2814> <t2815>He still wore the bizarre overcoat and the wide-brimmed hat, sweat dripping from his hair down his temples, exactly as he had appeared to Aureliano and José Arcadio when they were young.</t2815> <t2816>Aureliano Segundo recognized him immediately, because that hereditary memory had been passed down from generation to generation, and it came to him from his grandfather's recollection.</t2816>
<t2817>- Hello, sir, - Aureliano Segundo said.</t2817>
<t2818>- Hello, young friend, - Menkyadet replied.</t2818>
<t2819>Since that time, for a few years, they met almost every afternoon.</t2819> <t2820>Melquiades talked to the boy about the world, trying to pass on his ancient knowledge, but he refused to translate the handwritten pages.</t2820>
<t2821>"No one should know the meaning until a hundred years have passed," the old man explained.</t2821> <t2822>Aureliano Segundo kept these meetings a secret forever.</t2822> <t2823>There were times when the boy felt his private world was being shattered, because Úrsula had entered the room just as Melquíades was present.</t2823> <t2824>But she did not see him.</t2824>
<t2825>- Who are you talking to? - the old man asked.</t2825>
<t2826>- Not telling anyone, right? - Aureliano Segundo replied.</t2826>
<t2827>- Your great-grandfather was the same way, - Ursula said, - He would just talk to himself.</t2827>
<t2828>Meanwhile, José Arcadio Segundo found satisfaction in watching an execution.</t2828> <t2829>All his life, he would forever remember the flash of the six bullets fired simultaneously and the echo of the gunshots reverberating from the surrounding mountains, then recall the sad smile and the startled eyes of the man being shot, who stood proudly as the blood soaked his shirt, who kept smiling even as they untied him from the execution post and placed him in a coffin filled with lime powder.</t2829> <t2830>"Still alive," he thought.</t2830> <t2831>"They are going to bury him alive."</t2831> <t2832>He was so terrified, so much so that from that moment on he cursed the military profession and war, not just because of the executions but mainly because of the disgusting habit of burying the executed alive.</t2832> <t2833>Thus, no one knew when he started ringing the church bells, began assisting Father Antonio Isabel, who was nicknamed El Cachorro and had replaced the parish priest, and started taking care of the fighting cocks raised in the monastery yard.</t2833> <t2834>When Colonel Gerineldo Márquez found out, he scolded the boy for a long time for learning trades that were condemned by the Liberals.</t2834>
<t2835>- The problem is, - the boy answered, - it seems I have become a Royalist.</t2835>
<t2836>You believe that as if it were an affirmation of misfortune.</t2836> <t2837>Colonel Aureliano Buendía, annoyed, recounted the story to Úrsula.</t2837>
<t2838>- The better, - the old man praised.</t2838> <t2839>- Oh Lord, he will become a monk so that finally God will come to this house.</t2839>
<t2840>Immediately, everyone knew that Father Antonio Isabel was preparing to conduct the first Holy Communion for the boy.</t2840> <t2841>Father taught him catechism while plucking the neck feathers of fighting cocks.</t2841> <t2842>Father used simple, easy-to-understand examples to explain to him while placing the hens in their nests, just as it happened with God on the second day of Creation, the day the chicks were formed in the eggs.</t2842> <t2843>From that time, the pastor showed the first signs of senility, which many years later led him to say that perhaps the devil had won the rebellion against God's will, who sits on the heavenly throne, not revealing his form before the eyes of the foolish.</t2843> <t2844>Forged by the courage of his teacher, a few months later, José Arcadio Segundo had become very knowledgeable in religious tricks to deceive the devil and also experienced in raising chickens.</t2844> <t2845>Amaranta sewed him a linen suit with a collar and tie, bought him a pair of white shoes, and gilded his name on the ribbons tied to the candle.</t2845> <t2846>Two nights before his first Holy Communion, Father Antonio Isabel closed the holy storeroom to conduct a confession ceremony with him, aided by a book listing sins.</t2846> <t2847>It was such a long list that the old pastor, who was used to going to bed at six o'clock, fell asleep in the rocking chair before finishing.</t2847>
<t2848>The questions were a revelation to José Arcadio Segundo.</t2848> <t2849>He was not surprised to see the priest ask him if he had been with a woman, and he proudly answered no, but he was bewildered when the priest asked if he had been with animals.</t2849> <t2850>In May, on the first Friday, he took communion with a heart aching from curiosity.</t2850> <t2851>Afterward, he asked Petronio, the sickly monk who kept the holy relics, lived in the bell tower, and who, according to rumors, ate fireflies to survive, and Petronio answered him: "It's because there was a vile brother who slept with a female donkey."</t2851> <t2852>José Arcadio Segundo was more astonished than ever, demanding further explanations to the point that Petronio lost his patience.</t2852>
<t2853>- I go on Tuesday evenings every week, - he confessed. - If you promise not to tell anyone, next Tuesday I'll take you with me.</t2853>
<t2854>Indeed, on the third day, Petronio carried a wooden chair that no one knew what it was used for until now, down from the bell tower, and led Jose Arcadio Segundo to a nearby garden.</t2854><t2855>The boy was so passionate about those adventurous nights that it was a long time before people saw him appear at Catarino's dance hall again. He became a cockfighter.</t2855><t2856>"Take those animals somewhere else," Ursula said when she first saw him bring the beautiful fighting cocks home.</t2856><t2857>"Those chickens have brought so much bitterness to this house, and now you want to bring more suffering to us?"</t2857><t2858>Jose Arcadio Segundo did not argue with his great-grandmother; he quietly took the fighting cocks to be raised at the house of his grandmother, Pilar Ternera, who was willing to indulge him so he could be present at her home.</t2858><t2859>Immediately, he demonstrated the knowledge of Father Antonio Isabel at the cockfighting ring, preparing enough money not only to enrich the chickens he raised but also to satisfy his adventurous spirit.</t2859><t2860>At this time, Ursula compared him to his brother and could not understand how two twins who were identical in childhood could now be so completely different.</t2860><t2861>She did not have to ponder this thought for long because Aureliano Segundo soon began to show signs of laziness.</t2861><t2862>When sitting in Melquiades' room, he was as contemplative as Colonel Aureliano Buendia in his youth.</t2862><t2863>But shortly before the who greeted him warmly.</t2864><t2865>Aureliano Segundo was not surprised because it often happened that people mistook him for his brother.</t2865><t2866>But he did not bother to correct her even when she tried to soften his heart with her sobbing, and thus she led him to her bedroom.</t2866><t2867>From the first meeting, she liked him so much that she cheated during the lottery draw to ensure he won the accordion.</t2867><t2868>After a few weeks, Aureliano Segundo realized that this woman alternated between sleeping with him and his brother, thinking they were one person, and instead of clarifying the situation, he ignored it to prolong the state of both brothers sharing the same woman.</t2868><t2869>He did not return to Melquiades' room.</t2869><t2870>Every afternoon, he was outside in the yard, engrossed in learning the accordion despite Ursula's scolding, who had banned music because the house was in mourning, and besides, she despised the accordion as a toy for vagabonds, successors of Francisco the Man.</t2870><t2871>However, Aureliano Segundo became a famous accordion player and remained famous even after marrying, having children, and becoming one of the most respected people in the town of Macondo.</t2871>
<t2872>In the span of a few months, he and his brother shared nights of pleasure with that woman.</t2872> <t2873>He followed his brother, disrupted his plans, and when he was sure that José Arcadio Segundo would not go to the woman that night, he would sneak in to sleep with her.</t2873> <t2874>One morning, he discovered his brother was ill.</t2874> <t2875>Two days later, he caught his brother clinging to the latrine, drenched in sweat and tears, crying, and then he understood.</t2875> <t2876>His brother confessed that the woman had abandoned him because he had given her gonorrhea.</t2876> <t2877>His brother also recounted how Pilar Ternera had treated the disease.</t2877> <t2878>Aureliano Segundo secretly endured the burning permanganate baths and drank diuretics.</t2878> <t2879>Both of them recovered after three months of silently enduring the pain.</t2879> <t2880>José Arcadio Segundo no longer saw the woman.</t2880> <t2881>Aureliano Segundo received his brother's forgiveness and stayed with the woman for the rest of his life.</t2881>
<t2882>The woman's name was Petra Cotes.</t2882> <t2883>She and her husband, who made a living from the lottery, came to Macondo in the midst of fierce warfare, and when her husband died, she continued the trade.</t2883> <t2884>She was a clean and youthful mulatto woman, with almond-shaped hazel eyes that made her face as fierce as a leopard's, but she had a generous heart and an exceptional talent for seduction.</t2884> <t2885>When Úrsula discovered that José Arcadio Segundo had become a cockfighter and Aureliano Segundo played the accordion during the lively nights at his mistress's house, she was furious.</t2885> <t2886>It seemed as if these two had concentrated within themselves all the family's vices and shortcomings, failing to inherit its precious virtues.</t2886> <t2887>Thus, she decided that no one would be named José Arcadio or Aureliano. However, when Aureliano Segundo had his first son and named him José Arcadio, she did not dare oppose him.</t2887>
<t2888>- Alright, I agree, - Ursula said, - but on the condition that I get to raise it.</t2888>
<t2889>Although he was already a hundred-year-old man and nearly blind because of the film that had almost covered his pupils, he still maintained his health, without any bad habits or senility.</t2889> <t2890>No one was better than him in teaching a distinguished man who could restore the family's honor, a man who had never heard of war, cockfighting, prostitutes, or the booming agricultural trades, which, according to Ursula's thinking, had led to the downfall of his lineage.</t2890> <t2891>"This boy will become a priest," he solemnly promised.</t2891> <t2892>"And if God lets me live, he will become the Pope."</t2892> <t2893>When they heard this, everyone burst out laughing, not just in the room but throughout the entire house, which was currently filled with the jovial friends of Aureliano Segundo. War, forgotten by his own poor memory, suddenly came alive in the sound of popping champagne corks.</t2893>
<t2894>- Come on, let's toast to the health of the Pope, - Aureliano Segundo said, raising his glass.</t2894>
<t2895>The guests all cheered in unison.</t2895><t2896>Afterward, the host played the accordion.</t2896><t2897>Firecrackers exploded and drums resounded throughout the village.</t2897><t2898>Early in the morning, the guests, tipsy from champagne, slaughtered six cows and laid the meat out on the street, ready to invite the crowd to join the festivities.</t2898><t2899>No one was surprised.</t2899><t2900>Since Aureliano Segundo took charge of the family, such celebrations had become commonplace, even though they never had as legitimate a reason as this one, which was to celebrate the birth of a Pope.</t2900><t2901>In a few years, he had amassed one of the largest fortunes in the swamp region without much effort, simply due to sheer luck, which was the extraordinarily rapid multiplication of his livestock.</t2901><t2902>Cows gave birth to triplets, hens laid eggs twice a day, and pigs grew at an astonishing rate.</t2902><t2903>No one could explain the wealth that seemed to be the result of some miraculous phenomenon.</t2903><t2904>"Now you must be frugal," Úrsula told her great-grandson who spent lavishly.</t2904><t2905>"Luck won't follow you for your entire life."</t2905><t2906>But Aureliano Segundo did not heed her advice.</t2906><t2907>The more champagne he uncorked to entertain his friends, the more his livestock reproduced wildly, and he became very confident that this was the source of his wealth, and therefore he never let Petra Cotes stay far from the livestock, and even after marrying and having children, he continued to live with her with Fernanda's complicity.</t2908><t2909>With a robust and muscular body like that of his great-grandfather and grandfather, but with a more cheerful and carefree disposition, Aureliano Segundo hardly had enough time to tend to his livestock.</t2909><t2910>All he needed to do was bring Petra Cotes to the barns, let her ride a horse around the farms, and that was enough for his animals to reproduce rapidly.</t2910>
<t2911>Like all the good things that had ever happened in his long life, that unmanageable wealth seemed predestined by fate.</t2911> <t2912>Even during the final period of the war, Petra Cotes still issued lottery tickets, and at times Aureliano Segundo had to steal money from Úrsula to cover all the expenses.</t2912> <t2913>They lived together comfortably, without any concerns except for making sure they slept together every night, even on days when they should abstain, frolicking until dawn.</t2913> <t2914>"That woman will be the ruin of you," Úrsula scolded her great-grandson whenever she saw him enter the house like a sleepwalker. "She has made you foolish, and one day we'll see you writhing in pain with a toad in your belly."</t2914> <t2915>José Arcadio Segundo, who took a long time to realize that his younger brother had outsmarted him, could not understand his brother's passion.</t2915> <t2916>He remembered Petra Cotes as an easy woman, more accurately as someone lazy in bed and completely dull when it came to love.</t2916>
<t2917>Ignoring Ursula's scoldings and his brother's mockery, Aureliano Segundo only thought of finding a job that would allow him to maintain the house for Fernanda and to die with her, on her and under her, in a night of wild passion.</t2917> <t2918>When Colonel Aureliano Buendía reopened the goldsmith workshop, drawn by the serene joy of old age, Aureliano Segundo thought that following the trade of making little gold fishes would also be a good idea.</t2918> <t2919>He spent many hours in the sweltering small room watching the hard metal sheets, patiently forged and carved by the Colonel, gradually turn into shimmering scales.</t2919>
<t2920>He found the job very meticulous, meanwhile the longing for Petra Cotes was so overwhelming and intense that just three weeks later he disappeared from the workshop.</t2920> <t2921>That was the period when Petra Cotes was making a fortune by using rabbits as prizes to run a lottery.</t2921> <t2922>These rabbits were born and grew rapidly.</t2922> <t2923>They were countless.</t2923> <t2924>Even though she increased the tickets and the number of draws many times, she still couldn't use up all the rabbits for the prizes.</t2924> <t2925>At first, Aureliano Segundo did not realize how these rabbits were reproducing.</t2925> <t2926>But one night, when no one in the village wanted to talk about the rabbit lotteries, he felt a thunderous noise right by the wall.</t2926> <t2927>"Don't be startled!" Petra Cotes said, "those are the rabbits!".</t2927> <t2928>They couldn't sleep any longer, tossing and turning with worry about the livestock.</t2928> <t2929>At dawn, Aureliano Segundo opened the door and saw the yard teeming with rabbits, their fur gleaming in the morning light.</t2929> <t2930>Petra Cotes laughed uncontrollably, unable to resist the urge to tease him:</t2930>
<t2931>- These rabbits were just born the night the crow took them - she said.</t2931>
<t2932>- Oh dear, that's so scary! - he said - Why don't we try with a cow?</t2932>
<t2933>A few days later, while cleaning the yard, Petra Cotes replaced the rabbits with a cow.</t2933> <t2934>A few months later, the cow gave birth to triplets.</t2934> <t2935>Everything started from there.</t2935> <t2936>In no time, Aureliano Segundo became a wealthy landowner, possessing vast lands and numerous herds of livestock, and he barely had enough time to expand his cattle pens and grazing fields further.</t2936> <t2937>It was an intoxicating wealth that even he found overwhelming, unable to restrain his exuberant display of joy.</t2937> <t2938>"Oh, cows, spread out because life is too short," he shouted.</t2938> <t2939>Úrsula wondered if he had committed a crime, if not a thief then a cattle rustler, and every time she saw him pop two champagne bottles just to spray the foam over his head with delight, she scolded him for being a wastrel.</t2939> <t2940>She never left him in peace, so one day Aureliano Segundo woke up with a calm joy, grabbed a basket of paper money, a bucket of paste, and a brush, humming her old songs.</t2940> <t2941>Francisco - The Man, then he pasted the one-peso bills on the walls of the house, from top to bottom, both inside and outside.</t2941> <t2942>The old house, which had been whitewashed since the purchase of the automatic piano, now looked strangely like a mosque. Amid the family's chaos, Úrsula's anger, and the villagers' joy as they poured into the streets to witness the spectacle of a man flaunting his wealth, Aureliano Segundo finished pasting the paper money on the house, from the front wall to the kitchen, including the toilet and bedroom, then threw the remaining money into the yard.</t2942>
<t2943>- Now then, - he said, - I hope that no one in this house will insult me about money anymore.</t2943>
<t2944>The story happened like this.</t2944> <t2945>Ursula ordered the peeling of the banknotes stuck with plaster from the walls and whitewashed the house. "Oh, my merciful God," she prayed, "make us poor again like when this village was first established, so that in the next life you won't punish us for wasting money."</t2945> <t2946>Her prayers were answered in the opposite way.</t2946> <t2947>Indeed, they were. A worker peeling the banknotes off the wall accidentally bumped into the statue of Saint Joseph, a giant plaster statue that someone had brought to the house when the war was about to end, and it shattered upon hitting the ground.</t2947> <t2948>Inside, it was filled with gold coins.</t2948> <t2949>No one remembered who had brought the life-sized statue to the house.</t2949> <t2950>"Three men brought it," Amaranta said.</t2950> <t2951>"They asked me to tell our family to keep the statue until the rainy season passed, and I told them to place it there, in that corner, so no one would bump into it, and they carefully left it there and never came back for it."</t2951> <t2952>Recently, Ursula had lit candles and prayed before the statue of Saint Joseph without suspecting that inside it were nearly two hundred kilograms of gold.</t2952> <t2953>The late revelation of her polytheistic faith deepened her worries.</t2953> <t2954>She cursed the valuable gold coins, stuffed them into three burlap sacks, and buried them in a secret location, waiting for the three strangers to come and claim them.</t2954> <t2955>Much later, in the hard years of her old age, Ursula would often interrupt conversations with visiting strangers to ask if, during the turmoil, they had left a plaster statue of Saint Joseph at her house for safekeeping during the rainy season.</t2955>
<t2956>Those events, which often made Ursula very anxious, were commonplace during this period.</t2956><t2957>Macondo was submerged in a miraculous devastation.</t2957><t2958>The mud-walled thatched houses of the founding fathers were now replaced by brick houses with cement floors and sunshades woven from small wooden slats.</t2958><t2959>These houses made the already hot atmosphere even more stifling at two in the afternoon.</t2959><t2960>The old village of José Arcadio Buendía now only had dusty almond trees ready to endure much harsher conditions, and the crystal-clear river whose prehistoric rocks had been shattered into fragments by the frenzied hammer blows of José Arcadio Segundo when he began dredging the river to establish a waterway.</t2960><t2961>It was a crazy dream comparable to the dreams of his ancestors, because the riverbed full of boulders and its rapids prevented boats from traveling from Macondo to the sea.</t2961><t2962>But José Arcadio Segundo, in his bold and reckless determination, threw himself into the work with fervor.</t2962><t2963>Until now, he had never revealed his calculative abilities.</t2963><t2964>Except for a brief affair with Petra Cotes, he had never known a woman.</t2964><t2965>Ursula considered him a prime example of the lack of intelligence that her family, in its enduring history, had produced.</t2965><t2966>He almost had no ability to stand out even though he was an expert cockfighter.</t2966><t2967>When Colonel Aureliano Buendía told him about the Spanish ship lying twelve kilometers from the sea, whose hull had turned to coal, which he had seen with his own eyes during the war, this story seemed mystical to others for a long time but was understandable to him.</t2967><t2968>He sold all his fighting cocks at the highest price, gathered the village men, and spent money on saws and chisels, then began the grand task of breaking rocks, dredging the riverbed, and overcoming rapids... "I know it all too well," Ursula said.</t2968><t2969>"It's as if time runs in circles and now we are back at the beginning."</t2969><t2970>When he was convinced that boats could navigate the river, José Arcadio Segundo meticulously presented his plans, and his brother gave him enough money for the work.</t2970><t2971>He was absent for a long time.</t2971><t2972>People were gossiping that his plan to buy a steamboat was just a ruse to get money from his brother when news spread: a warship was approaching the town.</t2972><t2973>The people of Macondo, who had long forgotten the feats of José Arcadio Buendía, flocked noisily to the riverbank and, with skeptical eyes, saw the first boat slowly approaching.</t2973><t2974>It was the first and also the last boat to dock there.</t2974><t2975>In reality, it was just a wooden raft pulled by twenty men walking with thick ropes. Standing at the end of the raft, with bright, enthusiastic eyes, José Arcadio Segundo directed this costly journey.</t2975><t2976>Accompanying him was a group of flamboyant village prostitutes, carrying luxurious parasols to shield from the harsh sun, draped in precious silk scarves, faces heavily made up, hair adorned with flowers, golden snakes coiled around their arms, and teeth inlaid with pearls.</t2976><t2977>That wooden raft was the only means of transportation that José Arcadio Segundo managed to push upstream to Macondo, and it was only that one time.</t2977><t2978>But he would never admit it was a failure of his initial endeavor; instead, he always declared it as his achievement, "a victory of willpower."</t2978><t2979>He placed all the calculated expenses of the trip on his brother's shoulders.</t2979><t2980>The only thing left from this unfavorable beginning was the fresh breeze brought to the town by the French-style prostitutes.</t2980><t2981>Their exquisite art of pleasing men changed the traditional ways of prostitution, and its social welfare significance toppled Catarino's store, turning the streets into a common market with Japanese-style lanterns and phonographs playing melancholic tunes.</t2981><t2982>They were the driving force behind the bloody Carnival festival that plunged Macondo into fear for three days, and its only lasting result was that it allowed Aureliano Segundo to meet Fernanda del Carpio.</t2982>
<t2983>Remedios the Beauty, chosen as the beauty queen.</t2983><t2984>Ursula, who had once trembled before the mesmerizing beauty of her great-granddaughter, could do nothing to prevent this selection.</t2984><t2985>Until that moment, she had tried every way to keep her from going out, except for attending mass with Amaranta, but she forced her to use a black veil to cover her face.</t2985><t2986>The less devout men, those who pretended to be priests to conduct irreverent masses at Catarino's store, went to church with the sole purpose of catching even a fleeting glimpse of Remedios the Beauty, whose legendary beauty was fervently discussed throughout the swamp region.</t2986><t2987>This happened long before they found an opportunity that was even more significant to them, because many of them, after beholding the beauty of Remedios the Beauty, never regained peaceful sleep.</t2987><t2988>The person who created that opportunity was a stranger who had forever lost the tranquility of his soul, had fallen into a state of contemplative melancholy and easily provoked suffering, and a few years later was crushed by a train because he fell asleep on the tracks.</t2988><t2989>From the moment he was seen at the church wearing a white and blue flannel suit, no one doubted that he was a stranger who had come from afar, possibly from some ancient city deep inland, lured by the beauty of Remedios the Beauty.</t2989><t2990>He was a very handsome, gallant, and courteous man, with a harmoniously proportioned body to the extent that if Pietro Crespi stood next to him, he would seem sickly and frail.</t2990><t2991>Many women whispered to each other with sad smiles that he was worthy of the girl.</t2991><t2992>He did not associate with anyone in Macondo. He appeared early on Sunday mornings, like a prince from a fairy tale riding a horse saddled with flannel and stirrups.</t2992><t2993>That was his manner of presence, because with that manner, from the very first time they saw him in the church, everyone agreed that it was a silent and heavy funeral, a secret pact, a bitter disappointment whose outcome could not be love between him and Remedios the Beauty, but had to be death.</t2993><t2994>On the sixth Sunday at church, the young man appeared holding a yellow rose.</t2994><t2995>He stood listening to the mass, as he had always stood, and when the mass ended, he stepped forward to block Remedios the Beauty's path and offered her the single rose.</t2995><t2996>She naturally accepted the flower, as if she had been prepared to receive this offering, and immediately, in a flash, she lifted her veil to reveal her face and smiled to thank him.</t2996><t2997>That was all the girl did.</t2997><t2998>But that fleeting moment was an immortal instant not only for the young man but also for all the men who had the honor of experiencing that poignant moment.</t2998>
<t2999>From that moment, the young man set up a band right next to Remedios the Beauty's window, and sometimes the band played until the next morning.</t2999> <t3000>Aureliano Segundo was the only one who sympathized with him and tried to bring him back to reality.</t3000> <t3001>"You shouldn't waste any more time," he told him one night. "The girls in this house are dumber than mules."</t3001> <t3002>He befriended him, invited him to bathe in champagne, and tried to make him understand that the girls in his family had "hearts of stone," but still couldn't shake his determination.</t3002> <t3003>Annoyed by the never-ending nights of music, Colonel Aureliano Buendía threatened to cure him of his lovesickness with the butt of his pistol.</t3003>
<t3004>Nothing could force him to give up that mad love, except for his own pitiable state of insanity.</t3004> <t3005>From a charming and decent man, he became ill-tempered and ragged.</t3005> <t3006>It was rumored that he had abandoned his power and wealth in his distant homeland, although in reality no one knew exactly where he came from.</t3006> <t3007>He became quarrelsome with the waiters in the refreshment bar, and then one day woke up drenched in his own filth right in Catarino's shop. The saddest part of his tragedy was that Remedios the Beauty never paid any attention to him, even when he appeared as a prince in her house.</t3007> <t3008>She nonchalantly accepted the golden rose without any ill intent, and rather enjoyed his overly generous gesture, lifting her veil to see him clearly, not to let him see her face.</t3008>
<t3009>In fact, Remedios the Beauty was not a person of this worldly realm.</t3009> <t3010>Even when she was well past puberty, Santa Sofía de la Piedad still had to bathe and dress her, and even after she became self-aware, the woman still had to keep an eye on her to prevent her from drawing animals on the walls with her own excrement.</t3010> <t3011>At twenty years old, she still hadn't learned to read or write, hadn't sat at the table to eat with utensils, and continued to walk around the house naked because her human essence couldn't tolerate any form of ceremony.</t3011> <t3012>When the young commander of the guard confessed his love to her, she simply rejected him because his rash words astonished her too much.</t3012> <t3013>"You see, isn't he such a fool?" she said to Amaranta.</t3013> <t3014>"He told me he was dying because of me, as if I were some kind of cholera," she added.</t3014> <t3015>When she saw him actually dead by her window, Remedios the Beauty reaffirmed her initial feeling:</t3015>
<t3016>- See that? - she said critically, - the guy is really stupid!</t3016>
<t3017>It seemed that some sharp intelligence allowed the girl to see the essence of things hidden behind any phenomenon.</t3017> <t3018>According to Colonel Aureliano Buendía, Remedios the Beauty was not intellectually slow as people often thought, but quite the opposite.</t3018> <t3019>"It's as if she has just returned after twenty years of battling in a war," he often said.</t3019> <t3020>For her part, Úrsula thanked God for rewarding their family with an extremely pure girl, but at the same time, she worried about her beauty, considering it a supernatural force full of contradictions, a trap of the devil lying right in the midst of pure goodness.</t3020> <t3021>Therefore, she was determined to isolate her from life, protecting her from all worldly desires, unaware that Remedios the Beauty had been immune to all human vices since she was in her mother's womb.</t3021> <t3022>She never thought that people would choose her great-granddaughter to be the queen reigning at the center of the Carnival ball.</t3022> <t3023>But Aureliano Segundo, who greatly enjoyed disguising himself as a tiger, invited Father Antonio Isabel to the house to persuade his great-grandmother that the Carnival ball was not a pagan festival but a traditional Christian celebration. In the end, though still grumbling, she had to listen to the priest and agree to let them crown her great-granddaughter as queen.</t3023>
<t3024>Rumors that Remedios Buendía would preside over the festival had spread throughout the swamp region, reaching even the most remote corners where her renowned beauty was still unknown, causing anxiety among those who until then had thought her name was a symbol of corruption.</t3024> <t3025>It was an unfounded anxiety.</t3025> <t3026>If there was anyone at that time who was no longer a cause for concern, it was Colonel Aureliano Buendía, a sad old man who was gradually losing his connection with the reality of the country.</t3026> <t3027>Secluding himself in the jewelry workshop, his only link to the outside world was the trade of little goldfish.</t3027> <t3028>One of the soldiers who had guarded the colonel's house when peace was newly established would go daily to the neighboring villages to sell goldfish and return home with gold coins and news.</t3028>
<t3029>There are rumors that the Royalist faction, with the help of the Liberal faction, has reformed the law so that each President has the right to stay in office for about a hundred years.</t3029> <t3030>There are rumors that an agreement with the Vatican has been signed, stating that a Cardinal</t3030> <t3031>Patriarch, adorned with a diamond-studded crown and riding on a golden throne, has come from Rome, and the Liberal ministers have taken photographs of the ceremony where they knelt to kiss his ring.</t3031> <t3032>There are rumors that a Spanish theater actress, during a night stay in the capital, was kidnapped by a masked group right in her bedroom, and on Sunday she danced naked at the summer house of the President of the Republic.</t3032> <t3033>"Do not talk politics with me," the colonel said to the soldier.</t3033> <t3034>"Our issue is selling goldfish."</t3034> <t3035>The rumor that the colonel did not want to know anything about the state of the country because he had become wealthy from his jewelry workshop, when it reached Úrsula's ears, made her laugh with her very sharp sense of reality. She could not understand the colonel's business of constantly exchanging goldfish for gold coins, only to then turn the gold coins back into goldfish, and so on in a perpetual cycle: having to work harder the more he sold to satisfy the hopeless vicious circle.</t3035>
<t3036>Indeed, for the colonel, what delighted him was not business but labor.</t3036> <t3037>He had to concentrate his mind intensely to arrange the scales, to inlay the tiny golden pupil into the eyes, to thin the scales in front of the fins, to attach the tail to the fish, so that there was no room in his mind for the disappointment of war to creep in.</t3037> <t3038>The meticulous and demanding profession required him to exert all his mental strength, which aged him very quickly, faster than all the arduous years of warfare combined; his working posture bent his spine, and his pupils lost their sharpness, but the intense concentration on his work rewarded him with a serene spirit.</t3038> <t3039>The last time he was seen concerned with any war-related issues was when veterans from both political parties sought his help to demand the government pass the war pension, a pension always promised but perpetually in the initial stages of processing.</t3039> <t3040>"Forget about it," he told them.</t3040> <t3041>"You see, I refused it to avoid the suffering of waiting in vain until death."</t3041> <t3042>At first, Colonel Herinendo Marquez visited him every afternoon, and they both sat at the door looking out onto the street to reminisce about their past.</t3042>
<t3043>But Amaranta suffered unbearably from the old memories stirred up by the very man whose weary head, already bald, had thrust him into the abyss of premature old age, and thus she tormented him with trivialities until he only visited on special occasions and eventually stopped coming altogether because paralysis confined him to one spot.</t3043> <t3044>Sullen, silent, and oblivious to the invigorating new breeze blowing through his house, Colonel Aureliano Buendía had come to understand that the secret to a peaceful old age was nothing other than a deep attachment to solitude.</t3044> <t3045>He woke up at five in the morning after a fitful sleep, drank the sugarless coffee that had been a constant in his life, then locked himself in his gold workshop. At four in the afternoon, he dragged his stool along the hallway without ever looking at the garden ablaze with red roses, paying no mind to Amaranta's patient determination as she followed every faint hum that marked the arrival of dusk, and then he sat at the door looking out at the street until the mosquitoes drove him inside.</t3045> <t3046>Occasionally, someone dared to disturb his solitude.</t3046>
<t3047>- How are you, Colonel? - they asked him as they passed by.</t3047>
<t3048>- Still the same, - he replied.</t3048> <t3049>- I am waiting for my own funeral to arrive.</t3049>
<t3050>Thus, the concern caused by the public reappearance of the Buendía family through the crowning of Remedios the Beauty had lost its practical basis.</t3050>
<t3051>However, many people did not believe so.</t3051> <t3052>Naive in the face of the impending disaster, the populace flocked to the grand square in an uproarious and boundless belief.</t3052> <t3053>The Carnival ball had reached its feverish peak, and Aureliano Segundo, satisfied with his tiger disguise, joyfully moved among the self-indulgent crowd, shouting and singing until his voice was hoarse.</t3053> <t3054>At that very moment, on the road leading from the swamp into the town, appeared a procession of colorfully disguised people, carrying a gilded palanquin on which sat a girl so stunningly beautiful that people dared not even think of her.</t3054>
<t3055>The gentle residents of the town of Macondo simultaneously removed their masks to get a better look at the dazzling girl in a crown adorned with emeralds and a cloak made of bird feathers, as if she were a true queen and not just a beauty queen in the masquerade ball of Carnival.</t3055> <t3056>There was no shortage of people with enough clear-headed reasoning to suspect that the group intended to provoke.</t3056> <t3057>But Aureliano Segundo immediately overcame this complicated situation, declaring that the newcomers were honored guests, and he solemnly seated Remedios the Beauty and the disguised queen together on the high platform.</t3057> <t3058>By midnight, the strangers disguised as Bedouins joined the ecstatic festivities of the night and enriched the fun with brilliant pyrotechnics and rope-walking tricks that made everyone think of the magical games of the gypsies.</t3058> <t3059>Suddenly, in the midst of the most bustling part of the celebration, someone disrupted the warm festive atmosphere.</t3059>
<t3060>- Long live the Liberal Party! - he shouted. - Long live Colonel Aureliano Buendía!</t3060>
<t3061>The bursts of gunfire extinguished the brilliant, multicolored lights of the fireworks, the terrified screams stifled the lively music, and fear dispelled the joy.</t3061>
<t3062>Many years later, public opinion continued to assert that the royal guards protecting the queen were disguised as a platoon of regular soldiers who had hidden conventional weapons beneath the beautiful attire of the Bedouins. In a special communiqué, the government opposed this public opinion and promised to conduct a thorough investigation into this bloodshed.</t3062> <t3063>But the truth was never clarified, and the explanation that the royal guards showed no signs of provocation, acted under the commander's orders, and mercilessly fired into the crowd persisted indefinitely.</t3063> <t3064>When order was restored, there was no trace of any disguised Bedouins in the entire town; the square was left with the wounded and the dead, including three clowns, four people dressed as Columbus, seventeen emperors, one demon, three musicians, and three Japanese queens. Amid the chaotic fear, José Arcadio Segundo managed to save Remedios the Beauty, and Aureliano Segundo managed to embrace the girl disguised as the queen, with disheveled clothes and a blood-soaked feathered cloak, to escape.</t3064> <t3065>The girl's name was Fernanda del Carpio. She had been chosen from among five thousand of the most beautiful girls in the country and brought to Macondo with the promise that she would be crowned queen of Madagascar.</t3065> <t3066>Úrsula cared for her as if she were her own daughter.</t3066> <t3067>The villagers, instead of doubting her innocence, pitied her genuine virtues.</t3067> <t3068>Six months after the massacre, when the wounded had fully recovered and the last flowers placed on the communal grave had withered, Aureliano Segundo went to find her in a distant city where she was living with her father, and then married her in the town of Macondo in a noisy celebration that lasted twenty days.</t3068>
<t3069>Chapter 11</t3069>
<t3070>After two months, the couple almost divorced because Auréliano Segundo took a portrait of Petra Cotes dressed as the queen of Madagascar to comfort her.</t3070> <t3071>When she found out, Fernanda packed her bridal clothes and left Macondo without saying a word to him.</t3071> <t3072>Auréliano Segundo caught up with her on the marshy road.</t3072> <t3073>After begging and promising to reform, he managed to bring her back home and stopped visiting his mistress.</t3073>
<t3074>Petra Cotes believed in her own allure, completely unperturbed.</t3074> <t3075>She had made him into a man.</t3075> <t3076>From the time he was just a young boy, filled with fanciful thoughts and completely ignorant of life, she had pulled him out of Melquiades' room and given him a place in the real world.</t3076> <t3077>Nature had endowed him with a quiet and proud disposition, prone to solitary contemplation, but she had forged in him a spirited, strong, open, and extravagant character, instilling in him the joy of life, the joy of festivities and spending; thus transforming him from soul to outward appearance into the man she had dreamed of when she was a young girl.</t3077> <t3078>He had married because, like children, sooner or later they must marry.</t3078> <t3079>He did not dare to tell her the news early.</t3079> <t3080>He tried to maintain an innocent demeanor, whether by getting angry without reason or pretending to be sorrowful, all in an effort to make Petra Cotes the one responsible for their separation.</t3080> <t3081>One day, Aureliano Segundo scolded her unreasonably.</t3081> <t3082>Understanding his scheme immediately, she bluntly confronted him:</t3082>
<t3083>- It's just that you want to marry that beauty queen.</t3083>
<t3084>Aureliano Segundo, very embarrassed, pretended to be angry for a while, loudly proclaiming that he had been misunderstood and humiliated, and from then on, he no longer associated with her.</t3084> <t3085>Always in control of herself, with an extraordinary self-discipline, Petra Cotes listened to the music and the wedding fireworks, listened to the noise and the frenzy of the communal celebration, as if all of it were just another one of Aureliano Segundo's tricks.</t3085> <t3086>To those who showed pity for her fate, she reassured them with a smile: "Don't worry," she told them, "That beauty queen is just someone I use for errands."</t3086> <t3087>To the neighbor who brought her pairs of candles to illuminate the portrait of the lover who had abandoned her, she spoke with a mysterious certainty:</t3087>
<t3088>- The only candle that will bring him back will always burn brightly.</t3088>
<t3089>As she had foreseen, Aureliano Segundo returned to her house right after the honeymoon ended.</t3089> <t3090>He brought along his old friends and a photographer carrying the clothes and feathered cloak that Fernanda had worn at the Carnival ball.</t3090> <t3091>In the festive atmosphere that lasted all afternoon, he dressed Petra Cotes in queenly attire, crowned her as the queen of Madagascar, took photos, and distributed her portraits to his friends.</t3091> <t3092>She not only played along with the game but also felt pity for him, thinking that he must have been very desperate to come up with such a strange way to make amends.</t3092> <t3093>At seven in the evening, still wearing the queenly attire, she received him in bed.</t3093> <t3094>He had been married for only a few months, but she immediately noticed that the couple was not getting along even in their marital bed, and she was ecstatic to see her revenge fully realized.</t3094> <t3095>However, two days later, when he not only did not dare to return to her house but also sent an intermediary to settle the terms of their separation, she understood that she would need to be more patient and that he seemed determined to pursue that superficial glitter.</t3095> <t3096>She was not upset.</t3096> <t3097>She complied with all his demands, making everyone genuinely believe that she was a suffering woman.</t3097> <t3098>The only memento of Aureliano Segundo she kept was a pair of beige boots, which, according to him, he would wear when he was laid to rest in his coffin.</t3098> <t3099>She wrapped them in cloth and hid them carefully at the bottom of a chest, then waited, waited with great hope.</t3099>
<t3100>- Sooner or later, he will have to come back, - she said to herself, - even if it's just to get these boots.</t3100>
<t3101>She did not have to wait as long as she had previously predicted.</t3101> <t3102>In fact, Aureliano Segundo, right from the wedding night, understood that he would return to Petra Cotes' house much sooner than he would need to wear the beige boots, because Fernanda was a woman out of place in this life.</t3102> <t3103>She was born and raised in a place thousands of kilometers away from the sea, a desolate city with fearful nights echoing the clattering wheels of viceroys on the rough stone streets.</t3103> <t3104>Thirty-two bell towers tolled mournfully and deathly at six o'clock in the evening.</t3104> <t3105>The sun never shone on the old stone-paved house.</t3105> <t3106>The wind died on the tops of the cypress trees in the yard, on the faded wind screens in the rooms, on the intersecting arches of the camphor trees in the garden.</t3106> <t3107>Even when she reached puberty, Fernanda knew nothing of life beyond the melancholic, longing piano practice pieces played by someone permitted to skip the afternoon nap next door.</t3107> <t3108>Sitting in her sickly, pale mother's room in the filtered light through dusty frosted glass; she listened to the orderly, mournful, and sorrowful notes and thought that music existed in life, while she busily braided mourning hats from palm leaves.</t3108> <t3109>Her mother, sweating with fever at five in the afternoon, told her stories of past glories.</t3109> <t3110>One moonlit night, when she was still a child, Fernanda saw the shadow of a woman in white walking from the flower garden into the prayer room.</t3110> <t3111>The shadow that passed by so quickly worried her because she felt it looked exactly like her, as if it were her own shadow twenty years later. "That was your great-great-grandmother," her mother said as her cough subsided, "She died of a chill while cutting a camphor branch."</t3111> <t3112>Many years later, when she felt she looked exactly like her great-great-grandmother, Fernanda began to doubt the ghost she had seen as a child, but her mother scolded her for her lack of faith.</t3112>
<t3113>- We are inherently wealthy and incredibly powerful, she said to her.</t3113> <t3114>- Then you will be a queen.</t3114>
<t3115>She believed that even though her family currently owned only a long dining table with enough cloth and silverware, and this table was only used for eating pastries and drinking chocolate.</t3115> <t3116>Even on her wedding day, she still dreamed of the time of kings.</t3116> <t3117>This legend persisted even though her father, Don Fernando, had to mortgage the house to get money for her wedding attire.</t3117> <t3118>Not too naive nor pretentious about her family's prestige, she was raised in such a manner.</t3118> <t3119>From the time she had a little understanding, she remembered sitting on a golden chamber pot engraved with her family's coat of arms to relieve herself.</t3119> <t3120>The first time she left the house was when she was twelve years old, in a carriage that only needed to travel two blocks to reach the convent.</t3120>
<t3121>The classmates were all surprised that she was separated, made to sit on a very high chair, and that even during playtime she did not play with them.</t3121><t3122>"She is very different," the nuns explained to them, "She will be a queen."</t3122><t3123>Her friends believed it because even then she was already a beautiful, unusual, and discreet girl.</t3123><t3124>Eight years later, after learning to write poetry in Latin, to play the clavichord, to converse with young noblemen about falconry, with bishops about theological matters, to debate with foreign diplomats about state affairs, and with the Pope about matters of God, she returned to her parents' home to weave mourning hats from palm leaves.</t3124><t3125>She found her home cleared of furniture.</t3125><t3126>Almost only valueless beds and wardrobes, chandeliers, and the silverware remained, because the valuable items had been gradually sold off to cover the costs of her education.</t3126><t3127>Her mother had passed away from a fever at five in the afternoon.</t3127><t3128>Her father, Don Fernando, dressed in black, with a high-collared shirt and a gold chain draped across his chest, would give her a silver coin for food expenses on Mondays, then take away the mourning hats made of palm leaves that she had finished the previous week.</t3128><t3129>He spent most of the day in his private room, and on the rare occasions he went out, he hurried back home before six in the evening to pray the Rosary with her. He never befriended anyone.</t3129><t3130>He never spoke of the civil wars that were ravaging the country.</t3130><t3131>He never missed her piano practice sessions at three in the afternoon.</t3131><t3132>When she began to lose hope of becoming a queen, there was an urgent knock on the main door. She quickly opened it to a handsome soldier with a scar on his cheek and a gold medal pinned to his chest. He went to have a private conversation with her father.</t3132><t3133>Two hours later, her father came to the sewing room to find her.</t3133><t3134>"Prepare your belongings," he told her, "You need to go far away."</t3134><t3135>That is how she was taken to Macondo.</t3135><t3136>In just one day, life had brought down upon her all the weight of a reality that in</t3136>
<t3137>For many years, her parents deliberately shielded her.</t3137><t3138>Since returning home, she locked herself in her room, crying, ignoring the pleas and explanations of Don Fernanda, to erase the burning shame that had scarred her skin.</t3138><t3139>She had just vowed to herself that she would not leave her room until she died when Aureliano Segundo came to the house looking for her.</t3139><t3140>It was an unforeseen stroke of luck because, in her confusion and anger, in her fury and shame, she had lied to him so he would never trace her true whereabouts.</t3140><t3141>But the only tangible clue for Aureliano Segundo to follow in his search for her was her unmistakable plains accent and her craft of weaving mourning hats from palm leaves.</t3141><t3142>He patiently beautiful girl on earth was, mothers immediately took him to see their daughters.</t3145><t3146>He got lost in cloud-covered mountain passes, in forgotten regions, and in the maze of <t3156>Fernanda carried with her a precious calendar and a bunch of gold-plated keys in which the priest who taught her had marked with ever-fresh ink the days to abstain from entering the room.</t3156> <t3157>Excluding Holy Week, Sundays, days required to attend mass, the first Fridays of each month, feast days, and menstruation days, which were strictly to be abstained from, her useful calendar only had forty-two free days scattered within the web of still fresh ink crosses.</t3157> <t3158>Aureliano Segundo believed that time would break down that barrier and extended the wedding celebration beyond the set time.</t3158> <t3159>Exhausted from having to pour out bottles of Brandy and champagne into the trash to keep the house tidy, and at the same time curious to see that the bride and groom did not go to bed at the same time and even slept in separate rooms while fireworks were still going off, music was still playing, and livestock was still being slaughtered, Úrsula recalled her own life experiences and wondered if Fernanda was also wearing a chastity belt that sooner or later would provoke the villagers' malicious comments and be the source of a tragedy.</t3159>
<t3160>But Fernanda had confessed to the old man that she only had to wait two weeks before being allowed to sleep with her husband for the first time.</t3160> <t3161>Indeed, two weeks passed, and she opened her room door with the obligatory concession of a punished victim, and Aureliano Segundo saw the most beautiful woman in the world with eyes blazing with the pride of a frightened animal and locks of copper-colored blonde hair spread over the pillow.</t3161> <t3162>He was so delighted by that sight that it took him a moment to realize that Fernanda was wearing a pure white nightgown, covering her ankles and even her knuckles, with a large, round opening at the belly, carefully embroidered around the edges.</t3162> <t3163>Aureliano Segundo couldn't hold back a burst of hearty laughter.</t3163>
<t3164>- Oh, this is even more provocative than the scenes you've ever seen - he yelled, with a cackling laugh echoing through the house.</t3164> <t3165>- You married a nun from the Saint Vincent de Paul order.</t3165>
<t3166>A month later, unable to persuade his wife to take off that nightgown, he went to Petra Cotes's house to take a picture of her wearing the queen's attire.</t3166> <t3167>After that, when he managed to convince Fernanda not to return to her hometown and to come back to their home, she conceded to his earnest pleas for reconciliation, but he still didn't know how to arrange their rest as he had dreamed when he came to the city of thirty-two bell towers to find her.</t3167> <t3168>Aureliano Segundo only found in her a sense of disillusionment.</t3168> <t3169>One night, a few days before giving birth to their first son, Fernanda knew that her husband had secretly gone back to share a bed with Petra Cotes.</t3169>
<t3170>- That's right, - he admitted.</t3170> <t3171>Then, in a conciliatory tone, he explained: - I needed to act that way so that our herd could continue to grow and multiply even more.</t3171>
<t3172>He did not take much time to convince her about that strange measure, but once he had persuaded her with seemingly irrefutable evidence, the only condition that Fernanda forced him to promise was not to let death catch him right on the bed of his lover.</t3172> <t3173>The three of them lived together like that, without anyone mentioning anything to anyone.</t3173> <t3174>Aureliano Segundo was affectionate and punctual with both, Petra Cotes was satisfied with the harmony, while Fernanda pretended not to know the truth.</t3174>
<t3175>However, harmony was not achieved within the family: Fernanda did not live harmoniously with everyone.</t3175> <t3176>Úrsula tried hard to scold her into throwing away the felt cloth she used to line the bed whenever she was with her husband and then draped over her shoulders, which caused much gossip among the villagers, but she still refused to throw it away.</t3176> <t3177>She could not persuade her to use the toilet when she needed to relieve herself and to sell the golden chamber pot to Colonel Aureliano Buendía so he could make goldfish.</t3177> <t3178>Amaranta was annoyed by her rude speech and habit of calling things by the wrong names, so she often used slang in front of her.</t3178>
<t3179>One day, infuriated by the blasphemy, Fernanda wanted to know what Amaranta was saying, and without using any flowery language, she replied:</t3179>
<t3180>- He said, - she said, - that you are one of those girls who carry shrimp on their heads with dung.</t3180>
<t3181>Since that day, the two people did not speak to each other.</t3181> <t3182>When they were forced to talk to each other, they would send messages through others or speak indirectly.</t3182> <t3183>Ignoring the discomfort of the household, Fernanda did not give up her intention to impose habits on her superiors.</t3183> <t3184>She decided to abandon the practice of eating in the kitchen whenever one was hungry, as was done before, and required everyone to eat at the designated time in the dining room, sitting at a large, neatly arranged table with napkins, chandeliers, and silverware.</t3184> <t3185>The solemnity of what Ursula considered the simplest daily activity created a tense atmosphere, causing José Arcadio Segundo, who was already sullen, to be the first to oppose it.</t3185> <t3186>But the habits were still established, such as the custom of praying the Rosary before dinner, which caught the neighbors' attention and led to rumors that the Buendía family did not sit down to eat like other families but instead turned dinner into a solemn mass.</t3186> <t3187>Even Ursula's religious beliefs, which arose more from momentary hope than tradition, began to conflict with the doctrines Fernanda had inherited from her parents.</t3187> <t3188>While Ursula was still strong and capable, some old customs were maintained, and family life followed her subtle perceptions. But when she became blind and the weight of old age confined her to a corner, the reins that Fernanda had initiated since she first came to the house completely tightened, and no one but her decided the family's fate.</t3188> <t3189>The candy-making trade that Santa</t3189> <t3190>Sofía de la Piedad maintained according to Ursula's wishes was deemed lowly by Fernanda and was soon discontinued.</t3190> <t3191>The house, which had been open from early morning until bedtime, was closed during the afternoon nap with the excuse of keeping the bedrooms cool and then remained shut all day.</t3191> <t3192>The aloe branch and cake that had been hung at the door since the founding of Macondo were replaced by a niche for a statue of Jesus.</t3192> <t3193>Colonel Aureliano Buendía also noticed these changes and understood their reasons.</t3193> <t3194>"We are returning to being aristocrats," he protested, "At this rate, we will once again have to fight against the monarchy, but this time to put another king on the throne."</t3194> <t3195>Fernanda, being very delicate, restrained herself to avoid clashing with him.</t3195> <t3196>His free spirit and resistance to rigid social rituals made her very uncomfortable.</t3196> <t3197>The unsweetened coffee at five in the morning, the clutter in the workshop, the frayed coat, and the habit of sitting at the door looking out at
